Having completed for you, my children, this book, which is the final treatise in medicine with its completion, and having reached its end through its explanations and its declarations, it seemed to me that I should complete it for you with this treatise, which is a part of the operation, that is, surgery, with the hand.
Since the operation with the hand is languishing in our region and in our time, entirely private, until perhaps its knowledge perished and its trace was severed, and nothing remained of it but small descriptions in the books of the ancients, which changing hands have altered, and errors and hesitation have befallen them, until its intentions were closed and its force withdrawn: it seemed to me that I should give it life again with the arrangement of this treatise, according to the method of exposition, declaration, and abbreviation, and that I should come with the forms of the instruments of the cautery, and of the other instruments of the work, with the addition of a declaration and the haste of what is necessary.
The reason why a good craftsman of it with his hand is not found in our time is this: because the art of medicine is long, and its author must be trained beforehand in the science of anatomy, as Galen has related, until he knows the aids of the limbs and their forms, and their joining and separation, and has knowledge of bones, nerves, tendons, and their number, and their emergence, and of the pulsating and still veins, and the places of their exits.
And therefore Hippocrates said that name with name is much, and with work is little, and especially in the art of the hand.
We, however, have already spoken of that at the beginning of this book.
Because he who is not knowledgeable in what we have said about anatomy is not safe, but falls into error, by which people are killed; as I have seen many of those who are trained in this science and boast of it without knowledge and without experience.
This is because I saw a foolish physician cut an abscess in the woman's neck, and he cut some arteries in her neck; therefore the woman's blood flowed until she fell dead in his hands.
And I saw another physician who presumed to extract a stone from a man who had advanced in age, and the stone was large. Therefore, he reached it and extracted it with a piece of the bladder. Therefore the man died on the third day.
And I myself had already been called to extract it, and I saw from the size of the stone, and the condition of the sick man, that he would die.
And I saw another physician who was receiving payment from a certain Duke of our land for his medicine, and it happened to a black eunuch who was with him, a fracture in his leg near the heel; and the physician, in his ignorance, hurried and bound the fracture over the wound with pillows and splints with strong constriction, and did not allow the wound an outlet.
Then he discharged him according to his desires. Afterwards he dismissed him after a few days, and instructed him not to loosen the bandage; and he remained like that until his leg and foot became suppurated, and it came to ruin.
Therefore, I was summoned to him, and I hastened to loosen the bandage; and tranquility followed, and he escaped from his pains.
However, corruption had already become established in the limb, and I could not restrain it.
Therefore, the corruption ceased to wander in the limb, until it perished.
And I saw another physician, who pierced the cancerous abscess; and it ulcerated after days, until its malice increased with its owner.
That is, because when the cancer is pure, from melancholy humour, then it is not necessary to reach it with iron at all, unless it is in a limb that can tolerate its being completely severed.
And therefore, my children, it is necessary for you to know that surgery, with the hand, is divided into two divisions: namely, surgery to which health is associated; and surgery with which there is danger in many dispositions.
And I have already raised up in every place of this book, in which surgery comes, in which there is terror and fear.
Therefore it is necessary for you to beware of it and to divide it, so that fools may not find a way to speak ill of it and dishonour it.
Therefore, receive your measurements with care and protection, and your patients with ease and firmness, and use the better path leading by God's grace to health and praiseworthy succession; and leave behind terrible illnesses of difficult healing; and remove your minds from that which causes you fear, so that hesitation may enter into your faith and your well-being.
For it remains more to your glory, and more sublime in this world, and finally to your merits.
For Galen already said in some of his admonitions. Do not treat a bad illness, lest you be called a bad physician.
Now, however, I have divided this Treatise into three Chapters.
First Chapter.
Concerning the cautery with fire, and the cautery with sharp medicine, chaptered and arranged from head to foot, and forms of instruments, and tools of cautery, and everything necessary in the operation.
Second Chapter.
Concerning incision and perforation, phlebotomy and cupping, and wounds: and the extraction of arrows, and similar things: and all chaptered, arranged by forms of instruments.
Third Chapter.
Concerning restoration, and dislocation, and the treatment of twisting, and similar things: chaptered, arranged from head to foot, and forms of instruments.
But before I recount the operation with it, it is necessary that I speak of the quality of its benefits and harms, and in which complexion it is to be applied.
I say therefore, that discourse on the quality of the cautery's benefit and harm is a lengthy and subtle discourse, a matter of science and hidden secret.
Many wise men have spoken on it, and disagreed; and for me, a small part of their words suffices, fearing length.
I say therefore, that the cautery benefits every complexion, whether with or without matter, except for two complexions, which are a hot complexion without matter and a dry complexion without matter.
However, in a hot and dry complexion with matter, they disagreed. For some said that the cautery is beneficial in it. And others said the opposite, namely that the cautery is not suitable in an illness arising from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat, and it is unsuitable for being cured of a hot and dry illness with heat and dryness.
And he who says the opposite stated that through cauterization benefit occurs in every hot and dry illness that occurs in human bodies, because when you compare the human body and its humors to the complexion of fire, you find the human body to be cold.
And I myself say through his words: because by experience it has revealed this to me several times.
However, it is not appropriate for anyone to apply it to that unless they have already been proven and practiced in the final exercise of cauterization, and have known the diversity of human complexions, and the disposition of illnesses within themselves, and their causes and accidents, and their time span.
However, there is no fear regarding the remaining complexions, and especially in cold and moist illnesses. For all physicians have already agreed upon them, and have not disagreed on the benefit of cauterization in them.
And know, children, that from the secret of healing by cautery with fire, and its goodness over cautery with burning medicine, is that fire is simple, whose action does not reach, except for the limb that is cauterized: and it does not harm any other limb joined with it, except for a small harm.
However, the action of burning medicine reaches that which is distant from the limbs, and perhaps causes illness of difficult healing, and perhaps kills, in the limb.
But fire, because of its subtlety and the goodness of its substance, does not do that, unless it overflows.
However, this has now become known to us through experiment, because of the length of service and study with the art, and our standing upon the truth of things; and because of that, I excuse myself from the length of this discourse.
And if it were not so, because it is not permitted to this book of mine, I would bring to you the secret hidden in fire, and the quality of its operation on the bodies of its expulsion, by which it expels illnesses with a demonstrative philosophical discourse, which would be sharpened by your minds.
And know, children, that they disagree as to the time when the cautery should be applied, and they place a better time in the spring.
And I say that cautery is appropriate at all times, because the harm that is incidental to the time is submerged in the benefit which is drawn in with the cautery. And especially if the cautery is for pains from necessary, strong, urgent pains, which do not tolerate slowness, because it is feared from them that a greater evil will occur than the slight harm that enters because of the time.
And do not let it fall into your minds, O children, that which is feared only by the common people and foolish physicians, namely that the ailment which is cured by cautery does not always return. And they posit that as a consequence, but things are not as they think: because cautery is not, except in the place of a medicine, which alters the complexion and dries out the humidities which are the cause why pains occur.
However, cautery is superior to medicine by the swiftness of its health, and the power of its operation, and the vehemence of its control. And it is possible that the illness may return at some hour of time according to the constitution of the sick person, and the location of their illness, and its power; and that which is prepared in his body from the aggregation of superfluities in him; and that which comes to him in his acquisition from foods and similar causes, unless it is an illness in which cautery is administered.
A subtle illness in a limb from few superfluities and humidities, like the cautery of a tooth, for pain, and similar to it, it is possible that that pain will not return in it. But that happens in few cases.
But truly the common people's saying, that cautery is the last resort of medicine, is a correct statement; not to what they intend by it, because they believe that it is not a cure that compares with medicine, nor with anything else after the cautery has passed.
However, the matter is the opposite of that, and the intention that cautery is the last resort of medicine is not the case, except that when we administer methods of treatment for some illnesses, and those medicines do not work, then we administer cautery at the end of the matter, and it works; then it follows that cautery is the last resort of medicine, not according to the intention understood by the common people and many unintelligent physicians.
The ancients, however, said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron; and they did not say this unless because of the equality of gold and the nobility of its substance; and they said that the cautery site does not produce pus. But that is not absolutely so; because I have already tested it, and found that it does not do so in some bodies without others.
And cautery with it is good, and better than with iron, as they said. However, when you make cautery of gold in fire, it is not clear to you when it is heated to the quantity you want, because of the redness of the gold; and when it rapidly approaches cold, and if you add more heat to it, it melts in the fire and turns into sheets.
Therefore, the craftsman falls from that occupation; and because of that, cautery with iron is quicker and closer to us, according to the straightness of the operation.
And I have indeed already arranged this chapter on cautery, according to chapters; and I have arranged them from head to foot, so that they will relieve the one seeking what he wants from it.
Chapter II.
This cauterization contributes to the control of humidity and coldness over the brain; these two are the cause of headaches, and a multitude of discharges of humours from the head to the eyes and ears, and a multitude of sleep, and toothache, and sore throat: and universally every illness that occurs from coldness, such as paralysis, and headaches, epilepsy, and apoplexies, and similar illnesses to them.
The method of cauterization is, that you order the sick person: to be evacuated first with a cleansing liquid medicine, cleansing the head for three or four nights, according to what the strength of the sick person, and his age, and his custom demand.
Then order him to shave his head with a razor. Then make him sit between your hands in a square position, so that he has already placed his hands on his chest.
Then place the root of your palm on the root of his nose between his eyes. Wherever, therefore, your middle finger arrives, mark that place with caustic.
Then the Olive Cautery, whose form is this.
Then place him on the place marked with caustic, descending, so that your hand is gradually tightened, and you will turn him. Then lift your hand quickly, and look at the place.
If you see now exposed from the bone a quantity of alkiel, or a quantity of zeuherbi, then lift your hand. And if not, repeat your hand with the iron itself, or with another from it if it cools: until the bones appear, which I have told you.
Then take something of salt, and dissolve it in water, and soak in it cotton, and place it on the place: and leave it three days.
Then place over it the cotton soaked in butter, and leave it over the place until the eschar of fire is destroyed. Then treat it with the tetrapharmacon ointment, until it is healed.
They have said that the more a wound remains open, the more it emits pus: therefore, it is better and more helpful.
And some of them said that the skin should be cauterized down to the bone: and the cautery should be held until something of the thickness of bone is burned.
Then that which has been burned from the bone should be scraped off, then treated.
And others say that it is necessary to reach the end with the cautery, until it makes an impression on the bone, such that the bone falls off, which occurs like the shape of a kirate; or an instrument that women use with a small infused alphabet; and they believe that vapors of the head are exhaled from this place, and the wound should be left open for a long time.
Then it should be treated until it heals.
However, these two types of cautery do not seem to me to be suitable except in certain individuals, according to a way of caution.
And to leave it, in my opinion, is better with the health of the person.
For the head weakens from the disruption of its natural continuity, as we have observed in other limbs; and especially when the head of the weak person is naturally feeble. And indeed, the first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion, and I use it; therefore, work with it for the sake of health.
Chapter 3.
When chronic pain occurs throughout the head, and it prolongs itself in the sick person, and he uses leeches, and cupping, and head-cleansing, and oils, and emplastrum. And especially if it has already been cauterized with the one cautery, which we have described, and none of those things help it, then consider. For if the sick person's head is of strong natural constitution, and is not weak, and it finds severe cold, then cauterize it with another cautery a little beneath the first one. Then cauterize it with a cautery on every horn of his head, so that the skin is not perforated and a quantity of bone is uncovered, as we have described. And cauterize it with the one cautery on the back of his head, in the place called the vessel; and it is a place where a cupping glass is placed: and raise your cautery in this, and do not uncover the bone. For the sick person finds because of that a severe pain, and different from the pain of the other cauteries on all the head.
And I shall say this cautery is in its place: and it ought to be a cautery with which the horns of the head are cauterized, and the back of his head with a finer cautery, with which the middle of the head is cauterized.
Chapter IV.
When pain and throbbing occur in a part of the head, or the pain extends to the eye, and the head is purged with purifying medicines, and the remaining treatment, which I have mentioned in the divisions of illnesses, is applied, and it does not benefit entirely: then cauterization in it is according to two methods: either cauterization with a burning sharp medicine, or with iron.
The cautery with a burning sharp medicine is as follows: take one clove of garlic, and peel it, and cut off its ends from both sides. Then, incise the painful area with a wide lancet, until a space is made beneath the skin, in which the clove is placed.
Then introduce it into the space under the skin, until it is hidden; then bind it over it with a suitable bandage, and leave it for fifteen hours, then remove it and take out the garlic, and let the wound be for two or three days; afterwards place on it a compress soaked in butter, until the place forms pus; then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do it with certain burning medicines, which I have written in the twelfth treatise, concerning Burning Medicines.
However, its cauterization with iron is according to this method. The cautery iron, whose form is this.
And it is called a "nail" because the shape of its head is like the shape of a nail, in which there is some curvature, and in the middle of it is a small prominence.
Then place it upon the area, and hold your hand; and you should rotate the iron gradually with speed; and let the quantity burned be from the thickness of the skin, like half of it. And raise your hand, so that the artery, which is below, is not burned, and a flow of blood occurs.
Then soak lint in salt water, and place it over the area, and leave it for three days; then place lint with butter; afterward, treat with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize this hemicrania with the second cautery's knife-edge. And beware of incision into the artery in this hemicrania, not properly an old one.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in an old hemicrania.
When treating hemicrania, with what we have said about the preceding treatment, and what we have said in the divisions of illnesses, and if treatment is not helping; and you see from the illness that what we have said about the first cautery with medicine, or with cautery with fire, is not sufficient, then it is necessary to heat the cautery knife until it turns white, after marking the site of pain with a line whose length is half a finger, or similar; and place your hand, in turn, and press it until you cut into the artery, and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to be careful not to burn the jaw, which moves during mastication, or the nerve that moves it, lest a spasm occur.
And be on guard and careful about the flow of blood from the cut artery. For in its cutting is danger, and especially for him who does not know what he is doing, and is not diligent nor experienced; and he abandons the operation with that in the first place.
And I will come with a reminder of the regulation of blood flow arising from the artery according to its manner, and in its place in this book.
If, from the illness, you see that this cautery is insufficient for him, and you see the body of the sick man tolerating the cautery in the middle of the head, as we have related, treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, administer the cautery, which we mentioned in the chapter on the extraction of the artery with a cautery having two knives. For cauterization is better than this and more beneficial.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in ear pain.
When pain in the ear occurs from cold, and is treated with remedies and the rest of the treatment as we said in the division, and the pain is not removed, then cauterize with fire: and that is what is called the Point, whose shape is this.
Then the points, after its ignition, with it, in a circling motion around the entire ear, as it revolves; or in a circling motion around each, if the pain is in both at the same time. And move the cautery slightly away from the root of the ear, after you have marked the places with ink; and let the quantity of cautery be ten points in each ear, and approximately like that.
Then treat the places until they are healed.
Concerning the method of cauterization in Torture.
Chapter VII.
Torture which is treated with cauterization is only from the species which arise from phlegm, according to what we said in the divisions of illnesses.
And avoid cauterization of the species that arises from dryness and nerve spasm.
When you treat this type of torture with leeches, and head purgings, and gargles, and your treatment is of no avail, then you must cauterize the afflicted person with three cauteries: one near the root of the ear, and a second a little lower on the temporal region, and a third at the juncture of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side of the affected part. Because softening does not occur unless in the part that appears healthy.
And the method of cauterization is to cauterize it with a cautery directly at the tip of the upper earlobe, slightly under the horn of the head, and another on the temporal region, whose length should be equal to the length of the thumb. And hold your hand with the cautery until a quantity equal to half the thickness of the skin is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a type from the knife, of the form mentioned previously; however, it is not a little more delicate than that, as you see; and it is necessary that the knife within it has a little excess thickness.
Then treat the places, with what has gone before, until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterization in ancient apoplexy.
When the apoplexy is longstanding, and you have treated it with what we have said, and your treatment does not help, and the sick person does not have a fever, then cauterize him with four cauteries on each horn of his head, and with a cautery in the middle of the head, as we have said, and with a cautery on the back of the head, according to what has gone before; and the method of the cautery shall be according to what has gone before.
Also he is cauterized with a cautery on the mouth of the stomach: and it is more of a last resort.
Then let him be treated, with what has gone before.
Chapter IX.
It is necessary that large hyerae and pills that cleanse the brain be first given to the sick person in a drink.
Then shave the entire head of the sick person, and place on his back of the head a plaster of synapis, as described in the treatise on plasters, and place it several times.
For that is the method of the cautery; and do it according to the same order which I have stated there.
If, therefore, he is healed by it, it is good. If not, then make three cauteries on the back part of his head, which shall descend from the upper part of the head to the lower part of the eye; and place the thickness of a finger between each cautery.
Then treat the cautery, with that which preceded it.
If, however, you wish for an addition, and the infirm one is capable of tolerating it, then make a cautery in the middle. If you wish for an addition, then cauterize him above the two horns.
Then treat him until he is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped according to the form that was previously mentioned.
On the method of cauterizing in paralysis, and softening.
Chapter X.
It is necessary that you proceed in the cleansing of the head with hyerri and with that which we said.
Then shave the patient's head; afterwards cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with a cautery on each horn of the head, and with a cautery on its back, and with three on the vertebrae of the neck.
However, if in the illness you require softening of the body with more than those, and the patient is tolerant, and the illness is severe, then cauterize him with four cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and finally with one cautery, until the most of its skin is burned, and raise your hand. Then care for him until he is healed: and thus is the cautery of Olive.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in Epilepsy.
An epileptic is not cauterized, unless that one whose epilepsy is from phlegm.
Therefore, it is necessary that you first cleanse his brain with leeches, and with the remaining treatment which we have stated in the division: when the sick person is strong, or is tolerant in accepting medications.
However, if it is a child, who does not tolerate medications, then a gargle and chewing motions that cleanse the brain are administered, many days before, with the improvement of foods.
Then shave his head, afterwards cauterize him with one cautery in the middle of the head, according to what has preceded in the method: and with another cautery on his occiput, and with a cautery on every horn of his head. If however the sick person is strong and is tolerant, then cauterize him with the cauteries that I spoke of in the case of paralysis, and the softening of the body over the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back: and let the cautery be olive-shaped, according to the method that has preceded.
If however the sick person is a child, then place a fine cautery, according to this method.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in Melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupted humours and thick phlegm, then cauterize him with the cauteries that we have spoken of in the case of paralysis.
And if the cause of the melancholy is an excess of the downward inclination to melancholy, and the sick person's body is moist, then give him in drink that which will cleanse his brain, according to what has preceded in the division; then shave the sick person's head, and afterwards make a nicely made roll out of linen, like a circle.
Then place it in the middle of the head: and the sick person, sitting upright, is to be held firm on all sides.
Then apply the liniment. And it is better made from old sheep's butter. And heat it over the fire with a moderate heat, which the finger can tolerate when it is placed inside. Then apply it to the middle of his head in a circle, and leave it until it cools.
Do this to the sick person once a week, with the rest of his good regimen, until he is healed.
Or if you wish, cauterize it by making many small points on it, except that you should hold the hand with the cautery; indeed, let it be smelling.
For this type of cautery moistens the brain with quality.
Then place over it a cloth soaked in butter, or chicken fat.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in descending water to the eye.
Chapter XIII.
When you are shown the beginning of water in the eye by the signs that I have mentioned in the division, then begin and make the weak person drink it, with that which will cleanse his head.
And keep him from all moisture, and make him sit in a bath, fasting for several days.
Then command him to shave his head, and cauterize him in the middle of his head. Then cauterize him above the two temples with two cauteries, if it is the beginning of the descent of water in both eyes simultaneously; or from one side, if the beginning of water is in one eye only; and cut with the cautery all the veins and arteries that are under the skin; and let the cauteries be of the same length as the width of the temples; and beware of the flow of blood.
If you see anything from it, then cut it immediately with whatever medicine is possible; and I will come with the skill that is in the extraction of arteries, and the cutting of them, and caution against the flow of blood.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the neck, under the two bones, with two cauteries finally applied.
Concerning the method of cauterization in ancient lachrymal diseases.
When the tears of the eyes are ancient, persistent, and are situated above the veins and arteries that are apparent on the outer head, it is verified that it is from cold, phlegmatic superfluities; then cauterize with the same cautery that I described at the beginning of descending water, with a cautery in the middle of the head, and two cauteries above the two temples, and two cauteries in the neck beneath the two bones. And if it needs further treatment, then cauterize it on every side of the eye's socket, above the extremity of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Concerning the method of cauterization in nasal foulness.
When you treat it, with what we said about division, and the treatment does not help, then quickly give the weak person cochlias in a drink for three nights; then shave his head, and cauterize it with a middle cautery, with an olive cautery. Then cauterize it with a keyhole cautery, with two cauteries, above the two eyebrows under the hair a little.
And guard against the artery, lest you strike it.
And this is the form of the keyhole cautery.
Concerning the method of cauterization in the softening of the eyelid.
When the eyelid of the eye is softened by illness or moisture, then cauterize the eyelid with a single cautery, using this crescent-shaped cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize it above the two eyebrows a little with two cauteries on every side, and let them extend from the two temples; and let the length of each cautery be according to the length of the eyebrow; and do not place your hand with the cautery at the very end, but according to the amount that the third eyelid is burned.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the eyelid when the eyelashes are inverted and prick the eye.
The cautery in it is according to two methods: either cautery with fire, or cautery with a burning medicine.
In cautery with fire, first tell the sick person to remove his hairs, if he is of those who trim them, until they are lengthened and even. If, however, they prick him at their origin, then bind his eyes with a bandage so that they do not move until they are born.
When therefore they are born and are even, then place the sick person's head in your lap; then mark upon the eyelid of his eyes with ink a sign that is in the shape of a myrtle leaf; and let the beginning of the sign be near the hairs.
Then place a cloth soaked in egg white, or in psyllium mucilage, on the eye.
Then, apply the cautery, whose form is this.
Then cauterize over the figure, which you marked gradually in many stages, until the surface of the skin, which is like the figure of a myrtle leaf, is completely burned away.
And the sign of the success of your work is: that if you see the eyelid of the eye now contracting, and the hairs rising near the puncture of the eye.
Therefore, raise your hand then, and leave it for three days.
Then place a compress with butter on it until the eschar is removed. Then treat it with ointment until it heals.
If any hair returns after an hour, and the eyelid softens, then again cauterize that place, as you did at first.
If however the hairs are in the lower part, then cauterize it until it is raised to its natural place and made even so that the hairs do not prick the eye.
It is to instruct the sick person to let the hairs fall out until they are extended and made even. Make a shape of a myrtle leaf from paper on cloth.
Then take from known soap, and from unsoaked lime, one ounce or approximately that amount of each; then grind them together with good rubbing, and do it quickly so that it does not cool down. Then spread some of it from the mixture onto the paper you made in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and place it over the eyelid of one eye, or two. And place under the eye a poultice soaked in egg white; and let the sick person's head be in your lap.
And place your index finger on the medicine, and press it for a little while. And you should move it whenever the sick person feels the medicine's bite, since its bite is found to be like fire.
While, therefore, this bite which he finds persists, then remove the medicine, and move it with your finger. When, therefore, the bite subsides, then remove the medicine, and wash the eye with water.
And then consider, if you see the eyelid being lifted, as it is lifted with burning or incision, it is well; and if not, then again apply medicine to the place where the medicine does not take effect and does not darken, until your operation is equal to, and the eye is contracted.
Then place upon it a poultice with butter, until the skin of the burn is eradicated. Then treat it with diaphanicon ointment, or another, until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, during the operation, to observe the utmost care, so that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye.
If the eyelid softens after days, and something from the hairs fights in the eye, then apply medicine again to that place which softens from the eyelid properly, as you did at first. Then treat it until it is healed.
And this is the form of the myrtle leaf.
And know that the eyes of men differ in size and smallness. Therefore, according to that, let your consideration be: and the path of rectitude is not hidden from him who studies this art.
Concerning the method of cauterization in a tear duct fistula.
When treating a fistula, if your treatment, as we discussed in the divisions of diseases, is not helpful, then, you ought to cauterize it according to this method.
Instruct the patient to place his head in your lap, and let an attendant hold his head between their hands so that it does not move or shake with his head.
Then place a heated cautery, soaked in egg white or in psyllium mucilage, over his eye.  Next, apply the cautery, whose form is this.
Let it be concave, like the shape of a vulture's wing feather from one end, with which the cautery is made. And if you wish it to penetrate to the other end, and if you wish it to be held thus upwardly.
However, this concave shape is better for your work.
Then drain the fistula, if it is open, and extract the pus from it, and dry it.  And if it is not open, then open it, and extract its pus.
Then place the cautery, which is made very hot, upon it.
And hold your hand near the cautery, from the eye to the nose, for a short time, so that your hand does not wander or the sick person is not disturbed; and place the cautery on the fat of the eye and corrupt it.
If therefore you have reached the beginning of your cautery up to the bone, it will be well. And if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if you need it, and leave it for three days.
Then place a compress with butter, and treat it with a drying ointment until it is healed.
If forty days have passed and it is not healed, then place a sharp corrosive medicine upon it, until the bone is exposed, and scrape it according to what comes in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula, which some of the first ones said. Aim at the location of the fistula and split it; then place a thin camahan, that is, a thin cannula, whose shape is this, into the fissure. And pour into it a quantity of a drachm of melted lead, and hold your hand with a good retention cannula, and let the sick person not move at all, so that the melted lead does not run or reach his eye.
And it is necessary that you place over the eye of the sick person a cloth soaked in egg white, or in water.
For lead burns the fistula location, and heals it with a marvelous healing.
If the fistula is healed with what we have said from the cautery, and cure, it is good. And if not, then it is necessary to administer a nasal opening, and to reduce the fistula to the course of the nose, according to where it will return to its own proper place.
On the method of cauterizing in the fissures of the lips.
Fissures, called hairs, frequently occur in the lip, especially in the lips of children.
Therefore, when you treat these fissures, as we said in the division of diseases, and if treatment is ineffective, then use a small, knife-like cautery with fire, according to this shape.
And its concavity should be according to the sharpness of the knife.
Then quickly place it, heated, on the fissures themselves, until the cautery reaches the depth of the fissures.
Then treat it with a cauterizing agent until it heals.
Concerning the method of cauterization in an oral fistula.
When an abscess occurs at the root of the gum, or in the palate, or in the roots of the teeth; then it forms pus, and breaks open; afterward it becomes chronic, and pus runs, and a fistula is formed; then you treat it, and treatment is of no avail, then it is necessary to cauterize the fistula with a cautery according to its size. Then introduce the heated iron into the opening of the fistula, and hold your hand until the heated iron reaches its depth and its innermost part; do this once or twice.
Then treat it afterward, with what we have said about treatment until it is cured.
If therefore the pus is drawn out and it heals, it will be good. And if not, then it is necessary to expose the place and remove the corrupted bone according to what appears in its head.
Concerning the method of cauterization in softened gums and teeth.
When the gum softens due to moisture, and the teeth move, and you treat it with medicines, and it does not help, then place the patient's head in your lap.
Then with fire, a cautery, the form of which will come after this, so that you place the cannula on the tooth, and quickly insert the heated cautery into it, and hold your hand a little while, until the patient feels the heat of the fire have already reached the root of the tooth.
And raise your hand; then repeat the cautery several times, according to your will.
Then let the patient fill his mouth with salt water, and let him hold it for one hour, and let him spit it out.
For the moving tooth is strengthened, and the softened gum is tightened, and the corrupt moisture is dried.
Concerning the method of cauterizing toothache.
When toothache is due to cold, either cold or a worm is in it, and treatment with medicine does not help, then the cautery is applied to it in two ways: either cautery with butter, or cautery with fire.
However, the cautery with butter is to take cow's butter, and to boil it in an iron spoon, or in a pan. Then take a cotton ball, and wrap it around the tip of the tent. Then immerse it in the boiled butter, and quickly place it on the aching tooth, and hold it until it cools: then repeat it several times, until the power of fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, immerse wool or cotton in cold butter, and place it on the aching tooth; and place hot iron on it, until the fire reaches the depth of the tooth cavity.
Its cauterization with fire is to apply a bronze or iron tube. And let there be something of thickness in its body, so that the sharpness of the fire does not reach the sick person's bone.
Then apply the cautery, whose shape will be given, and place it upon that tooth; and hold your hand until the cautery cools.
Do it several times, for the pain will be removed, either on that same day, or on another.
And it is necessary, upon the application of that cautery, that the sick person fill his mouth with good butter and hold it for one hour; then he shall spit it out.
And this is the shape of the cautery.
Cauterize with whichever extremity you are able; and this is the form of the cannula.
Concerning the method of cauterization in the Strophuls.
When Strophuls are affected by phlegm and cold humors, and do not yield to digestion through medicines, and you want to hasten their digestion, then use a concave cautery, whose shape is this.
Having two open ends, so that smoke may escape from the cautery through the other end; and place it ignited upon the abscess once, and a second time, if it needs it, until it reaches the depth of the abscess.
If the abscess is small, then place the cautery according to the size of the abscess.
Then leave it for three days, and place upon it a poultice infused in butter, until what the fire burned is removed.
Then treat it with a tooth ointment, until the shape of the cannula heals.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in hoarseness of the voice and constriction of breathing.
When humidities overcome the lung passage, and especially when it is with a cold complexion, then it is necessary that the infirm person be evacuated primarily with dissolving medicines.
Then cauterize it in the nocrati at the root of the throat in a depressed place: and beware, lest you reach the throat with the cautery, nor the skin, unless something of it.
Then cauterize it with another cautery at the joint of the neck in the last vertebra of it, and let the cautery be narrow according to the manner which preceded.
Then treat it with that which preceded, until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterization in illnesses of the lungs and cough.
When cough and illness of the lungs are from cold humors, and the sick person does not have a fever, nor consumption, and the illness is old, then cauterize him with two cauteries above the fork in low, smooth, empty places. And with another cautery in the middle of the chest between the two nipples: and let the cautery be of a cannula according to the form that preceded.
And if your desire is cauterization by puncturing with a cautery, called Punct, whose form preceded in the puncturing of ear pain: and let the punctures be from thirty punctures up to almost another.
Then treat him with what preceded, until he is healed.
And now let the cautery have three branches: according to this form. Therefore, cauterization will be alleviated when you do it with three cauteries at once.
Chapter XXVII.
When the head of the humerus is dislocated due to humidity, and does not settle back into place after the dislocation, until it is accustomed to returning, then it is dislocated by a slight movement, which happens as we have already testified, then it is necessary to reduce the dislocation first. Then the patient shall be placed on their back, or on their healthy side. Then lift the skin, which is on the inner side of the titillico towards the upper part with the fingers of your left hand; and if the joint is dislocated inward.
Then having a cautery with two prongs. Whose shape is this.
Then cauterize the skin until it penetrates it to the other side, and the figure of the cauterization will come with four cauteries.
And sometimes a cauterization is made with a cautery having three prongs, wherefore the figure of cauterization then is of six cauteries: and there are prongs according to the fineness of the thread.
And this is the shape of the cautery having three prongs.
And sometimes one is added over this manner, wherefore there are eight cauteries.
Then place on the cautery bruised leeks with salt. Then make the infirm adhere to quietude, so that the limb does not move at any time until it is strengthened.
And if the dislocation is upwards, and rarely does it happen thus, then cauterize it above the shoulder with a clavicle cautery, or with many cauteries by puncturing.
For the joint is strengthened, and the moisture is removed: and the infirm is cured.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the stomach.
When there occur in the stomach cold and many moistnesses, until they draw it away from its complexion, and the phlegms multiply within it, and frequent methods of treatment do not help: then it is necessary that the sick person lie on their back, and extend their legs and hands. Then cauterize with three cauteries, one cautery beneath the spoon-shaped part of the chest, the size of one finger with a nail cautery, and two cauteries below it on either side, one cautery each, until the shape of the cauteries becomes triangular. And lengthen them between each so that they do not join, when they will cause pus.
And let the depth of the cautery be the amount of two-thirds the thickness of the skin.
And let the shape of the cauteries be according to this form without addition; and if you wish to cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach according to this form: and let the cautery used to make this cautery be one according to the form of a circle in this way.
And the stomach is cauterized by puncturing it for him who is terrified by this cautery; and it is, as if you mark on the stomach a point according to the quantity that you wish with ink; then cauterize it with the point cautery; then treat it with the treatment that preceded until it is healed.
Concerning the manner of cauterizing a cold liver.
When pain occurs in the liver due to cold and moisture, or to thick wind, until it emerges from its natural complexion by a superfluous discharge, and the sick person is treated with what we have said, and it does not benefit him: then it is necessary that the sick person be placed supine on their neck, and mark with ink three cauteries according to this figure and this same quantity on the lower liver from the hypochondria, where the human elbow reaches; and let the space between each cautery be according to the thickness of a finger; and let the cautery be straight according to the length of the body; and do not press your hand with the cautery too much.
And let the quantity of what is burned be the thickness of the skin, the quantity of half of it not added; and let the sick person be standing on their feet.
If, however, he is not standing, then let him lie down, having already extended his legs and raised his arms.
This is the form of the cautery.
And it is possible for you, indeed, to cauterize these cauteries with a knife cautery, when you have subtlety and prudence in the art.
And guard yourself so that you do not proceed too far in the cauterization, and the thickness of the entire skin is burned, wherefore the whole body is burned, and it reaches the intestines: for the skin there is thin, therefore know this.
Concerning the method of cauterizing liver abscesses.
When an exit occurs, and you wish to know if the abscess is in the flesh of the liver, or in its tunic: then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the sick person is invaded by pain and heaviness without sharpness; and if it is in the tunic of the liver, there is intense sharpness with the pain: and you see now, that the treatment has fatigued its physicians.
Therefore, it is necessary that the sick person lie on their back. Then mark the location of the abscess with ink; then heat the cautery, which resembles a ray. And this is its form. And cauterize with it a single cautery, until the entire skin is burned and reaches the suppuration, until all the pus comes out; then treat it with the treatment for exits, until it is healed.
And it is not fitting that one use this kind of cautery, unless someone whose study in the medical art is prolonged, and through whose hands these illnesses have come with experience many times; then indeed let him dare upon this kind of operation.
But truly, to leave it alone, is better in my opinion.
Concerning the method of cauterization in Pleuresy.
The ancients said that cautery with aristolochia roots should be applied to a cold pleurisy in this manner. And it is that you take from the dry long aristolochia roots, one root longer than the others, and which is the thickness of a finger. Then immerse it in oil and light it in fire. Then cauterize with one cautery at the junction of the fork and the neck, and with two small cauteries slightly sloping downward on the part below the beard.
And with two cauteries also on that which is between the fifth and sixth ribs, sloping slightly backward; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest, and another above the stomach. And with three cauteries from behind: one on the area between the two shoulder blades; and with two cauteries on the two sides of the chest, below the cautery between the shoulder blades.
And you should not compress your hand with the cautery, rather it should be clearly visible when smelling the skin.
And some ancient ones have said that there are men who use a cautery of iron similar to a rod; and they burn, and insert it into that which is between the ribs, until they reach the abscess with it, and pus comes out; as we have said in the case of liver abscess.
But in this perforation with the cautery there is a fear that either the weak man dies immediately, or a fistula occurs in the place, which has no cure.
Of the method of cauterizing the Spleen Chapter XXXII.
When you treat the spleen's ailment, with what we have said about treatment in the division of illnesses, and your treatment does not help, then the cautery is used for it in three ways, all of which are correct.
One of which is to make three cauteries, or four, by lining them along the length of the spleen in the manner of the liver cauteries, whose figure preceded.
And let there be between each cautery a quantity equal to the thickness of a finger, or a little more or less.
And let the method of the cautery be as we have said in the cautery of the liver, equally.
And do not press your hand with the cautery: and let the sick person be lying weak on his back.
And another method of cauterization is: that you heat a cautery having two points, as we said in the chapter on the dislocation of the shoulder; and lift the skin, which is directly over the spleen, where the sick person's left elbow reaches; and let your lifting of the skin be according to the width of the body, so that the cauteries fall along the length of the body.
Then insert the two very hot points, until you penetrate the skin with them on the other side; then withdraw the cautery; thus four cauteries are made.
And if you wish to cauterize with another cautery having three points.
Then treat the cautery after you have let it discharge pus for many days; for it is more beneficial than any other preceding treatment.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in dropsy.
The cautery does not fall upon dropsy unless applied properly.
When you treat a dropsical patient with the methods of treatment, which we have stated in the division, and the treatment is not beneficial, then you should cauterize him with four cauteries around the navel, and one cautery on the stomach, and another on the liver, and one on the spleen, and two cauteries on the back between the vertebrae, and one directly on the chest, and another directly on the stomach: and let the depth of the cautery be near the thickness of the skin.
Then leave the cautery open, so that it discharges pus for a long time.
And do not remove the sick person from treatment after the cautery with him; for two intentions should be gathered to him: and healing will hasten to him with the help of God.
The shape of the cautery with which the belly is cauterized should be pointed, according to its preceding shape; and that with which the back is cauterized should be olive-shaped, according to its preceding shape.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the feet and legs.
When abscesses form in a hydrops patient, and they fill with yellow water, it is necessary to cauterize on the back of the foot in the nocrati, which is between the auricular and annular [parts], and raise your hand with the cautery, and do not twist it; then raise your hand, and do not repeat it at all.
For the yellow water will sweat out, and the cautery should be of this shape.
Then, make two cauteries on each leg, and let the cautery be made with the knife-like extremity of the cautery; and let the cautery be made along the length of the leg, one below the knee, and the other below that, approximately in the middle of the leg; and on each thigh, make two cauteries; and leave the cautery open without treatment for a long time, so that some water will sweat out from it; then treat it with the rest of what we have said.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in abdominal distension.
Chapter 35.
When abdominal distension is due to cold and moisture, weakening the retentive and digestive powers in the stomach and intestines; and it is treated by the methods of cure, but not healed; and you see the infirm patient tolerating a cautery of exuberant strength, then cauterize him with a large cautery over his stomach, according to what preceded in cauterizing the stomach with a circular cautery, and four subtle cauteries in the circumference of the navel, with a fine claviform cautery, and a large cautery over the alcatin, above the alhosos, or two cauteries.
And if you see excessive moisture, and the patient tolerates it, then cauterize him with a cautery over the pubis, and a cautery over each hip; and perhaps add two small cauteries near the large one on the stomach.
For it is a beneficial treatment, whose help does not err.
Concerning the method of cauterizing hemorrhoids.
Chapter XXXVI.
When in the anus there are many old hemorrhoids, or one, and they are from thick, cold humors, or corrupted moistures, and are treated with what we have said in the division, and the treatment does not help: then cauterize with three cauteries above the lower spine of the back a little triangularly, and with a cautery under the navel as if with two fingers.
And if you think that his stomach is chilled, and his food is not digested, and you see his face becoming ulcerated, then cauterize him on the stomach with a large cautery, as previously described; and cauterize him on his liver, and another on his spleen, with a clauile cautery; and leave the cautery open for some time; then treat him until he is healed.
Chapter XXXVII.
When you excise althelul, then heat the cautery that resembles a ray. Then introduce it, heated, into the excised althelul; and finally, move your hand until the cautery reaches the opening of the vein from which the blood flows: do it once or twice.
If therefore there are many althelul, then cauterize each one with the cautery as we have described. Then treat them with that which you apply from the ointments, until they are healed.
And if you cauterize it with a large cautery over the alcatin, it is more effective in helping.
Concerning the method of cauterizing a fistula in the anus.
Chapter Thirty-eight.
When the sick person does not respond to the incision and operation we described in its place, and is frightened by it, perhaps he is cured with a cautery.
Therefore, when a fistula occurs in someone, and it becomes old, pus flows from it, and corrupted humors. Therefore, it is fitting that you probe it with a fine probe. Then ascertain its depth with the probe.
Then heat the cautery, which is similar to a ray. Then introduce the heated cautery into the fistula itself, and the extent to which the cautery entered.
And repeat the cautery over it, until all those corrupt bodies are burned, once, or twice, or thrice, according to your necessary quantity.
And guard yourself from burning the nerve, if there is one there, or a large vein; or if the fistula goes to the body of the bladder, or to the body of the intestines, then guard yourself from all these places.
And do not do that, unless when the fistula is in a fleshy place and you think that it is not penetrating.
Then treat the place until it is healed.
If, however, the place is sealed, and the materials are cut from it, and it remains like that for a long time, then know that it is now completely healed.
And if materials are not cut from it, then know that it is penetrating, or a corrupted bone is deep within it, or something like that from those: whose mention will come in its place.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the kidneys.
When pain in the kidneys occurs from cold or strong wind, and it lessens because of that infirmity, then it is necessary that you cauterize it above the almethen [region] above the kidneys, cauterizing each kidney with a clauili cautery, whose description preceded.
And perhaps we cauterize it a second time in the almeidan itself. Therefore three lines will appear. Therefore the latter will be more beneficial.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the bladder.
When weakness and softening of the bladder occur due to cold and dampness, to the point that the weakened person cannot retain urine, then cauterize it on the lower part of the summit above the bladder, where the hairs of the pubic region begin.  Cauterize with one cautery from the right side of the summit, and another from the left.  Let the length of each cauterization on the side be according to the size of a thumb's breadth.  And cauterize it with a cautery from the lower back, or with two cauteries if one is insufficient.  Let it be as the previous cautery, a closed cautery.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the womb.
When cold and dampness occur in the womb, and it is prevented from conception by this, and her menses are corrupted, and their expulsion is delayed, and pain occurs to her upon their arrival, then it is necessary that she be cauterized with three cauteries around the navel, as we said in the cauterization of the bladder, and with a cautery above the lower back, or with two cauteries.  And let the cautery be closed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the anus in dislocation.
When mucous humors are discharged into the anus's pouch, and cause its exit from its place: and a sign of this is that one leg is prolonged over the other when compared together; and the place of the dislocation is found empty in it.
Therefore, it is necessary to cauterize the affected area over the anus's pouch with a similar circular cautery; after you mark with ink around the pouch, as it revolves; and so that the pouch itself falls into the middle of the circle.
If this cautery is not available to you, then cauterize it with three cauteries, with a large olive-shaped cautery; and let the cauterization's depth be according to the thickness of the whole skin.
Then treat it until it heals.
Concerning the method of cauterization in Sciatica.
When pain occurs in the hip joint, and its cause is cold and moisture; and the patient is treated with what we have mentioned in the division, and the treatment does not help, and it is prolonged: then it is necessary to relieve the patient from thick humors with foul pills, or with artificial pills, and similar remedies. Then cauterize it.
And the method of cauterization in it is according to two methods: either cauterization with burning medicines, or cauterization with fire.
Cauterization with fire is according to many methods.
One of which is that you cauterize above the hip joint itself with three triangular cauteries, according to this shape. And make them a good depth, or a bad one. And let the space between each cautery be the thickness of a finger; and let the cautery be olive-shaped.
Sometimes however, the cautery is made in the middle above the head of the hip joint itself. Therefore there are four cauteries.
And if you wish to cauterize with a circle, whose form preceded in the cauterization of the hip dislocation, one: so that the circle contains the entire hip.
And burn the thickness of the entire skin.
This is the method of cautery for sciatica, when the pain is adhering to the hip itself, and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
However, if it extends to the thigh and leg, then cauterize it with two cauteries on the thigh, over the place to which the infirm person there points, where the pain is, and with your cautery over the ankle four fingers to the wild side for a little while: and let the cautery be a knife-like one.
And let the depth of the cautery be according to the depth of the thickness of the skin.
If the sick person indicates that the pain extends nearly to the toes of the foot, then cauterize it there where he indicates with a point cautery, three, or four, or more, if he needs it.
And if he indicates the pain to be below the knee towards the leg, then cauterize it there with one knife-like cautery.
And beware in all your cauterizations, that you do not proceed with the cautery to the point of burning a nerve, or a large artery, and thereby bring upon the sick person a bad injury, or a contraction.
And indeed I have already seen one and another, of those who cauterized above the ankle, and went too far in their cauterization, and the leg became swollen, until the swelling reached the foot, and the whole was perforated, and the whole foot was corrupted: then came a loosening of the belly, and death after that.
If the pain is in two parts at once, cauterize each according to this method.
And some wise men said regarding the cauterization of the buttock, of which this is the method of cautery. Make a cautery iron similar to a Samian one, whose diameter is half a palm: and let there be at its opening according to the thickness of a date stone, or a little less: and within that cautery, let there be a third cautery.
And let the space between each cautery be the quantity of a thumb's knot: and let the cauteries be open on both sides: and let their height be like a knot, or two knots: and make for them a handle of iron, fitted well to the cauteries.
Then let it be heated in fire until it glows red and throws off sparks; then let it be placed on the box of the buttock of the sick person lying on the healthy side. Therefore cauterize him with three round cauteries at one time.
Then leave him for three days. Afterwards, apply plasters with butter, and leave the wound open for many days. Then treat him with ointment until he is healed.
Albucasis says, by this type of cautery, O children, we have used little, because of the terror of its appearance, and because we have found few who will wait upon it.
However, it is from good cauteries for him who suffers, and he finds with it its place.
A cautery, however, is with burning medicines, so that you make two samia having a perforated armilla, into which the hinge of its door descends, of bronze or iron, the length of a wall; and whose height they should have two knots, or approximately themselves.
And let the space that is between both be the thickness of a finger, and let them be open below and above, and let that which contains them adhere to each other, according to this form.
Then place it upon the box of the anca, the sick person lying on their healthy side. And press your hand heavily upon the box of the anca. Then pour between the two circles hot water, which has been heated a little with fire, and hold it for a quantity of an hour's time; and let the sick person tolerate its burning and its cauterization, for he finds the burning like fire, until the burning subsides.
Then raise your hand with the instrument, and wipe the whole anca with sweet water from the hot water; then leave it for three days.
And place butter over it until the blackness of the cauterization is removed; and leave it for a few days, and pus will run from it. For it is the final help.
Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
If the pain extends to the hip or leg, make a poultice on it containing water, as you did on the anca equally.
And this is the method of hot water: and I have already written it in the treatise on the rectification of medicines. Take alkali and quicklime, both one part, grind both, and place them in a new pot, whose lower part has a small hole, according to the quantity that the radius enters: and place another glass pot under the bottom of the pot: and pour over the alkali and lime with fresh water, that both are submerged by a finger: after which you press them with your hand with a good pressure: and leave the pot until the acute water descends to the lower part of the glass pot.
Then gather all that water and pour it over more lime, and strain it until it is renewed.
For it will then be very strong and sharp, which is administered in many medical operations and in the cauterization of other limbs. Because it produces the action of fire itself.
And among the medicines with which the anchas are cauterized, also as with tesisie, and honey of anacardi, and lime with soap, so that both are mixed together.
And Galen, indeed, narrating, reported the cure of a certain man from the ancients, by which the pain of the ancha and sciatic vein was cured: and he magnified that it was very great. And he said, because he did not need any other cure, and because he was healed in one instance, until he reported, because he perhaps entered a bath carried, and emerged from it already healthy.
And it is to be taken from fresh seitaragi: and if fresh is not found, take dry, fresh: and it is to be ground a great deal with a little fat, and placed on the ancha, where the pain is, or leg, or thigh: and it is to be bound, and left for the quantity of three hours, or for the quantity that the sick person feels a lessening of the burning. Then put him into a bath.
When therefore his body becomes moist, put him into a couch. For the pain recedes, and he is healed.
And know that it is not fitting for anyone to use a cautery with these remedies unless after evacuation of the body.
And Dioscorides, who said that the dung of goats, when the sciatic vein is cauterized with it, helps it: and the cautery is done in this way. Take wool, and soak it in old oil, and place it over the deep place that is between the toe and the ankle, and is closer to the ankle.
Then take dry goat dung, and burn it in fire until it becomes charcoal.
Then place it on the wool, and leave it until it is extinguished. Then repeat it. Therefore do not cease to do this until feeling comes about by mediation to the ankle, and the pain ceases.
And this method of cautery is called the Vaccinum cautery.
Concerning the cauterization of back pain.
Sometimes pain occurs in the back due to many causes, either from an accident, or a blow, or an evacuation of superfluous matter, and similar things, from the infusion of cold, damp matter: and indeed cautery does not fall upon, except in this single kind, which is brought about by the infusion of cold matter.
Therefore, after the patient's evacuation with foul pills, and similar things, let three lines be made upon the back, where the pain is, according to the width of the affected area itself; after marking with ink five cauteries on each line, or more according to the amount that comes from the tolerance of the patient's strength: and let cauterization be performed with the cautery-point.
And if you wish, cauterize with three or four cauteries, with the middle cautery-iron, according to this form.
Concerning the method of cauterization in the beginning stages of a hump.
This illness often happens to young infants.
And the sign of its beginning in children is that a constriction occurs in their breathing during elevation and movement, and you find in the lowest thoracic vertebrae and their nodes that it has already begun to project above the other vertebrae.
Therefore, when you see it and wish to treat it, then cauterize it with a cautery that is circular in this shape.
And let the cautery encompass the entire vertebra equally; and if you wish to cauterize around the vertebra with a pointed cautery, make two or three lines; and let the points be close together.
Then treat the place until it is healed with what we have said.
And beware, lest you apply the cautery to the hump which arises from a nerve spasm.
Concerning the method of cauterization in gout and joint pains.
When the pains of the joints are from cold humors that flow to any member of the body, then when such pains occur in the feet, it is the custom of physicians to call it gout properly.
Therefore, when gout of a cold cause is treated with methods of cure, and the pains are not removed, then the cautery removes them.
And it is to cauterize it after evacuation, around the joint of the feet, with many cauteries; and let the cautery be olive-shaped.
And if you need to pierce on the surface of the foot, then do it with a pointed cautery. If the pains ascend to the knees, or other joints, and this often happens, then cauterize it above each knee with three or four cauteries on every side, with this same claw-shaped cautery.
If, however, more of this cautery is necessary for you, then cauterize it. And do not immerse your hand with the cautery, but let it be about the thickness of the skin only.
If the pains ascend to the knees and back, then administer what we have said about cautery in its chapter.
If the pains are only in the hands, then puncture in a circular fashion around the foci, as if two lines are revolving.
If however the pain remains in the fingers, then puncture them, above every knot, and above the palm of the hand.
If, however, the pains ascend after days to the elbows or shoulders, then cauterize both from every side.
And do not evacuate the sick person from good regimen, from the reception of medicines. For if a good regimen is made, and phlegm is evacuated, it will be healed with the help of God with this cautery.
Concerning the method of cauterization in Ruptures.
When a rupture occurs in the groins, and the intestine or the zirbum descends to the testicle, and it is beginning to be near: then it is necessary that you command the sick person to abandon eating on his day. And let him use from the lenitives, so that the excrement descends from his belly.
Then let him sit between your hands on his back; and command him to hold his breath, until it emerges, or the zirbum or the intestine appears.
Then reduce it with your fingers, and mark with ink under the rupture above the pectineal arch, with a sign whose extremity is similar to half a circle towards the upper part of the body.
Then with a cautery of this form, until it becomes white and throws off sparks. Then reduce the intestine, or zirbum, to its concavity. Then let the attendant place his hand on the area, so that the intestine does not come out; and you have already opened between the patient's legs, and placed a cushion beneath him, and another attendant above his legs, and another above his chest holding his hands.
Then place the cautery upon that mark, your hand with the cautery standing upright. And hold it until with it you reach the mouth; or again with it another time, if you have not reached with it the mouth at first.
And carefully observe the exit of the intestine during your cauterization, lest you burn it, and death or great harm befall the patient through it.
And know that when you do not reach the mouth with the cautery, your operation is of no avail.
And it is necessary that the cautery for children be subtle, according to their size.
Then treat with the remaining ointments until it is healed.
And let the patient lie on their back for forty days, until the wound is sealed.
And it is necessary that you provide him nourishment for the duration of his treatment, that which will soothe his stomach so that his intestines do not expel during attempts at and acts of evacuation.
Then when he wishes to rise after forty days, then administer a properly made bandage, and let him keep it for another forty days.
And let him lessen his work, and the fullness of food and drink, and loud noises.
For when he uses this regimen, thus he is healed with complete healing.
And I will speak of the treatment of Ruptures with incision in its chapter.
When, however, Ruptures occur in the rest of the belly, and are incipient, and the will is that they do not add, then cauterize the Rupture with a round cautery according to its size: and let that which is burned be from then on as one third of it. Then treat him with what we have said: and it will not be added.
And already some early ones have said that the Rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery, according to this form.
After it is marked with ink, and a line is placed which is across the width on the upper side of the Rupture, and another line below. And place your hand in the middle with the cautery-iron.
But the first cautery is easier and better.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in pains due to a fall or blow.
Chapter XLVIII.
When in certain limbs, pain from a fall or blow occurs and persists for a long time, and treatment with medicines does not help, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cautery of every limb be a cautery according to its size, and its smallness, and its weakness, and its strength, and the location of the twisting, and the pain from it.
If, therefore, it is healed by the first cautery, it is well. If not, then repeat the cautery on it.
Because it is customary for these pains to shift from one limb to the one adjacent to it. Therefore, it is necessary to pursue them with cautery until the infirmity is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in Leprosy.
Leprosy patients are sometimes greatly helped by cautery. And especially the species of leprosy which arises from the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy: therefore, when you intend to cauterize it, consider its nature.
If the leprosy is extensive, and you have treated it as we have described in the divisions, and it does not decrease nor disappear, and you fear that the corruption will spread throughout the entire constitution, then cauterize the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the noted head, and a second below it towards the forehead at the end of the hair, and two above the two horns, and one behind on the base of the neck.
And lastly, hold your cautery for a little while, until it imprints a small mark on the area, and the layers are loosened from it: so that the expulsion of gross vapors from it may be alleviated; and also cauterize the spleen with the cautery, according to what has been said before.
But if the leprosy has already spread over the patient, and is clearly visible, then it is necessary to cauterize with the cauteries we mentioned for the head, and with a cautery on the end of the nose, and two cauteries on the two jawbones, and two cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck, and six on the vertebrae of the back, and one large one on the haunches near the base of the tail, and one above it, in the same region; and two on the hips; namely, one on each hip, and two on the knees; one on each knee, and two on the shoulders, and two on the elbows, and two on the soft parts of the chest; and cauterize on every joint, from the joints of the fingers of his hands and feet; and cauterize on each heel of his feet, and on the fleshy parts of his hands.
And beware of the nerves that are above the last of the heels, so that you do not burn them.
And sometimes a cautery is applied to the bone of the breastbone, and another to the bone of the stomach, and another to the liver.
And know that the more you apply the cautery, the more help and benefit there is.
And know that the sick person does not find pain in the cautery as healthy people do, because his body is already numb.
And it is necessary that the cauteries be of a size, and smallness, according to the members and joints, according to what has gone before in the types of instruments.
Then treat the cautery with flour of the herb with honey, and the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in a state of stupor.
When any limb is numb, and is treated with medicines, and oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then cauterize that numbed limb with cauteries, according to the deserved size of the limb, or its smallness.
And let your cautery be entering into the thickness of the skin a little. Then treat it with an ointment, until it is healed.
And sometimes it is cauterized for some numbness, which happens to the hand and foot, in the spines of the back at the exit of the nerves, which move that limb: and the numbness is removed.
But let no one presume upon it, unless he is wise in the anatomy of the limbs, and the exit of the nerves moving the body.
Chapter LI.
When Albaras is aged and no remedy from the arts of medicine is applied to it, then cauterize above it itself with a deep cautery for a short time, according to the thickness of the skin, until the whiteness is removed and its color is changed.
Then treat it with flour of herbs, and lentils with rose oil, and arnoglossa leaves, and pigeon's blood, and swallow's blood; of each take one part; mix these all together, and cover a cloth with it, and apply it to the affected area until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterization in Cancer.
When Cancer is beginning, and you wish to stop it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it revolves.
However, the wise men said that it should be cauterized with the final cautery in the middle.
However, it does not seem right to me, because I fear it will ulcerate, and I have seen that happen several times already.
And it is better that it be cauterized around it with a circle, as we said, or with many cauteries.
Concerning the method of cauterization in Adubelati.
When someone has Adubelati, and it is delayed in maturation, either because of excess causing it, or because of the patient's age, when he is decrepit with little blood, or because of the time, and the need to hurry the maturation of the Adubelati, then cauterize around it with many small cauteries by puncturing. Then leave it. For it will hasten with maturation.
If however you wish a perforation of it with a cautery, then heat the cautery, which has this form.
And place it in the middle of the Adubelati, until it penetrates the skin; and let the cautery be from that which follows the patient, so that the course of purulence is relieved.
Then treat it until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in a corroding evil.
Chapter LIII.
Corrosion is not unless it is a corruption that walks in a limb, and it corrodes it; just as fire consumes dry wood.
Therefore, if you see corrosion in a place that can tolerate a cautery with fire, then use many cauteries of fire, small and large irons, according to what is suitable for the place where the corrosion is.
Then cauterize it from every side until you eradicate the entire corruption, and nothing of it remains at all.
Then leave it for three days, and put on the cauterized places ground sulfur with oil, until all the eschars and all corruption are eradicated.
Then treat it with ointments that generate flesh.
If, therefore, you see that after three days healthy flesh is born, in which there is no corruption, it is good.
And if not, then repeat the cautery on whatever remained of the corrupted places.
And it is sometimes cured by corroding medicine. For indeed it arises in the place of the cautery.
However, cautery with fire is of quicker help; and I have already said that its cauterization with corroding medicine is in division; therefore take it there when you need it.
On the method of cauterization in corns and warts.
Chapter LV.
Often this disease occurs in the lower parts of the feet, and it is a thick, filtered substance that harms the foot.
And cautery in them is in two ways: cautery with fire, and cautery with hot water.
As for cautery with fire, make a concave cautery like a vulture's wing, which is made of iron, according to the size of the corn from all sides, and with thin edges.
Then place it, heated, upon the corn. Then rotate the hand with the cautery around the corn, until the cautery reaches the depth of the corn, and leave it for three days, until it begins to emit pus. Then plaster it with crushed wild mallows and salt, and leave the plaster on it overnight. For it will be uprooted from its roots.
Then treat the area with a generative flesh ointment until it heals.
If they are not cured, and often occur on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet, then apply a cautery with hot water to them. And it is, that you take a cannula of bronze or iron or from a vulture's feather, and place it on the corn or whitlow. Then introduce a small amount of hot water into the cannula, hold your hand, and rotate it with the pressure of your hand for a short time, so that the edge of the cannula presses against the root of the corn, and the hot water finds its way deep into the root of the corn.
