Let the patient wait a little while on the hot water's action for an hour.
Then release it, for the corn is uprooted with its roots.
So do with them one by one, until you come upon all that are on the body.
Then treat them after they are uprooted, with that which generates flesh from ointments.
Concerning the method of cauterization in Tremore.
When tremor occurs in someone from cold in a nerve, or from quartan fever, or otherwise: then it is necessary that you cauterize it with four cauteries, or five, over the vertebrae of the back, and between each vertebra with a cautery, and on his chest with a cautery, and on his stomach with a cautery.
For tremor ceases with the olive-shaped cautery, and the healing of cold illness is hastened.
Concerning the method of cauterizing Albathir.
Chapter LVII.
Sometimes foul things, that is, pustules, are expelled in the body of Albathir, which are formed from corrupted gross matter.
Therefore, when they appear, it is necessary to cauterize every pustule on the head with a fine cautery made of myrtle wood, whose tip is ignited with fire. Or with the root of long aristolochia. Or with a lenticular cautery.
And sometimes they are cauterized at the beginning of their expulsion in this way: and they are not increased; and the superfluity that makes them is broken, and they are healed from them.
However, it is necessary for this to be done after bloodletting.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in the flow of blood from an artery in a wound.
Often, a flow of blood occurs from an artery that has been cut in a wound, either externally, or in an incision of an abscess, or a cauterization of a limb, and similar things: and it is difficult to stop it.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then quickly apply your index finger to the opening of the artery, and press it firmly until the blood is contained beneath your finger, and nothing escapes from it.
Then place small, olive-shaped cauteries, and many large ones, in the fire, and blow on them until they become very hot.
Then take one of them, whether small or large, according to the wound and the place where the artery has been ruptured, and place the cautery upon the vein, after you have quickly removed your finger; and hold the cautery until the blood is stopped.
If it is expelled when you lift your finger from the opening of the artery, and you extinguish the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from those prepared in the fire.
Therefore do not cease doing this with one after another until the blood is stopped.
And beware, lest you burn the nerve which is there, and another harm befall the sick person.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, then it is not possible to stop it; especially when the artery is large, unless by one of the four methods. Or with a cautery, as we have said. Or with extraction, when it is not extracted. For when it is extracted, its extremities contract, and the blood is cut off. Or it is to be tied with a strong ligature. Or medicines of a nature to cut off blood are placed upon it, and it is bound with a fitting constriction by means of a cushion.
However, he who performs its excision with a ligature, and constriction with a cloth, or the application of burning things, and the like, does not truly help it, except rarely.
If however this happens to someone, and a physician, or medicine, is not present for him, then let him begin with the positioning of the index finger upon the orifice of the vein itself, as we have narrated, and its forceful constriction, until the blood is obstructed from the upper part of the wound, and above the artery. And the finger is not to be removed, above it continually with very cold water, until the blood has congealed, and thickened, and been cut off.
And meanwhile, let him consider in it what it needs from a cautery or from medicine.
Concerning Incisions, Perforations, and Phlebotomy in wounds, and similar things.
We have already said in the first chapter, that every illness in which it is appropriate to use cautery with fire and burning medicine, and the occasions for it, the causes themselves, and the instruments, and the forms of cauteries.
And I have placed there chapters from the head to the foot.
And I shall proceed in this chapter along the same path: so that the seeker may have his question relieved.
And before I begin that, it is necessary for you, my children, to learn that in this chapter is contained the terror that is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And because of that, it is necessary that there be greater caution in it, since in the operation which is in this chapter, many times evacuation occurs with blood, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or incision above an abscess, or opening of an exit, or the treatment of a wound, or the extraction of an arrow, or incision above a stone, and similar to these: from all of which tremor and fear are associated, and death occurs in many of them.
And I warn you, lest that in which there is hesitation befall you, for sometimes in this art the ways of men fall upon you along with the ways of illnesses. For among them is he who is angry with his illness, and death is slight to him; because of the severity he finds in his illness and the length of his malice. And with illness is the fear that signifies death.
And among them is he who gives his instrument, and enriches you with it, hoping for health, and his illness is harmful.
Therefore, you should not agree with him who comes to you from among them, whose manner is such. And let your caution be more intense than your greed and your rapacity. And do not presume upon anything from it, unless after certain knowledge, which verifies for you that to which a praiseworthy success has arrived.
And use in the treatment of all your sick people, prognostication and indication concerning that to which health is fittingly achieved.
For in that is help for you concerning the acquisition of fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire you, children, with his understanding, and may he not deprive you of direction and inspiration; for it is in his hand.
And I have indeed arranged this chapter, distinguishing it into sections, according to what preceded in the chapter on cauterization, from the neck to the feet, so that your inquiries concerning it, and what you need, may be eased.
Concerning the treatment of water that gathers in the heads of children.
Chapter 1.
Know that this illness often happens to children at birth, or when the midwife tightens the child's head without easing it.
And sometimes it happens also from a hidden cause that is not known.
And I myself have not seen this illness in anything but children, and death hastened to every one whom I saw afflicted by it; therefore, it seemed to me to omit treatment in it.
And indeed I saw one child among them whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger every day, until he could not sit on himself because of the size of his head; and the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture, indeed, either collects between the skin and the bone, or collects under the bone on top of the sifac.
And the treatment in that, if the moisture is between the skin and the bone, and the abscess is small, then it is necessary that you cut a single fissure in the middle of the head, in width, and let the length of the fissure be like two knots, until the moisture runs out.
And this is the form of the spatule.
If therefore the moisture is more increased, and the abscess is larger, then place those two fissures, according to this form.
And if the moisture is under the bone, and the sign of it is that you see the sutures of the head open from every side, and water is depressed when you press it with your hand inwards, and it is not hidden from you: then it is necessary that you cut in the middle of the head with three fissures, according to this method.
And after the incision, extract all the moisture. Then bind the fissures with cloths and poultices. Then apply a liniment of wine and oil over it until the fifth day.
Then loosen the bandage, and treat the wound with tents and ointments.
And do not allow the head to be bound with equality. And feed the infirm person with all dry foods of little moisture, until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And another method of incision is to consider where the size of the abscess and the collection of water appear.
Because sometimes there is more in the occiput, or in the forehead, or in the right, or in the left.
Therefore, ascend with the incision where the abscess and the filling of water appear to you: and cut it according to what is possible for you.
And observe, lest you cut an artery, and a flow of blood occur, and the infirm person die from that flow with the evacuation of moisture.
Concerning the incision of arteries that are behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When cataracts occur in someone's eyes, or in the chest, and it is prolonged, and treatment with medicines does not help, then the ultimate treatment in that case is the incision of these two arteries.
Therefore, when you wish to cut them, the infirm person should shave his head. Then rub the area with a rough cloth, so that the arteries become visible. Then the infirm person should tie his neck with any cloth of his. Then consider where the vein beats. And the places of both are the places that are depressed behind the ears: and it is rarely hidden, except in some men.
Then mark with ink each one: and cut each one with a scalpel precisely on both sides, the incision reaching the bone: and let it be done across the breadth of the head.
And if you wish, insert the scalpel under the artery, and cut it above with the incision.
And the length of the incision be like two joined fingers.
For when a vein is cut, blood emerges with a pulsating outflow, which leaps inward with frequent jumps.
If however the artery is not apparent to the senses, then it is necessary to measure from the ear a space of three fingers' breadth. Then mark with ink, and cut until you reach the bone.
And that which it is necessary to expel from the blood, is six ounces, and perhaps you expel from it more, or less. All of that according to the amount that appears to you from the strength of the sick person and the fullness of his artery.
Then consider the wound. Then if anything remains adhering to the bone from the sifac, then cut it away, so that a hot abscess does not occur; then bind the wound with bandages of linen cloth.
Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
Concerning the extraction of arteries, which are in the temples.
Chapter III.
When a man suffers from chronic hemicrania, or acute catarrhs due to acrid humors, and heat in the temple's muscles, or chronic, intense, and similar afflictions: and is treated with the usual methods of medicine, and it does not help: then we are experienced in these illnesses in the extraction of arteries from the temples, or in their cauterization, as we have recounted.
And the method of operation in extracting them is that the weak man should shave the hair that is in the temples, and aim at the artery appearing in the temple. For it indeed shows itself to you by its pulse, and is rarely hidden except in a few men, or by intense cold.
If it is hidden from you, then the weak man should bind his head with any cloth of his: then rub the area with a rough cloth. Or apply steam to the area with warm water, until it appears to you in a clear manifestation. Then take the spatula, which I formed a little earlier. Then scrape the skin with it, easily, until you reach the artery. Then insert a hook into it, and pull it upwards, until you pull it out of the skin, and cause it to escape from the coverings which are beneath it on all sides.
If however the artery is slender: then wrap it with the hook's end. Then cut a part from it, according to the amount that its ends will be elongated, and contracted, and that a flow of blood will not occur. For if it is not cut, and if it is not severed, the blood does not ascend completely.
Then evacuate six ounces of blood, up to three.
If the artery is large, then it is necessary to tie it in two places with a strong, double thread, of silk or other strong cord, so that putrefaction does not quickly occur before the wound is healed and a flow of blood occurs.
Then cut away the excess that is between the two ligatures. Do that at that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery to the bone, with a knife cautery, until its extremities are cut, and it will stand in place of the same operation.
And it is a better operation, unless the sick person has a fever, or is of a hot complexion. Because cautery is from among those things that help in ending moistures. Therefore, it is a better help.
And it is necessary after the extraction of the arteries, that the place be filled with old cotton, and cushions properly made be placed upon it.
And after the removal, treat it with dry medicines that cause flesh to grow, and with dressings, until it is healed.
If meanwhile, during the operation, a flow of blood from an artery occurs, then hurry to cut it off with a cautery, or to fill the place with zegi, and tightly bind your hand, until the blood is cut off. If none of those things are present for you, then place your finger on the place, until the blood coagulates. And embrocate the place with water of intense cold, until the sharpness subsides, and bind it as necessary.
And that which is easier and less difficult than extracting an artery, is to cauterize the vein with this cautery having two blades: and let the sharpness of the two blades be similar to that of a date stone. However, they are of much less sharpness than two blades. Because if they are sharp, like two blades, the cold proceeds in both of them; and they do not cut flesh with speed. Therefore, since there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire is held within them, and the flesh is cut quickly: with this, therefore, the work is better than any other, and easier and more easily done.
And this is the form of the cautery.
And let the space between the two blades be the thickness of a finger, after you mark two places with ink: and place both of them intensely heated, until they reach the bone, and cut the vein in two places: so that that which is between its extremities may be stretched out. For they do not heal completely with this cautery.
Chapter IV.
Concerning the treatment of persistent acute lachrymal discharge from the eyes.
When the tears are persistent, and their flow is from the veins on the exterior of the skull, and no medical treatment with medicines is effective, and you see the patient's face already reddened, and he feels a crawling sensation on his forehead, like the crawling of ants; and his eyes are weakened, moist, so that their hairs are already corroded; and his eyelids are heated by the intensity of the tears; then the ancients needed this operation for their treatment, and they named this treatment, with the Knife.
And it is necessary to instruct the patient to shave the hairs that are on his forehead. Then make three equally spaced incisions on the forehead, along the length of the forehead; and the length of each incision should be about two fingers; and one incision should be equidistant to the length of the nose, in the middle of the forehead, and the second slightly offset from the movement of the temples, and the third on the other side; and beware of cutting the arteries that are on both sides; and keep your hand away from the continuation of the two jaws, and the space of each incision should be the width of three joined fingers; and have prepared for you a piece of sponge, or many dried cloths, with which you will dry the blood.
Then insert a spatula with two sharp ends, whose shape follows next. From the incision that is on the temple side, up to the second incision; and scrape away all the skin that is between the two incisions, with the spatula, that is above the bone. Then do that also from the middle incision to the second incision. Then pull out this spatula, and insert into the first incision again another instrument, which is called a sharp scalpel, smooth on one side, not sharp on the other side. And this is its shape.
And place its sharp side towards the flesh attached with the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone; and push it, until it reaches the middle fissure; and cut with it all the vessels, which descend from the head to the eyes; except that the incision continues up to that which appears of the skin.
Then do that in the middle fissure up to the other fissure.
And after an equal quantity of blood runs out, express the places from clots of congealed blood.
Then place in every fissure a linen cloth from old cotton, and place on them cushions soaked in wine, or oil, or vinegar. And see that a hot abscess does not occur.
And on the third day, loosen the bandage, and administer much infusion with lukewarm water. Then treat it with basil ointment, and with the other remedies by which wounds are cured, until it is healed.
Chapter V.
Concerning the treatment of acute tears and catarrhs to the eyes from the interior of the head.
When acute, sharp, persistent catarrhs befall someone, and you see their eyes weakened, small, and their gaze already impaired; and the eyelids are ulcerated, and hairs fall from them, and there is sharp, piercing pain deep within the head, followed by sneezing; then, understand from these symptoms that those matters and catarrhs do not come except from many veins.
And the better cure for them is this. And it is that you should instruct the sick person to shave their forehead. Then make one incision in the middle of the forehead, or a little one across, and begin with the incision from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision go as far as the bone; and draw your hand away from the temporal muscles, which move during chewing, until the bone is exposed; and you shall dry up all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate the two edges of the incision with old cloth, or with linen strips. Then bind them from above with pledgets; and soak the pledgets with wine and oil, so that a hot abscess does not occur.
And when you loosen it, and see that the hot abscess is now lessening, then it is necessary that you rub the bone, until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that causes flesh to grow, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of resin, and prepare a plaster from them, and apply it to the growth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter VI.
Concerning the treatment of that which falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a mineral stone, or something similar to a stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as a chickpea and bean; or it is a fluid substance, such as water, vinegar, and similar things; or some animal.
Therefore, when a stone, or a type of stone from those that do not adhere nor grow in the ear, falls into the ear, expose the ear to the sun.  If you then see the stone, then instill some oil of violets or sesame oil into the ear. Then administer extraction of it, and make them sneeze with incisions, and plug the nostrils at the coming of the sneeze, after you place around the ear scraps of cloth or wool, and extend the ear upward.
For it often goes out with this treatment.
If however it does not go out, then administer extraction of it with gesti, that is, with thin forceps; whose form is this.
If, therefore, it goes out with the forceps, it is good.
And if not, then administer extraction of it with a thin, single-hooked, slightly doubled instrument.
If, therefore, it does not go out by that, then make a tube of bronze, and introduce the end of the tube into the ear's opening far, and fill what is around the tube with softened wax mixed with oil, so that there is no way for wind except through the tube; then extract with your wind by strong suction.
For many times it goes out with what we have described. If, however, it does not go out in this way, then take a small amount of helkalimbat, or of glue, that is, prepared glue, with which birds are caught, and place it at the end of the rod after you have wrapped a suitably positioned piece of cloth around it; then introduce it into the ear canal easily, after you have dried the ear from moisture.
If, therefore, it does not go out with all that we have described, then hasten to make an incision, before a hot abscess, or spasm, occurs.
And the method of the incision is, that a weak phlebotomist first in the cephalic vein: and draw from him from the blood according to the measure of his strength. Then make the sick person sit between your hands, and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of his ear near his fat in the depressed area of it: and let the incision be crescent-shaped, until you reach the stone; then remove it with whatever instruments are possible for you.
Then close the incision promptly and treat it until it is healed.
If the thing falling into the ear is one of the grains that grow and swell, then administer its extraction.
If however it does not yield to you for removal, then take a slender, thin spatula, according to this form.
And administer it with the removal of that kind of grain that falls into the ear, and do not do this when you think that the grain is already moistened by the ear's vapor, until you have put many fragments of it; then extract them with hooks, or with a fine instrument, or with suction, as we have said, for its extraction is thereby relieved.
However, for water that enters the ear, it is necessary for the sick person to use sneezing with condiments at first; and already he has filled his ear with some old cotton; and he lies on that ear in which the water is. If therefore it emerges through that, it is good.
And if not, then take many stones, according to the length of a finger, thin, smooth, and warm them a little with fire, and let the sick person insert one of them into the hole of his ear; and let him jump on one of his feet on that side, and strike with another stone on the stone that is in the ear. Therefore, he should not cease to do this with stone and stone until all the water emerges.
And now the water emerges so that you take one from papyrus, or from rush, and insert one end of it into the ear, and light the other end with fire, until most of it is burned. Then again another rush, and do this many times, until all the water emerges; and remove it with a tube, in accordance with what was previously stated concerning the stone.
In extracting an animal that has entered it, if it is of small body, such as a flea, and similar to it, treat it as I said in the division.
But if it is of large body, which appears to the senses: then administer its extraction with forceps, and hooks: and its removal is easier than all those held in the ear.
However, in extracting a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we said in the division, and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment does not help: then you must look at the ear in the sun. If therefore anything of the worms appears there, then extract it with forceps, or with fine hooks.
If, however, nothing appears there, then take a cannula, which has this form, narrow below, wide above: and insert the thin end into the ear according to the amount that the weak one tolerates it: then suck with it by a strong suction. Do this many times, until all the worms come out.
If, however, they do not obey you for exit, then smear the circumference of the cannula with wax, as I said in the stone.
If they do not come out with what we have said, then administer the distillations which the ancients have already experienced in the killing of worms: and you will find it in the treatise on distillations.
And let your pouring, in which you pour oil and medicines into the ear with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. As you see, make it of silver, or bronze, narrowed below, in which there is a small opening, wide above.
Let the impelling part, which is in the concavity of the cannula, be made of bronze properly. And if you wish, take a rod, and wrap a cloth around its end in a proper wrapping; then pour oil, or juice, or whatever medicines you wish, into the cannula; and let it be in the ear; then insert the rod with the cloth from above, and press it with your hand with moderate pressure, until the oil is driven into the concavity of the hearing, and the weak person feels it internally; and what is poured into the ear should be heated a little with fire; and be careful that the distilled substance is not excessively cold, for medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
Concerning the treatment of sudden ear blockage.
Sometimes infants emerge from their mothers' wombs unperforated.
And it also happens to some people that blockage in the ear canal occurs from a wound, or from flesh growing there.
And this blockage sometimes occurs deep within the ear opening, where vision cannot reach it; and sometimes it occurs near the outer part of the opening, and vision can reach it.
Therefore, that which occurs deep within the ear, and which the eye cannot encompass, is in many cases difficult to cure.
And in that which vision can reach, you should place the patient's ear in the sun, and look into it.  Then, if you see the blockage visible, open it with a thin probe, whose shape is as follows:  Its tip should have a small width, and a pointed edge; and the rest of the probe should be smooth on both sides, so as not to injure the ear.
If therefore the blockage is from flesh growing in the ear canal, then grasp it with a fine hook, and cut it away with a forceful yet gentle hand, until all the flesh is removed.
If the blockage is deep within the ear, then take a thin, smooth rod and warm it slightly with fire; then insert it into the ear canal.
If, therefore, you sense a blockage with the patient's complaint of difficulty hearing, attempt to open it gently, and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
Then place a bandage on the ear, fitting the size of the opening that you prepared, and wrap it with Egyptian ointment.  Do this for several days, until you are certain of the wound's healing.
Or take a bandage, and soak it in rosewater, and sprinkle crushed zegi powder on it, and use it.
If you see that the ear has already become abscessed, then it is necessary to remove the bandage, and replace it with another bandage, which you will wrap in a cloth made with rose oil, until the hot abscess subsides; then treat it until it heals.
If, however, bleeding occurs, then immerse a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear.  And use the remaining treatment, which stops bleeding, as described in the tract on distillations.
On the treatment of the eyelid's ulcer.
Chapter VIII.
These ulcers that occur on the eyelids of the eye are sometimes moist and sometimes dry.
Therefore, you should grasp them with forceps or a hook, and cut them away with a scalpel from their roots. Then apply pounded zegi to the area.
If blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a fine, lenticular cautery.
And to cauterize them is better after the incision. For they often return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned, and they do not return.
Concerning the treatment of granulations of the eyelids of the eye.
Chapter IX.
Often something happens in the eyelids that is similar to a granulation in its strength and hardness, and because of that it is called a granulation: and it is a collection of thick moisture on the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
Therefore, the procedure in them is, that you should consider then, if it is a granulation proceeding in that which appears on the eyelid, and it moves to every part, and its removal is easy.
Therefore make a fissure over it, in width. Then scrape it from every part with ease, until it disappears. Then suspend it with a hook and cut it off.
However, if its excision is not prepared for you unless after you have pierced the eyelid with a small incision, then that harms the patient in some way.
If the fissure is large, then collect it with a suture and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For ointment restores it and makes it flesh.
And if the swelling is sloping inward toward the inner surface of the ear, then invert the eyelid, and suspend the swelling with a hook; besides which you need an incision, and press it from every side.
If the eyelid is extended by incision, that will not harm it in any way.
Then wash the eye after the incision of the swelling, with salt water, and treat the place with that which makes it flesh, until the sick person is healed.
Concerning the treatment of the swelling of the eyelids.
Chapter X.
Axirnath is a fat that is produced in the tissues of the upper eyelid; it most often occurs in children.  It burdens their eyes, and they suffer from morning catarrh, and cannot look at the sunlight, because their tears rush to them, and therefore you see them always lying on their faces, and sleeping on them.  Their eyelids, covered by the swelling, are damp under the eyebrows.  The prominence of the Axirnath is visible to the eye, and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the Axirnath between them.
And the method of its operation is that the sick person should place their head in your lap.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare strips on it, and make a circle from that strip according to the amount that Axirnath encompasses on all sides. Then place it over it, and press with your fingers on all sides, so that the Axirnath is gathered in the middle of the circle.
Then find the middle of that moisture with a lancet, making incisions across the width, and let the incision not be larger than that made in phlebotomy in the depth.
Or it is necessary that you cut through the entire skin, until you reach the Axirnath, for in many ways the Axirnath proceeds from the incision in the form of a white fatty fragment.
Therefore, extract it with the cloth, which you have inserted between your fingers on the outside; and you turn your hand to the right and to the left, until it is dispersed; and be careful not to press into the incision, so that you do not reach the eye with the lancet and injure it.
If, however, the Axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the incision, you should gently insert a little more into the incision until the Axirnath proceeds. Then extract it, as we have said.
Then immerse cloths in vinegar and water, and place them on the area, and bind it with bandages.
And there are men who grind salt and place it in the concavity of the incision, so that the remaining moisture from it liquefies.
Then treat it until it heals.
If however a hot abscess occurs in the place, then treat it with soothing plasters until it heals.
Concerning the methods of Ataxmir of the eye, that is, when the eyelid of the eye softens, so that it wrinkles improperly.
Chapter XI.
When additional hairs grow on the eyelids beyond the natural hairs, and become numerous, then they harm the eye and cause various ailments, such as constant tears, softening of the eyelids, and cloudiness, until it becomes the cause of the eye's destruction.
And Ataxmir of the eye is indeed treated according to four methods. Or by cautery with fire. Or with a sharp medicine, according to what was previously stated in the chapter on cautery. Or Ataxmir is treated by incision and suture. Or by canes, according to what I will say.
You should place the patient's head in your lap. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If therefore it turns, it is good. And if not: insert a needle into it, in which there is thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and cause the needle with the thread to penetrate from above, and let it be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the edge of the eyelid where the hairs grow; and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it with a radius.
Then find in the inner part of the eyelid beneath the hairs, with a spatula sliding from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct.
Then pull out the thread, and place a small cushion of cotton, or cloth, under the eyelid; then mark the eyelid with ink, which should be in the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that the shape be according to the size and elevation of the eyelid, since it varies in humans.
For among them are those who need a large amount to be cut from the eyelid, according to the amount that softens from the eyelid.
Then, with a spatula, find along the two lines you marked, and begin from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct, and let the incision be close to the natural hairs, as thick as a ray.
Then introduce the hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then scrape the whole of it. Then bring together the two lips with a needle and fine wool thread, and wipe away the blood, and cause that which is superfluous of the threads to adhere to the two eyebrows, with certain invisible things, if you wish to do it; and if not, do not worry.
Then leave the sutures and threads for up to three or four days; afterwards, treat it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without sutures, and treat it with that which dries and constricts it. For the eyelid is raised at the sealing of the wound and its bringing together; but it will be better.
This method of treatment the ancients taught Ataxmir.
However, there is care for the sick person in it, and it is from good treatment, and there is no fear in it.
And another method is in Ataxmir also: and it is, that you mark on the eyelid a shape, which is like the shape of a myrtle leaf, as we have narrated.
Then raise the eyelid with three separate hooks, or joined together, according to this following form, then cut what is superfluous with small scissors, according to this form, with equal cutting.
If however it is not possible for you to hold the hook, and it is not equal: then take a needle in which there is a thread, and insert it in the middle of the figure; and insert another thread near the larger tear duct, and a second thread near the smaller tear duct; and join the threads between your fingers with equality.
Then raise your hand with equal lifting; and lift the skin over which the mark is made, as we have narrated.
Then join the two lips of the wound with sutures, and care for it until it heals.
If however a boil occurs at your incision, or your fissure: subdue that boil with cauterization, and similar things, from soothing ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid also, that its hairs are turned.
Therefore, you should use what we said about the incision; but sutures are more artificial for both. Concerning the treatment of Ataxmir with canes.
And it is that you turn the eyelid, and cut the fissure which is within, according to what I have described. Then make two canes or two thin pieces of wood, whose length be according to the length of the eyelid, and their width be less than the width of a spatula; and you have already incised at the extremities of each from both sides where you hold the threads.
Then, carefully place each one upon the excess from the eyelid, and tightly bind the two canes from both sides with a strong ligature, and leave it for some days. For the tightened skin will die, and turn black, and putrefy, until it falls away of itself.
If, therefore, it is delayed, then cut it with scissors. Then treat it until it heals.
When it therefore consolidates, the eyelid is raised: and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of two canes, which you shall make according to this figure, and quantity in length and width.
Chapter XII.
Concerning the raising of pricking hairs in the eye with a needle.
The operation in it is, that you take a fine needle, and introduce into it a fine, smooth silk thread. Then tie its ends, and make a very small knot on each end: and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then place another fine thread beneath it in the Alinxotati, and tie its ends if you wish.
Then let the sick person place their head in your bosom, and be near the sun so that the operation is revealed: because it is a subtle or minute operation.
Then introduce the needle with the alinxotati into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly, until you pull it out above the natural hair.
Then pull the hair up above the alinxotati: and insert that hair into the alinxotati: if it is one, or two, or three, not more.
Then pull your hand with it, until the hair comes out with the alinxotati into the eyelid with the natural hair.
However, if the alinxotati is pulled, and the hair does not come out with it: you will pull the alinxotati downwards, with the thread that you have placed in it, until the alinxotati emerges from the upper opening, and you see it with the eye's vision: then you will pull the alinxotati and the thread; and remove both, and close the eye; and leave it closed for two days, or three, until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, then either associate a long hair from the natural hairs with it, and lift it with it; or leave it until it lengthens after a period, and then lift it.
Chapter XIII.
When this Alxatrati happens to the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this Alxatrati is either natural or accidental.
Accidental, therefore, it arises from the consolidation of a wound, or incision, or cautery, and things similar to them.
And the method of operation in it is that you find that consolidation, and separate its lips, and place in that which is between them a linen pad, and tie it, until it is healed.
And it is not necessary that you use in its treatment things which dry and constrict, for if you do that, the Alxatrati returns worse than it was: indeed, use in it things which soften, such as fenugreek, and embrocate with water in which cooked althea, linseed, and the softened dyaquilon ointment have been mixed with some oil, and wrap with it a linen pad, and treat with it.
And the treasure of your treatment is to try by all means that it does not consolidate according to the form it first had.
And this operation which we have described is indeed only a certain rectification of Alxatrati, so that the form of the place does not return to what it was entirely.
Concerning the treatment of Alxatrati which occurs in the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkling or contraction upward to the right and left.
Chapter XIV.
This Alxatrati which occurs lower down is truly called Alxatrati. And it is natural, and it is accidental.
And the accidental kind results from a wound, or fissure, or cauterization, and similar things.
And the method of treatment in it is, that you take a needle, on which there is a double thread, and you dip it into the flesh, and make it penetrate from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread reaches the two extremities of the flesh.
Then stretch the flesh upwards with the needle, and cut it with a wide spatula. If therefore the shape of the eyelid returns as it should, it is good.
And if not, take a radium, and place it in the fissure, and turn the eyelid with it: and make two fissures in the inner side of the eyelid, and let the extremities of the two fissures be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and form an angle from them: until when they are gathered, the shape of them becomes this shape, and it is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh by the amount that is its sharp lower side, from what follows the eyelid. Then bring together the separated parts; sew it with wool thread.
Then treat them with what we said, of softening medicines, and with poultices, until they are healed.
And if a laceration occurs from the fissure, and suture, or cautery: then it is necessary that you make a flat fissure beneath the hairs of the eyelids, even according to what preceded. Then separate between the two lips with a poultice, according to what we said.
And the summary of treatment for Alxatrati, when it is above or below, is that the operation should proceed according to the form of the Alxatrati.
For it is indeed of many varieties in form; and a prudent craftsman prepares the instrument in whatever way is possible for him, until he restores the form to the natural one, or nearly so, and the afflicted person himself, according to what suits him, from the operation and instruments in various positions.
Chapter XV.
Concerning the adherence of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
Sometimes this adhesion occurs in many people, so that the upper eyelid becomes attached to the white of the eye and prevents it from its natural functions.
Therefore, it is necessary that you introduce the tip of the probe under the eyelid and lift it up; or extend it with a hook; then cut the adhesion with a fine spatula, which should not be sharp, but slightly blunt; so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula is removed, and does not injure the eye with its sharpness; and your incision should be such that you scrape off that adhesion easily, until the eyelid returns to its natural form, and the entire adhesion is healed.
Then pour salt water into the eye, washing it with water; or distill upon it from a still, from dissolved zinc with water.
Then separate the eyelid and the eye with flax thread; and place on the eye wool soaked with egg white; and after three days use the still and solidifying agents, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the nail and the prominence of the tear flesh.
Chapter XVI.
Know that the nail is according to two ways. Or it is nervous; and it is similar to hard, fine sifac. Or it is not nervous, similar to frozen white moisture; which when it touches iron, or you attempt to grasp it with a hook, is severed, and the hook does not fix itself in it.
And both types do not begin, unless from the larger tear duct, until it gradually progresses, until it covers the sight, and prevents light, and the movement of the eye.
And the method of operation in its incision is: that the infirm person should place their head in your lap. Then open their eyes, and elevate the eyelid with your hand. Then collect the nail with a hook of few doublings, and extend it upwards. Then take a needle, and insert into it a hair from a horse's hair, or an ox's, or a cow's: and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle a little, and fix it in the middle of the nail: and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with the thread, and extend it upwards: and scrape with the hair the side of the nail that follows the pupil, as if you saw it with the hair until its last part.
Then cut the remaining part at the root of the tear duct with a fine spatula, or with small scissors: and leave the natural tear-duct flesh, so that a constant flow of tears does not occur from its incision.
And sometimes the nail is extended with a hook alone, or with a thread, as we have said. Then it is scraped with a gentle spatula, whose form is this.
And beware, lest the spatula touch the horny membrane, and a rupture occur in it, and the pupil be pierced.
Then after the incision, put some crushed salt, or a concoction made from ziniar, into the eye, and leave it until the next day. Then treat it with whatever is necessary until it is healed.
If the nail is not nervous, and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor a hook fixed to it, then this type of nail is treated many times with medicines and heals as I described in the division.
If you wish to cut it, then open the patient's eye and take a thin, gentle, weakly sharpened spatula, according to this form. And scrape the nail from above with care. If you see that it becomes thin and breaks, and the scraping is imprinted on it, then drop from your hour glass into the eye a concoction made of ziniar, or a red sieve, or crushed salt; and cover the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the operation, until the entire thing is removed. Unless there is a hot abscess in the eye; for then leave it and treat the abscess until it heals.
Then repeat the operation on it with the scraping, until it heals.
However, if the prominence of the tear duct is harmful to the eye with great damage, it should be suspended with a hook, and a part of it should be cut out; and do not make the incision too deep, so as not to interrupt the flow of tears.
Then drop into the tear duct a red liquid, or one made from ziniari, until it heals.
Concerning the incision of Alguardenic, and the flesh that grows in the eye.
Chapter XVII.
Sometimes in some people's eyes, red flesh grows, either mixed in or piled up, until it covers the pupil, or approaches it, or presses against the eyelids, and perhaps turns the eyelids outward: and it resembles a rose hip.
And the operation in it also is that the infirm person should place his head in your bosom. Then open his eyes, and gather all that flesh with appropriate hooks: and hold it with one forceps, or tweezers.
Then cut into the flesh at first, until all of it is finished with the scalpel. And beware of the eye, so that you do not injure it in the operation: and let your incision, with which you cut it, be either with a spatula, which we have described in the incision of the hoof, or with small shears, with which the asel is gathered, as will appear from its form after this chapter.
When therefore your incision is complete, and the entire alguardenici is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt: or distill into it red powder, and similar corrosives from medicines: and place upon the eye externally a bandage with the white of an egg: so that it is secured from a hot abscess.
If any alguardenici remains, and hot blood dominates you, and you fear a hot abscess: then leave the eye, and treat it with that which quiets the abscess.
Then repeat upon it the operation, until it is healed.
And similarly do with the added flesh that occurs in the eye of this kind.
However, it is necessary that you avoid the treatment of every weak eye by a natural or accidental path, operating with iron; and do not put your hand in any of these operations until you see that the member is tolerant of that operation.
Concerning the collection of Sebel from the eye.
Chapter XVIII.
Sebel are red veins that are woven over the eye, and hinder the eye's operation of sight; and they weaken the eye with the length of days.
Therefore, it is necessary for you first to consider if the eye in which Sebel is present is strong, and there is no illness in it besides Sebel; then collect its Sebel.
And it is that you should instruct the sick person to place their head in your lap.
Then suspend those veins with a single hook, or two, according to your judgment; and let the hook be a fine double hook, according to this shape, or let there be two hooks graded on a single body, according to this shape.
Then collect those veins with fine scissors delicately, and wipe away the blood hour by hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye and resolved; and beware of injuring the eye with the extremities of the forceps.
And let your operation be in the middle of the day before the sun, and be very firm in your operation, so that you do not cut anything but those veins.
And when you have finished, then distil into the eye either green or red dye, so that its sharpness corrodes what remains of the sebum.
However, if it is not possible for you to collect it all in that hour, then plaster the eye with what quiets the pain of the eye, and leave it for several days until its pain subsides and is safe from a hot abscess; then repeat it again according to the same method until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps.
Concerning the reduction of the Arissati to the nose.
Chapter XIX.
Physicians call Arissati a fistula, which you treat with a cautery, or with a burning sharp medicine, according to its preceding narrative.
And if it does not heal, then there is no skill in it: unless you should open up the abscess near its origin, and extract all the moisture and pus from it, until the opening is exposed.
When therefore the opening is exposed, and you see corruption or blackness in it, then scrape it with an instrument, whose shape is this.
And it is called Asperi's head.
Let it be made of tempered iron, and let its head be round like a small shield,  pointed with the file's point, or with a fine point of an aliskilfer, and place it upon the corrupted bone area.
Then turn it between your fingers, and you press your hand a little while, until you know that the corruption has been scraped away: and do that several times.
Then restore the place with drying, astringent medicines.
If therefore the place consolidates, and flesh grows in it, and the flow of pus is cut off: and it remains for forty days, and is not renewed, and does not become abscessed, and no accident occurs in it: then you shall know that it is now healed.
And if not, then there is no remedy for it, except to redirect the fistula into the nostril opening, according to this method.
And it is, that you uncover the second bone with iron, or with a sharp medicine: and when the bone is uncovered, then take a drill according to this form, let its iron end be triangular: and its wood be a pyramid tapered down to the end, as you see.
Then place it on the bone itself: and let it be near the tear duct: and move your hand away from the eye a little. Then revolve your hand with the drill until it penetrates the bone, and the sick person feels it: through this, that they find air escaping from it when they hold their hand over their nose.
Therefore, then restore the place with astringent, drying medicines, as I taught you.
When therefore flesh arises, and the place hardens, then the material that was escaping outward returns to the nose: therefore it is lighter for the sick person.
Concerning the reduction of the prominence of the Eye.
Chapter XX.
When the eye protrudes entirely, and no harm or diminution occurs in vision, then you should release the patient, then bleed him, afterwards apply cupping to his neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then apply a liniment to the eye made from acacia, aloe, olibanum, and sarcocolla. Then bind the eye from above over a plaster with many bandages.
And the manner of the bandages is: that you take soft cloths, many, and each cloth is doubled over four times; make many of them; then place them one over another, according to the amount needed for the eye; then bind over them with a strong bandage.
And again apply cupping with suction without scarification.
Then remove the cupping, and leave the eye bound day and night.
However, if the bandage softens again, then it is necessary that it be bound tightly; then remove the bandage after a day and night, as we said.
If therefore you see the eye has now returned to the uvea tunic, it is good; and if not, then again apply the plaster, and bandages, and binding, and cupping, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the uvea tunic.
Chapter XXI.
When a rupture occurs in the uvea tunic, then it protrudes and proceeds outside the eyelids like a grain of the uvea, and it disfigures the appearance of the man because of it, and one wants to cut it: then the operation on it is as I describe: and it is that you insert a needle into the root of the uvea from the lower to the upper part.
Then insert another needle in which there is a doubled thread from the tear duct side, and make it penetrate; and leave the first needle according to its position.
Then cut the place of the double thread, and tie with it the part of the uvea around the needle; and tighten it greatly.
Then pull out the needle, and place on the eye wool soaked in the white of an egg; and leave it until the thread with the uvea falls off.
Then treat the eye with what strengthens it until the wound is healed.
And sometimes the uvea is tightened from all sides with a circle of cloth, until the eye gains great prominence.
Then it is pierced with a thin spatula, which reaches the depth of the eye, and the albuminous moisture runs, and the eye descends, and immediately enters; then tighten it until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of Alcomnat.
Chapter XXII.
This ailment, called Alcomnati, is nothing but a discharge that gathers in the eye, similar to descending water, and is not the eye itself.
And the method of operation in it is that you make the weak person sit on a prepared seat, or upright.
Then take his head in your hands from both sides, and move it, until you see the discharge descend to the lower parts of his eyes. Then it is fixed, and does not descend, and the light is released: and the sick person sees things as he saw them before: and if it did not descend lower, know that it is water.
If however the descent of the discharge is not prepared with what we have said, then make the sick person sit between your hands. Then take a thin spatula, and split with it slightly above the cornea, near the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea at the limbus, until the discharge comes out.
When it comes out, then instill into the eye warm water in which you mixed honey, or water in which you have boiled fenugreek and honey; then treat with the rest of the treatment, until he is healed.
Concerning the treatment of water descending in the eye, or cataract.
Chapter XXIII.
We have already spoken of the kinds of water in the division, and what is suitable for piercing, the speech declaring and explaining it; then take the same according to health.
Then, therefore, it is necessary that you make the infirm person sit between your hands, square, opposite the light, near the sun; and bind his healthy eye, and tightly restrain it; then with your right hand, if the eye in which there is water is the left eye, or with your left hand, if it is the right eye, press down the eyelid of his eye.
Then take with your right hand an almond, if the eye is the left eye; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right eye.
Then place the end of the almond near the crown, by the thickness of a ray, upon the white of the eye, where the smaller tear duct appears; then press the almond with force, and you revolve your hand with it, until it penetrates into the white of the eye; and you sense that the almond has now reached the empty space.
And it is necessary that the measure of the almond's journey to the depth be the measure of the space that is from the pupil to the end of the blackness; and it is the crown of the eye. For you will see it in that very pupil of the eye's vision, because of the clarity of the cornea.
Then place the almond up to the place where the water is. Then press it downwards, one time after another.
If therefore the water descends immediately, then the sick person sees that upon which he opens his sight immediately, and the almond in his eye.
Then rest a little. If therefore the water descends, then remove it a second time, apart from letting the almond emerge.
When therefore it is fixed, and does not ascend, then extract the almond with ease, and you revolve your hand gradually.
Then dissolve some clear gem salt in water, and wash the inside of the eye with it. Then place a whole piece of lint, or wool soaked in rose oil and egg white, over the eye, and bind the healthy eye with it.
We, and those of our time, place crushed cumin with egg white on it.
If the almond does not yield to you for entry into the eye because of its hardness, since it is from people whose eye is very hard, then it is necessary that you take a spatula, which is called Alberid, whose shape is this.
Therefore, gently scrape with it only the conjunctiva, and do not put your hand in the opening: and it is only to make a subtle way for the almond's place.
Then introduce the almond, as we have said.
And at the completion of your work, let a suitable bedchamber be prepared for the sick person, in which they sleep on their back in a dark room; and let them be forbidden all movements, and coughing; and place their food such that it soothes their nature; and let them not move their head to the right, nor to the left at all.
And let the bandage remain according to its position until the third day.
Then remove it in that dark room, and test his eyesight; and make him see things. Then reduce the bandage until the seventh day.
And it should not be done at the time of treatment, or immediately after the incision; rather, it should be delayed; because water ascends quickly through intense exertion.
However, if a hot abscess occurs, then it is necessary to remove the eye before the seventh day, and treat it with that which quiets the abscess, until it is calmed; then, restore its vision and place over its face an oral thing by which its vision is regulated for a few days, while it is in that dark room.
Then let him leave the house gradually and return to his own homes.
And know that in the zaltea a disciple is not excused from a vision sometimes; then let him dare to operate.
And now indeed one of those from Alayrach has come to us, who said that in Alayrach a perforated magdan was made, through which water is drawn.
But truly I have not seen anyone in your land who has done that, nor have I read it in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of species from the magdan, so that he who is ignorant of them may stand upon it.
Let them be made properly of air; and let their extremities be triangular with this same subtlety, and the extremity sharp.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
Concerning the treatment of flesh grown in the nose.
Sometimes various flesh growths arise in the nose, some resembling a scorpion with many legs, and some cancerous, stony, and dark-colored flesh; and some that is soft, not dark-colored flesh.
Therefore, in the case of the soft, non-deceitful, non-cancerous flesh growths, you should have the patient sit between your hands, facing the ground, and open their nostrils. Insert a hook into the flesh growths; then draw them outward, and cut what you've grasped with a sharp, fine scalpel on one side, until you know the entire flesh growth has been removed.
If any part of it remains, which cannot be removed, then scrape it away with a fine instrument with ease, until no part of it remains. If, however, blood dominates it, or a hot abscess occurs, then apply what is appropriate. Or, if it is from malignant abscesses, then hasten to cauterize it, until the blood is separated and all flesh is removed.
Then, after the incision, vinegar and water, or wine, should be placed in the nose. If, however, the nose opens and moisture flows from it to the palate, then know that it is now healthy.
If, however, the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then know that flesh has grown within the upper nasal bones, to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
Therefore, it is necessary to insert a linen thread of some thickness, and tie many knots in it; and place a finger's width, or less, between each knot.  And let the sick person, with a rod or whatever is possible for him, push one end of the thread into the nose after he has made it like a probe.  And let him pull it until it reaches the abscess and comes out above his throat.
And often children, like Attomi, do a similar thing in schools; and it is an easy task for whoever wants to.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end that emerges from the nose, and the other that remains in the nose. Then cut away the flesh with the knots that are on the thread. Do that until you know that the flesh is now with the knots of the thread.
Then withdraw the thread; and after cleaning the blood, place in the nose a bandage that you have soaked in Egyptian ointment: do this for three days, or more, until the ointment has corroded all that remains of the flesh.
Then place a lead cannula at the end of the thing in the nose for a few days, until it heals.
If, however, it needs a treatment that will dry it out, use that.
To make a nasal douche, with which oils and other medicines are instilled into the nose, make it of silver, or bronze: it should be like a small lamp, open like a shell; and its passage should be similarly shaped.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula, like a reed.
And the shell-like instrument for nasal douching should be full, uncovered; and there should be a handle at its rear end, with which you hold it when you heat oil, or whatever you wish from extracts and liquid substances, in it.
Chapter XXV.
Often a wart arises at the tip of the nose, and it grows larger and larger with the days, until its appearance becomes unsightly.
And therefore it is necessary, that in the beginning of its appearance you should cauterize it and eradicate the whole of it. Then place upon the site either a cautery or a burning medicine which acts in its place.
If therefore its incision is delayed until it grows large, then consider, if it is stony, hard, of a dark colour, with little sensation: then do not touch it with iron, because it is a cancerous abscess.
For I have often seen that he who incised a man's abscess, and great harm resulted from it to the one who had it.
And if the abscess is soft to the touch, not dark in colour, and you see that incision is possible throughout it, then evacuate the pus, and incise it without fear, without hesitation: and treat the site with what dries and constricts it, until it is healed.
Chapter XXVI.
Concerning the suture of the nose, lip, and ear.
Know that when separation of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, little benefit comes from treatment, except in some individuals.
Therefore, when something happens to one of those [parts], you must consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood; and then bring together the edges of the wound with a suture; afterwards, treat it until it is healed.
And if the lips of the separation of continuity are already separated, and each part is healthy: then it is necessary to scrape away every part from its visible skin until it bleeds. Then bring together the two lips with a suture, and tighten them, and powder over it the blood of the dragon, and crushed olibanum: and place above the powders a piece of Diafinicon plaster, and some other of the consolidating plasters; and leave it bound for two or three days. Then loosen it, and change the medicine; and leave it until the threads are cut off by themselves. Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
The method of the suture is, that you bring together the separation of continuity, either with needles, as we have narrated in the suture of the belly; or bring together the separation with thread, as I have made you know.
Chapter XXVII.
Concerning the extraction of knots of the lips.
Many men indeed often suffer abscesses hard like grains of grass on the interior of their lips: and some of them are smaller, and some larger.
Therefore, it is necessary that you turn the lip and cut open over each knot of it and suspend it with a hook and cut it off from every part.
Then fill the place after the incision with pounded zegi until the blood is cut off.
Then it should be washed with vinegar, and the place where there is astringency should be treated with it, until the wounds are healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
Concerning the incision of flesh growing on the gum.
Flesh often grows over the gum, which the ancients call a growth.
Therefore, you should suspend it with a hook, or hold it with forceps, and cut it at its root, and allow the pus or blood to flow.
Then place upon the area ground zegi, or other astringent, drying powders.
If that flesh returns after treatment, for it often does, then cut it a second time, and cauterize it: for it does not return after cauterization.
Chapter XXIX.
Concerning the extraction of teeth with an iron instrument.
Sometimes rough, unsightly, and sometimes blackened, yellowish, or greenish, hard crusts collect on the surfaces of the teeth, both inner and outer, and between the gums, until corruption spreads from it to the gums, and the teeth become unsightly due to it.
Therefore, you should have the weak person sit between your hands, and his head in your bosom, and scrape the molar and tooth, where crusts and a substance like sand appear, until nothing of them remains, and similarly do with blackness, greenness, yellowness, and other discolorations until they are cleansed.
If therefore that which is on them from the initial scraping is removed, it is good; and if not, then repeat the scraping on them another, second, and third day, until at last the desired effect is achieved.
And know that a tooth requires scrapers of various shapes and forms, according to what is prepared for your operation; therefore, the scraper used to scrape the tooth from the lower part is different from the scraper used to scrape between the teeth, according to different shapes.
And these shapes of scrapers should be prepared by you, as you see them.
Chapter 30.
Of the extraction of toothache.
You should treat the tooth from its pain with all your skill, and delay in its eradication. For there is nothing to replace its function when it is eradicated, since it is a noble substance; until, when there is no excuse for its eradication, then it is necessary when the afflicted one is compelled to eradicate it, that it be strengthened and be certified to you that the aching tooth is [now] firm.
For often the pain deceives the afflicted one, and he thinks that it is in the tooth's saha and eradicates it; afterward, the pain does not recede until the afflicted one's tooth is eradicated.
For we have already seen that from the operation of bloodletters.
Therefore, when the aching tooth itself is certified to you, then it is necessary that you scarify around the tooth with a spatumile, in which there is something of virtue, until the gum is loosened on all sides; then move it with your fingers, or with small forceps at first gradually, until you break it.
Then place large forceps in a good position on it, and place the patient's head between your knees; which you have now held firmly, so that it does not move; then pull the tooth in a straight line, so as not to break it.
If therefore it does not emerge, then take one of those instruments, and introduce it under it from every side with ease, and attempt its movement, as you did at first.
If however the tooth is perforated or corroded, then it is necessary that you fill that hole with cloth; and bind it with the end of a thin reed, so that it does not break at the time of your constriction upon it with forceps.
And it is necessary that you perform the scarification around the gum on all sides very carefully.
And maintain your strength so as not to break it, and lest a part of it remain, and return upon the sick person from that malice which be greater pain to him at first.
And beware, lest you do that which ignorant phlebotomists do in their haste and boldness in eradicating it, beyond what we have described.
For many times they cause great infirmities upon men, which are more difficult to break, and the roots of it all remain, or some of them; or to extract it with some of the jawbones, as we have witnessed many times.
Then it should be rinsed after its eradication with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
If a flow of blood occurs from the places (for it often happens), then take some of the zegi and fill the place with it; and if not, cauterize it if the zegi does not help you.
The shapes of the fine forceps with which the tooth is moved at first should be of long extremities, short handle, thick, so that they do not double when pressed upon the tooth; they should be like you see thick handles, until when you grip with them, they do not give way, nor double; of short extremities, and be of good iron, or of well-made steel, with straight extremities; and on their extremities there be teeth, some of which enter others, and grip with a proper, strong constriction.
And sometimes the extremities are shaped like the beak of a bird called a stork, and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter XXXI.
Concerning the eradication of teeth, and the extraction of fractured mandibular bones.
When, during the eradication of a tooth, something remains, then it is necessary to apply a poultice of butter to the area for one or two days, until the area softens. Then introduce the algasti, or forceps, whose extremities are shaped like the beak of a bird called a stork. However, this is the shape of the forceps.
Let their extremities be made like a file within, or like an aliskilfegi.
If therefore it does not yield to you for extraction with these forceps, then it is necessary to cut around the root, and uncover all the flesh, and introduce the instrument called a small lever, whose form is this.
Let it be short in length, somewhat thick, and not be soaked, so it does not break.
If therefore the root emerges through it, it is good; and if not, it is necessary to assist with these other instruments whose shapes are these.
The shape of the first is triangular with a certain thickness at the end.
The shape is triangular with a slender end.
And with other instruments and tools that preceded in the discussion of teeth.
There is also assistance with this instrument, resembling a large hook, of this shape, a twisted triangular end, where there is some thickness so that it does not break, and let it not be soaked.
And know that there are many instruments for teeth; and similarly, other instruments are not counted, and a skillful craftsman, subtle in his art, finds for himself the instrument according to the operation and illnesses themselves that they signify.
Because there are illnesses for which the ancients did not say an instrument, due to the diversity of their types.
If, however, a bone breaks from the jaw or from other bones of the mouth, or it rots, then probe above it in its place with that which suits it from the instruments and forceps which I have mentioned in the extraction of roots, and aid with a forceps, whose shape is this.
Let there be some gradual thickness in it, so that the bone is held, or it is held with it, and does not escape and depart, until the bone emerges; and restore the place with suitable medicaments for it.
If however there is small rot in the bone, then scrape it from its rot, and care for it until it is healed.
Chapter XXXII.
Of the sawing of prominent teeth above others.
When teeth protrude not according to their natural course, an ugly form is made by it; and especially when this happens in women and girls, namely, those of a venal nature.
Therefore, it is necessary that you consider. For if a tooth is born behind another tooth, and is not positioned properly, or its sawing or filing is not possible, then remove it; and if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it off with this instrument, whose shape is this.
It is similar to a small beak, and is made of tempered iron, with a very sharp tip.
And let your incision be such that you cut it over many days, and due to the hardness of the tooth, and so that another tooth is not moved.
If however, it is prominent, exposed to its filing: then file it with a file of tempered steel, whose shape is this.
Let the whole be of tempered steel, and its handle of very small points, like the file with which needles are made: with which the tooth is filed gradually over many days with ease, so that the tooth does not move and fall out; then, gently, at the end, scrape it with some razors.
If it is a tooth from which a part breaks off and hurts the tongue: then it is necessary to file it also, until the roughness of that fracture is removed, and it is made even: and flattened, and does not hurt the tongue, nor spoil the speech.
Chapter XXXIII.
Concerning the retention of teeth with gold wires.
When the front teeth suffer concussion and displacement from a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite down on food lest it fall out: and you have treated them with astringent remedies, and the treatment has not succeeded: then the method is to bind them with gold wire; for silver becomes green and putrefies after a few days, but gold never changes.
And let the wire be of a medium fineness and thickness according to the size that it will take between the teeth.
And the method of retention is to take the wire, and to pass a double length of it between two healthy teeth; then to weave the two ends of the wire between the displaced teeth, whether one or more, until you reach with the weave to a healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving until the point where you began; and tie your hand with delicacy and skill until it is completely immovable; and let your tightening of the wire be at the roots of the teeth, so that it does not slip out.
Then cut off the excess ends of the wire with scissors; and bring both together and turn them with forceps; and hide them between the healthy tooth and the displaced tooth, so as not to injure the tongue; then let it remain tightly bound.
If however it comes loose or is cut, tie them with another wire, and it will stay with that wire for all the time.
And this is the method for teeth, and the method of retention of two healthy teeth and two displaced teeth, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that one or two teeth return after falling out to their places, and are retained as we have described, and remain; and no one can do this unless they are an artful, prudent, and experienced craftsman.
And sometimes a piece of bone from the gums is fitted, and it is made into the shape of a tooth, and placed in its place, from which the tooth is strengthened and tied, as we said; and it remains and serves with it for a long time.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
Sometimes this ligament that occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, from a wound that has already healed.
And the operation on it is, that you open the mouth of the infirm person on his head in your lap, and raise his tongue; then cut that nervous ligament in width, until the tongue is released from its constriction.
If there is something of hardness and knotting in it, and it is from the consolidation of a wound: then insert a hook into it and split it in width, until you cut the ligament, and the knotting is released.
And beware, lest there be a fissure in the depth of the flesh, and you cut an artery there, and a flow of blood result.
Then it should be washed with rosewater, or vinegar, or cold water, at the site of the incision.
Then place a linen bandage under the tongue, which the infirm person should hold all night: so that it will not consolidate a second time.
If, however, a flow of blood occurs, then place pounded zegi over the area. If, however, blood overcomes you, then cauterize the area with a lentiginous cautery, which is appropriate for it; then treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter XXXV.
Sometimes a boil occurs beneath the tongue, resembling a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural function, and perhaps enlarging so much that it fills the mouth.
And the operation in it is that you open the sick person's mouth in the presence of the sun, and consider the boil.
If therefore you see it of a dusky color, or black, hard, and the sick person feels no sensation in it, then do not touch it, for it is cancer.
And if it is inclined towards whiteness, in which there is moisture, then insert a hook into it, and cut it with a fine scalpel, and free it from all sides. If blood overcomes you during your operation, then place on it pounded zegi until the blood is stemmed; then return to your operation until you extract it with its entirety.
Then wash it with vinegar and salt, and treat it with the appropriate subsequent care until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of an abscess of the two tonsils.
Chapter XXXVI.
Sometimes there occur between the throat, the glands, which are similar to algodad, which occur from the outside; and they are called two tonsils.  When you treat them with what we have stated in the division, and they do not heal, then consider.
If therefore it is a hard abscess, of a dark color, with little sensitivity, then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of a reddish color, and its root is thick, then do not touch it even with iron, fearing blood flow, rather leave it until it ripens. And then either you will pierce it, or it will burst on its own.
And if it is white in color, round, and its root is thin, then it is necessary to cut it.
And the operation on it is that you should consider before its operation, if its abscess has subsided, its heat having been completely calmed, or if it is diminishing with a certain reduction: then, therefore, make the infirm person sit in the sun, and let his head be in your lap: and open his mouth, and let the servant take between his hands, and press his tongue downwards with an instrument, of which this is the form.
Let it be made of silver, or bronze, which is as thin as a knife.
When, therefore, you press with it the tongue, and the abscess is revealed to you, and your gaze falls upon it, then take the hook and fix it in the tonsil, and pull it outwards as far as you can, except that you pull out with it something from the coverings.
Then cut it with an instrument, whose form follows this. It resembles shears; however, its extremities should be curved, so that the opening of each of them is in a straight line with the other's point: hence, take care that it is made of tempered iron, or soaked in alfuled.
If, however, this instrument is not present, then cut it with a spatula, whose form is this. One side should be sharp, the other side not sharp.
And after you cut one tonsil, cut the other according to this same type of incision.
Then, after the incision, let the sick person gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
If, however, a flow of blood occurs, let him gargle with water in which the pomegranate rinds have been boiled; or with myrtle leaves, and similar astringents, until the flow of blood is stopped; then treat him until he is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses arise in the throat, besides the two tonsils. Therefore, cut it according to what I said about the incision of the two tonsils equally.
And I myself have already treated a woman from an abscess that had arisen between her throat, which was drawing to a foul condition, few senses, which had almost closed her throat: and the woman was breathing from a narrow passage, and I had already prohibited her eating and drinking water: and she had already come to death, if it had remained one day or two days.
And the abscess had risen so much that it had emitted two branches, until they had exited above the two nostrils.
Therefore I hastened, and fixed a hook into one of them. Then I pulled it, and a large portion came out of it.
Then I cut it off where I grasped it from the nostril.
Then I did the same to that which emerged from the other nostril.
Then I opened its mouth and pressed its tongue; and afterwards I fixed a hook into the abscess; then I cut a part from it, and only a little blood ran from it: therefore the woman's throat was healed, and she immediately began to drink water, then she took food.
Therefore I did not cease to cut from that abscess for a long time, and the abscess succeeded the place where I cut, until it was prolonged too much and with it.
Therefore I devised and cauterized the abscess between the throat.
Therefore it stood apart; then I walked away, and I did not know what God did with it after me.
Concerning the incision of the abscess of the uvula.
Chapter XXXVII.
