When the catarrh descends to the uvula, and becomes an abscess, and is long, then it is called perpendicular; and if it is in the lower part thick, round, then it is called a grape.
When it is treated with what we have said in the division, and the treatment does not help, and you see that the hot abscess has subsided from it, and it is thin, then you must incise it.
And whatever is collected from it, round, and has no length, and is bloody, or of a brown or black color, and has no feeling, then it is necessary to avoid its incision. For in it is fear upon the infirm.
Therefore, when you see it according to the manner I have described, by its whiteness and length, you must make the sick person sit facing the sun, and press his tongue with the instrument of which the account has preceded; then fix a hook into the grape, and pull it downwards; and incise it with one of the two instruments I mentioned in the incision of the two tonsils.
And it is necessary that you not incise from it, except that which is added beyond the natural limit without addition.
For if you incise from it more, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then, after the incision, use that which we have described in the incision of the two tonsils, and care for it until it heals.
If, however, the sick person is frightened by the incision, then it is necessary that you use skill in cauterizing it without fear and terror.
And the method of the cautery in it is not, except with sharp water, and it is, that you place the head of the sick person in your bosom. Then press his tongue with the instrument that we have said. Then take from the water that I have told you in the chapter on cauterization, and make with it quicklime that is not extinguished, and place it neither thick nor thin, and fill with it the concavity of this instrument, of which this is the form.
Let the concavity in which the medicine is placed be, like the concavity of a spoon's radius.
And place the instrument with the medicine upon the uvula itself; and let the sick person lie on his side, so that saliva runs from his mouth from the internal medicine, so that nothing descends from it to his throat and harms him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you shall hold it on the uvula for a half-hour, until you see it blackened and the biting of the medicine calmed.
And if you wish to take a clot, and wrap it around the end of the radius, and pour the clot with the medicine, and insert the radius with the clot into the cannula from above, until the clot adheres to the uvula.
Do this many times, until you achieve what you desire in terms of cauterizing the uvula. Then leave it; for it will dry up and fall off after three or four days.
If you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it.  And after the cauterization, wipe around the uvula with a clot soaked in butter, and cleanse with what is around it from the medicine; and wash it with cold water.
And treat it externally with embrocations, and internally with gargles, until it heals.
This method of treatment is therefore more beneficial than incision and less fearful.
And sometimes the uvula is treated with something finer than cautery and incision; for it is treated with fumigation, according to this method. Take calamint, hyssop, oregano, rue, sseah, chamomile, and wormwood, and similar herbs: and gather them all, or some of them, in a pot, and submerge them with vinegar, and boil them.  And let the pot be properly coated with clay; and let there be a hole in the middle of the pot, over which a concave instrument should be placed in this manner.
Let it be made of silver, or bronze; and let the end, on which the pomegranate is, be inserted into the patient's mouth, until the vapor ascends to the uvula over the cannula, until the uvula is greatly darkened.
Then repeat over it many times, until it dries.
And beware, lest you perform this treatment at the beginning of the abscess's onset. For it usually adds to the abscess; and it is not fitting that you do it except at the waning of its hot stage.
If however this instrument is not present for you, then take a reed and place the rind of an egg at its end, so that the patient's mouth is not burned; because the egg rind prevents the vapor from burning the mouth; and this is from a good treatment with health.
Concerning the extraction of thorns and bones from the throat.
Chapter XXXVIII.
Often a bone, or fish-thorn, or something else adheres in the throat.
Therefore, it is necessary to extract what is evident from them, upon which the sight falls, after pressing the tongue with the instrument in the presence of light, and it is revealed to you what is in the throat.
And what is not apparent to you and is hidden in the throat, it is necessary to make the weak vomit before food is digested in the stomach. For perhaps the attached thing will be expelled through vomiting.
Or the weak person will swallow a piece of turnip, or a root of lettuce, or swallow a piece of dry bread; or let him take a piece of soft dry sea sponge; and tie it to a thread, then let him swallow it. When therefore it reaches the place where the spines or bone are, and is extracted, then it departs.
If therefore it does not depart with what we said: then use an instrument of lead according to this form.
Let it be thicker than a radius a little, and at its extremity let there be a curvature, which he should introduce into his throat with ease; and let him have his head raised upwards.
And let him beware of touching his epiglottis, lest a cough befall him, and propel with it the bone, or spine, or let the physician introduce it with his hand.
But that the sick person introduce it is better: because of his knowledge of the place of the attached thing; and let him propel it downwards, or draw his hand with the instrument upwards, the whole of it according to the quantity which is prepared for him, until it departs.
Concerning the extraction of the leech.
Chapter XXXIX.
When the leech is treated with what I said in the division on treatment with medicines, and it does not help, then consider in the throat of the sick person in front of the sun, or in the sun after you have pressed his tongue with the instrument I have told you.
If therefore your sight falls upon the leech, extract it with a small hook, or with fine tweezers.
If it is not possible with them, then take a hollow tube, and introduce it into the throat of the sick person almost up to the throat; then introduce into the concavity of the tube a heated iron with fire. Do this repeatedly, and let the sick person abstain from water all day.
Then let him take a vessel full of cold water, and open his mouth in it; and let him wash his mouth with it, and not swallow anything from it; and let him move the water one hour after another with his hand. For the leech immediately, when it feels the water, falls off.
If however it does not come out with what we have narrated, then fumigate the sick person with fleas, or with asa with the instrument which I have narrated in the fumigation of the uvula.
Do that repeatedly, because it will fall off.
And the method of operation in fumigation is, that you take a pot, in which there are live prunes with fire; and the pot is covered with a cover, in whose middle is a hole; and the extremity of the instrument is placed in that hole.
Then direct the fumigation, and let the sick person place his mouth at the end of the tube, and close his mouth, so that the fumigation does not escape, until you know that the fumigation has already reached the leech. For the leech will immediately fall off.
If it does not fall off, then repeat the fumigation many times.
And let the sick person endure thirst, and eat salt and garlic, and not drink water.
Therefore, it is necessary that it depart with this treatment.
And this is the form of the instrument with which a leech is extracted from the throat, when it falls upon it in sight.
It is similar to forceps, as you see: except that it has this curvature which is inserted into the throat. And its tip is similar to the beak of a bird called a stork, on which there is the roughness of a file: when it grasps something, it does not let go of it at all.
Concerning the opening of abscesses, and their incision, and their general care.
Chapter XL.
There are many kinds of abscesses, of many types, according to their occurrence, namely, one, and one in this book; and they differ in their perforation and incision in two ways.
One of which is from the species of abscess in itself, and because it comprises moisture.
And the second way is due to the locations in which it occurs in the body.
Because an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the anus; and an abscess occurring in a fleshy area is different from an abscess occurring in the joints; and each of them has its own judgment from the operation.
And of abscesses, some must not be perforated unless after the maturation of the pus within them, and its completion; and of them some must be perforated while they are raw, and do not mature according to completion.
Such as abscesses that are near the joints.
Because when an abscess occurs near a joint, and its duration prolongs itself until the surrounding matter putrefies, perhaps the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted: wherefore it is a nearer cause of contraction of that member.
Or it is an abscess on a principal limb: for if you delay its perforation until it matures, it will harm either that limb, or through that limb, the principal limb.
Or it is near the anus, therefore perforate it unripe; and it is not fitting to perforate a raw, not fully digested abscess, except that which is near the anus, lest it putrefy deeply, and extend to the interior of the anus, and become a fistula, or become in its terminus that which is not healed.
It is necessary to know the time of perforation of an abscess which has matured according to its completion: and it is by the cessation of pain, the recession of fever, and the diminution of redness and throbbing, and the sharpness of the abscess head, and the other signs.
And it is necessary to make the same perforation in the lower place of the abscess, and of the more vehement prominence: and let the perforation be directed in the length of the body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands and feet, and the places of the tendons, and cords, and nerves, and arteries, and ultimately in all equal places where there is no reflection.
In those, however, which are reflected, proceed with the perforation along that limb.
However, when the abscess is in fleshy places, then it is better to leave its perforation until its digestion is confirmed, as we have said according to its completion.
Because if you perforate it before that, the course of the virus from it is prolonged, and there is much pus and filth, and perhaps its lips will harden, and its depth.
And some abscesses are perforated in accordance with the width of the body, where necessary, or according to the limb it needs.
And it is necessary that you administer a small perforation in small abscesses, and a large perforation in large abscesses, or many incisions in proportion to the size of the abscess.
And there are abscesses in which the skin should be hollowed out, and incised when it has already become like a cloth, and is in its final stage, that is, already dead, like that which happens in many, such as ulcers and buboes.
And some of them are cut with an incision having three angles. And some of them are incised like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Like a groin abscess.
And among them are some in which a round incision, and a crescent-shaped incision, and similar incisions are administered.
However, that which does not have a head, like mild superficial abscesses, ought to be perforated with a superficial, or merely a flat, perforation.
And it is necessary, when the abscess is large, and has already collected much pus, and you perforate it, that you do not rush to extract all the pus at once; rather, extract only a portion of it. Then plug the abscess until another day. Then extract some more of the pus.
Do this repeatedly, in stages, until it is expelled; especially if the sick person is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or a decrepit old man; for the animal spirit of the creature is often resolved with the expulsion of the pus; and perhaps the sick person dies, and you do not perceive it.
Therefore, beware of this chapter with great caution.
And after your perforation, with which you perforate these abscesses, you ought to cleanse the wound: and consider. If therefore the wound of the abscess is small, or the incision is flat, then apply strips of linen, or old cloth. And if the abscess is large, and there are many fissures in the perforation, then you ought to insert a strip into every fissure, until they meet.
And if in the abscess you have cut away a part of the skin, or hollowed it out in the surrounding area: then you ought to fill it with old cloth, or as when a cloth is woven, or shaved with linen fluff without moisture: and bind it until the third day.
Then remove it, and treat it with what is necessary from the ointment, until it is healed.
If however a flow of blood occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and vinegar, after having soaked a cloth in them; and place it repeatedly over the site of the blood flow.
If however the flow of blood persists, then you should administer the regimen, and treatment, and powders, which I have described in many places of this book of ours, and in the divisions.
If however it is during winter, and the site of the abscess is one of many nerves: then you should apply cushions with warm wine and oil; and place them over the areas.
And if it is in summer, and the areas are fleshy: then you should place cushions soaked in water and oil, or in wine and oil, and make it all cold. Until, when the third day arrives, as we said, then you must open the abscess, and cleanse it, and administer the treatment that is appropriate for it, until it is healed.
This is therefore what is necessary from the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally.
According to the path of distinction, I have now spoken of every abscess, how there is a way to its treatment, section by section, individually.
Chapter XLI.
Concerning the section of abscesses that occur on the scalp.
Small abscesses occur on the scalp, and they are of the species of nodules, which the membranes containing them enclose: as if they were the sacs of hens' eggs.
And their species are many.
Some of them are fatty; and some contain moisture similar to mud; and some contain moisture similar to gruel and porridge; and similar to these, and some are hard, stony.
And in the incision of all of them, and their extraction, there is no fear, provided that in their incision no artery is encountered.
And the procedure for their incision is that you should probe them with an instrument, whose form follows after this, called the Intromissum, until you know what they contain.
For if it contains moisture, then cut it with a superficial longitudinal incision, according to this form.
And begin with an incision from line b to line g. When the moisture has thus been evacuated, then scrape the cyst containing the moisture, and do not leave anything of it at all.
For it often returns if anything is left of it.
Then immerse the wound in Egyptian ointment, if it is present; and if not, in water and salt: and fill the wound with it, and leave it until another day. For it will corrode what remains of the cyst.
Then repeatedly apply the dressing with Egyptian ointment a second, and a third time, and if you need it, until you are confident that nothing of the cyst remains; for then treat the wound with ointment, until it is healed.
If the abscess contains a fatty tumor, then cut it according to this form, the incision having the shape of a cross; and insert hooks between the two edges of the incision, and scrape it from every side; and exert your skill in removing the tissue which contains it.
If, however, an artery opposes you, then do that which I will tell you afterward.
And similarly do in an abscess, if it is stony, by the same incision and treatment, according to what we said.
And the incision upon a stony abscess is easier, because there is little blood and moisture. And sometimes there are some of these abscesses that are in the head in some people, in which there is no moisture at all.
That is because I incised upon an abscess in the head of an old woman, and found an abscess like a solid, hard, rough, white stone, which could not be broken; and if anyone struck it while throwing something, it wounded them.
In other abscesses, however, occurring in the head, apart from those abscesses which occur in the heads of children, and at the roots of the ears, the incision of all of them should be a superficial incision.
Or make a perforation of them always from their lower part, so that the course of pus may be relieved downwards.
Then treat them with whatever is suitable for them from the treatment.
Concerning the incision of swellings on the neck.
Chapter XLII.
These abscesses often occur in the neck, and under the armpits, and in the groin: and sometimes they occur in the rest of the body.
And those that occur in the neck from them are single, and multiple: and they are generated from one another: and every swelling from them is within its own proper tunic, as is in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have narrated.
And the varieties of these swellings are numerous. And from them are stony ones; and from them are those that contain humours; and from them are malignant ones, which do not yield to treatment.
Therefore, in that one of them which you see of good disposition to the touch, whose proximity to the skin color is evident, and which moves in every direction, and is not attached to the nerves of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, nor is deep: you must excise it with a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body along the line, b, to the line, g, and scrape it from every side: and spread the two lips of the skin with a hook, or with many hooks, if you need it, as we said regarding abscesses of the head, and gradually extract it.
And it is a precaution not to cut a vein or nerve.
And let the scalpel not be very sharp, so that it does not cause you to injure your hand with the incision, or trouble the sick person, and cut what is not necessary.
However, if you cut a vein or artery, or it prevents you from the operation, then place pounded zegi in the wound, or some powders that cut off blood, and bind the wound, and leave it until the sharpness of the abscess subsides, and the wound softens; or it begins to and wants to putrefy.
For the blood is cut off then.  Therefore return to your operation until you complete it.
Then inquire with your index finger if anything remains of the small other Strophules: therefore cut them and clean them.
If there is a large vein at the root of the Strophula, then it is necessary that you do not cut that Strophula from its root; rather, it is necessary to tie it with a strong doubled thread, and leave it until it falls away by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old lint, which you have already soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then treat it.
If the entire Strophula is cut, then it is necessary that you bring together the two edges of the wound; and close it immediately, after you see that no superfluity remains at all.
If however you see that superfluity remains due to the size of the Strophula, then it is necessary that you aim to cut its root and remove it; and apply the suture which we have described.
And whatever humours the Strophulae contain, pierce it with a superficial puncture, so that you may see the site of its maturation; and place the puncture from that which follows below the body, as we have said.
Then, after the puncture, apply lint with ointment, and similar things to it, to corrode what remains of corruption, until when you see the wound now cleansed: then treat it with a generative flesh ointment, until it is healed.
Chapter XLIII.
Concerning the incision of the Epiglottis from an abscess occurring within the throat.
The ancients said this incision in the Epiglottis, and I have not seen anyone in our land who has done it: and this is the writing of their words.
In those with quinsy, the incision of the epiglottis should be avoided; for it does not bring benefit, because all the veins and the lung are weak.
In those, however, who have a hot abscess in the mouth, or throat, or both tonsils, when there is no illness in the canal, the incision of the epiglottis should be used, to avoid the harm that occurs from suffocation.
Therefore, when we wish to do it, we should cut the epiglottis below three or four rings of the epiglottis, with a small incision in width, at the point between two rings, so the incision is in the soft tissue, not in the cartilage. This place is suitable for the incision, as it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are distant from it.
If the physician is timid, then he should extend the skin of the throat with a hook. Then he should divide the skin until he reaches the canal; he should avoid the blood vessels, if he sees any of them.
Then he should cut the soft tissue as we have described: and it is signified by the incision of the canal from the phlegm that comes out of it, along with the rupture and the severance of the voice.
And leave the wound open for a time.
When therefore the hour in which suffocation is feared has passed, bring together the two lips of the wound, skin, and suture it alone, without cartilage.
Then administer medicines that cause flesh to grow, until it is healed.
Says Albucasis. The summary of this account we have given is not to be applied except when they see that some of these abscesses have already filled the throat of the sick person; and the sick person is raised above death, and his breathing is about to cease; they go to cutting the epiglottis, so that the sick person may breathe over the wound with a certain respiration, and be saved from death; and for this reason they ordered the wound to be left open until the malice of the illness is consumed, and its malice is three days, and as much as that: then, therefore, they ordered the wound to be sutured and cared for until it is healed.
And that which I saw for myself is that a certain maidservant took a knife and thrust it into her throat, and cut a portion of the lung's passageway with it.
And I was called to treat her, and I found her groaning, as is the groaning of the dead.
Therefore, I discovered from the wound, and found blood which had emerged from the small wound. Therefore, I was confident that she had not cut a vein, nor a ligament; and wind was escaping from the wound.
Therefore I hastened to the wound and treated it, until it was healed; and nothing happened to the maidservant except hoarseness in her voice; and she returned after days to her former state of health.
Therefore, I say that there is no fear in the section of the epiglottis.
Concerning the section of the hernia of the throat.
Chapter XLIV.
This abscess, called hernia of the throat, is a large abscess, according to the color of the body; and it is very common in women.
And it is in two ways: either it is natural or it is accidental.
In the natural one, certainly there is no ingenuity.
The accidental one, however, is in two ways: one of which is similar to fatty tumors. And the other way is similar to the abscess that forms from the knotting of an artery. And in it there is fear, and it is not appropriate to presume to cut into it with iron, unless it is small; and you must probe it, and investigate it with the [surgical] probe. If therefore you find it similar to a fatty tumor, and it is not attached to any vein, then cut it, as tumors are cut; and extract it with that which contains it from the cyst, if it is in a cyst; and if not, then explore the entire thing. Then treat the place with what is necessary from the treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter XLV.
Concerning the cutting of tumors.
There are many kinds of tumors, and I have already mentioned all their types in the division; and it is necessary to describe here the difference between a tumor and a fistula, since it is a source of doubt.
Therefore, I say that when a fistula is present, there is heat, fever, and intense pain until the ebullition of the superfluity subsides and the putrefaction is complete; then the fever and heat cease.
However, with a tumor there is no heat, no fever, no pain, and it contains small cysts, which are their own sacs, and they are the same color as the body.  Their beginning is like a chickpea, and it becomes like a batecha, larger or smaller.
And they are of two types: either fatty, or containing moisture. And the colors of the moisture are many, as I stated in the division.
Therefore, when you reach the treatment of a tumor, you should examine it and inquire into its nature first with an instrument called an Intromissum, as its form will be described in the chapter after this one.
And the method of investigating abscesses and all tumors is to take this instrument and insert it into the moistest place you find in the abscess, and you should slowly rotate your fingers with it until you know that the instrument has penetrated the skin. Then place your hand, according to the size of the abscess.
Then withdraw the Intromissum and consider what comes out in its wake.
If, therefore, running moisture of any color emerges, then cut it with a superficial incision, as I have said in the other abscesses.
And if no moisture emerges in the wake of the Intromissum, then know that it is greasy. Therefore, cut over it with a cross-shaped incision, according to this form, as I have taught you. And suspend it with hooks, and peel the skin from every side with ease. And if you can, guard the cyst over it, so that you may extract it healthy with the tumor.
If, however, the cyst breaks during the operation, and it is not possible to extract it whole, for this often happens, then extract it piece by piece, until nothing remains of it.
For if any small or large part remains of it, the tumor returns for the most part.
If it defeats you, and a small part remains, then fill the wound at your completion with some sharp corrosive powders, and constrict the wound, and place over it what sits as a warm abscess, and treat it with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
If therefore the tumor is large, then sew its two lips together, and treat it with that which causes it to heal.
If however there is a pulsating or non-pulsating vein there, then hurry and fill the place with ground zegi, and leave it constricted for two or three days until the wound putrefies and the blood's boiling subsides; then return to cutting what remained of the tumor.
Chapter XLVI.
Concerning the external instruments for incision and perforation.
Among them are the forms of instruments for insertion, and they are of three kinds.
For among them is a large one, and among them is a medium one, and among them is a small one.
Let them be made of iron, with squared ends, fittingly, so that they hasten the entrance into the abscess.
And these are the forms of instruments, and no, that is, piercing ones: and one of them is called *beris*, that is, piercing: and they are of three kinds.
For among them is a large one, and a medium one, and a small one.
They are suitable for investigating abscesses, and exits, and fistulas, and buboes, from whatever is between them of bones.
And others are made round, smooth, and slender, like large needles with which saddles are sewn, namely of citron wood, or of bronze, or iron, or silver.
And the best of them are those made of citron wood.
And black lead is also made, which is suitable for examining fistulas, whose concavity has reflections, so that they may be reflected with their gentleness with those reflections.
And they are again of three kinds. Since some of them are long, some are medium, and some are short, according to the size of the fistula and its constriction.
The shape of hooks, and there are many kinds.
Since some of them are simple, namely those with a single curve: and there are three kinds, as you see, Large, Medium, and Small.
And some of them are blind hooks: and there are three kinds.
And some of them are hooks having two curves, and there are three kinds: and some of them are hooks having three curves, and there are three kinds: and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the shapes of scalpels, with which knots and abscesses are cut and excoriated: and there are three kinds: since some of them are large, medium, and small.
Their cutting edges are sharp, other edges are not sharp: and they are not placed like this, unless to help in excoriating knots for fear of cutting a vein or nerve: and so that the sick person may be healed by them, and find some relief from the burning sensation he finds in the excoriation of an abscess.
They are made of bronze, and are similar to a bellows with which alcohol is made: and on the wide end a hidden spatula point is placed: and it runs into the interior, and to the exterior when you wish, as you see.
The shape of spatulas that are hidden between the fingers when perforating abscesses: and the sick do not perceive them: and there are three kinds, large, medium, and small.
Forms of cupping vessels with which blood flow is cut off: and there are three kinds, large, medium, and small.
They are made of bronze or brass, round, somewhat elongated, as you see: and let them be of a fine delicacy.
And it is necessary that there be these kinds of cupping vessels, large and small, with you, so that you may cut off blood with speed when necessary, and when no medicine is present for you.
However, do not use them for cutting off blood in every part of the body, and do not use them except on fleshy parts: such as the thighs of the legs, and the thighs, and the arms, and the nipples, and the belly, and the groin, and similar to them, from fleshy, moist parts.
And sometimes from them are made other small instruments similar to hazelnut shells, according to their shape: with which blood is cut off when it flows from the site of phlebotomy, or at the incision of a vein or artery: and sometimes they are made round in this manner.
Chapter XLVII.
Concerning the treatment of male nipples resembling those of women.
Sometimes the nipples of some men become inflated at the completion of sleep (that is, when they begin to be defiled in sleep) until they resemble the nipples of women, and remain foul-looking, ulcerated.
Therefore, whoever abhors that, should cut above the nipples with a crescent-shaped incision, according to this form from line b to line g.  Then scrape off all the fat. Afterwards, fill the wound with a flesh-forming medicine. Then bring together the two edges of the wound with sutures, and care for it until it heals.
However, if the nipple inclines downwards and softens due to its size, as happens in women, then it should be cut into two parts on its upper sides, with two similar crescent-shaped incisions, each contained within the other at the ends of each, until there is a larger line containing a smaller one, according to this form from line b to line g. Afterwards, scrape off the skin between the two edges, and remove the fat, and administer what we have said about sutures and powders, what is necessary from the treatment, until it heals.
If, however, you do not complete the incision that you ought to make, because of the patient's distress, or because of the flow of blood, then it is necessary that you fill the wound with cotton soaked in a corrosive ointment, and leave it until it corrodes what remains of the fat. Then care for it until it heals.
Concerning the perforation of an abscess, which occurs under the armpit.
Chapter XLVIII.
These abscesses which occur under the armpit are of the species of hard Strophulae, some containing cysts, and some containing moistnesses.
Therefore, those of them containing moistnesses should be cut with a crescent-shaped incision, following this form, from line b to line g.  When, therefore, all that is in the abscess, of pus, is evacuated, then fill it with old lint, and leave it until another day.
Then remove the lint, and treat it with an appropriate ointment, until it is healed.
If the abscess is of the species of knotted Strophulae, then cut on it, as we said in the section on Strophulae, equally.
If however the wound extends in this, so as to emit pus, then administer a cautery on it, according to what we said in what has been said before.
Chapter XLIX.
Concerning the incision of an abscess which occurs because of the artery and vein.
When an artery bursts and the skin above it consolidates, then very often an abscess occurs from this: and similarly it happens to the vein also, so that an abscess and swelling occur in it.
And the signs by which it is known that the abscess and swelling are due to the artery and vein are: because, when the abscess is due to the artery, it is long, gathered deep within the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel as if there is a creaking sound to it.
And that which is due to the vein is round on the surface of the body.
And cutting over this abscess is an error, and especially that which is under the armpits, and in the groin, and the neck, and in many places of the body: and it is very large. Therefore, its treatment with iron should be avoided, and that which is also from them in the extremities, or in the head, should be avoided.
Therefore, for that which is from them, from the swelling of the artery's opening, make a lengthwise incision in the skin. Then open the incision with hooks, afterwards the artery; and free it from its coverings, until it is exposed.
Then insert a needle under it, and cause it to penetrate to the other side, and tie the artery with a double thread in two places, according to what I taught you in the extraction of the two arteries that are in the temples.
Then puncture the place, which is between the two ligatures with a lancet, until all the blood contained within it comes out, and the abscess is resolved.
Then administer a treatment that produces pus until the ligatures fall off; afterward, treat it with suitable ointments until it heals.
If there is an abscess because of the incision of a vein, then it is necessary that you hold with your hand whatever you can from the abscess with the skin. Then insert a needle under the place you held with your hand, and cause it to penetrate; and let there be a double thread on it, until you extract it from the other side. Then tie the abscess itself with a good ligature, according to what I told you in the grapevine ligature, from two sides with two ends of thread.
If, however, you fear that the thread will be pulled out, then insert another needle with another thread under the entire abscess near the incision of the needle: press, and tighten your threads in four places.
Then cut the abscess in its middle, until what is within it comes out; then cut away the excess skin, and leave what is tied from it.
Then place upon it a poultice which you have steeped in wine and oil. Then administer treatment, which is done with linens and ointments, until it is healed.
Chapter L.
Concerning the abscess which occurs from the twisting of nerves.
Just as an abscess occurs in the artery and vein, so too does an abscess occur in the nerves: which occurs in them through blows, or happens from excessive labor, and similar things; and it happens most often in the places of the helpers, or the heel, and in every place where the joints move: and it is a serious abscess.
Its color is similar to the rest of the body, and it is in many dispositions without pain; and when it is squeezed with force, the sick person feels a sensation similar to numbness in it.
And it is not an abscess collected deep within the body, rather it is under the skin, and it moves to every part, and does not go forwards, or before, nor backward to the rear.
Therefore, those that are in the joints should not be lanced with iron. For perhaps contraction will result.
However, in that which is in the head, or in the forehead, cut the skin with a lancet. If therefore it is a small abscess, then hold it with scissors and cut it from the root.
And if it is large, then suspend it with a hook, and scrape it: then remove it, and join the wound with stitches: and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter LI.
Concerning the incision of an abscess which occurs in the belly.
Certain people often have, in their bellies and in their other bodies, tumors which are called Fungalia, because of their resemblance to fungi.
Their root is subtle, and their head is large, and their lips are turned inward. And some of them are small, and some of them become very large.
And indeed I have seen a man who had two tumors in his belly, which were more like fungi than other things; there was no difference between them, white, with small roots, and their lips were turned inward, and they split open, and moisture constantly flowed from them.
Therefore, I cut them open, and found one weighing approximately ten and eight ounces, and the other approximately six ounces.  And the procedure for cutting them is, that you should observe, if the patient is moist, and the color of the tumor is white, moist, with a fine root, then cut it with a broad scalpel, and have cauteries at the ready in fire.
For often a great deal of blood is expelled when it is cut; therefore, if the blood overwhelms you, then cauterize it.
If you see the patient is fearful and timid of the incision with iron, then take a thread made from lead properly, and bind it with it to the tumor; with the method we described, and let it be for two days.
Then add to the lead dressing. Therefore do not cease binding the lead, whenever it presses upon the tumor, until it is severed, and falls away by itself without effort.
If however the tumor is large in its root, then in its incision there is something of fear and dread; especially that which is in the belly.
However, it is possible to cut it in half. Then cauterize it, so that it does not return.
Then treat it, until the wound is healed.
And beware lest you presume to cut a tumor that is dark-colored, with little feeling, and foul in appearance: for it is indeed a cancerous abscess; and I shall come with a discussion of Cancer after this.
Chapter LII.
Concerning the treatment of the navel's eminence.
The navel's eminence arises from many causes. Or from fissures in the skin above the belly; and from it comes forth wind, or an intestine, according as it happens in other ruptures. Or from blood that flows from a vein, or from an artery, as was previously stated. Or from wind constricted within it.
If however it is due to a fissure in the skin, and the exit of pus, then its color is similar to the color of the body; and it is smooth, without pain; and appears in various locations. And if it is due to the extrusion of an intestine, then its location, with that which we have described, is of a more varied nature. When therefore you press it with your fingers, it disappears; then it returns, and perhaps with a sound; and it greatly enlarges at the entrance of the bath, and with great exertion.
If however it is due to moisture, it is smooth, then it does not disappear when you press it with your hand, nor does it contract, nor does it increase.
If however it is due to blood, then with these signs the abscess appears black on the side.
If however it is due to formed flesh, then it is a severe, large, hard abscess, and it adheres to a certain quantity.
If however it is due to wind, then its touch is smooth.
And the operation on it is that you should then consider, if the prominence of the navel is due to blood of the artery, or vein, or wind: then you ought to refrain from its treatment. For there is fear and terror in it, as I taught you in the chapter, in which I said abscesses due to artery and vein.
If the prominence of the navel is due to the intestine or the diaphragm, then you ought to order the sick person to hold his breath and stand stretched out. Then mark with ink around the entire navel. Afterwards, order him to lie on his back between your hands. Then encircle with a broad spatula around the navel, over the place you marked with ink. Then extend the middle of the abscess upwards with a large hook. Then tie it firmly around with thread, or with a silk cord, a strong ligature; and let the knot of the ligature be tied.
Then open the middle of the abscess, extended above the ligature. If you find it already captured by the ligature, then extract the ligature, and insert your index finger into it, and push the intestine towards the interior of the belly; and if you find a hernia, then take it with a hook and cut off its excess.
If, however, the artery or vein is opposed to you, then surround it extensively. Then return to your operation. And take two needles, and insert into them two strong threads; and insert the two needles into the circumference you have made around the abscess in the manner of a cross, causing them to penetrate.
Then tie the abscess in four places over the needles; and if you wish, remove the needles and leave the place until the extended flesh rots; and it falls away by itself; or cut it when it becomes thin and rots.
Then treat it with what is necessary of ointments, until it is healed.
If the eminence of the navel is from flesh that is there, or from moisture, then it is necessary to cauterize the abscess, as we have said. And extract the blood, or moisture, which you find in it.
Then treat it with what solidifies the place.
Chapter LIII.
Concerning the treatment of CANCER.
We have already discussed the classification of cancers and how to treat them with medicines; and we warned against treating them with iron lest they become ulcerated.  We have also spoken of cancers originating in the womb, and cautioned about their treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, such as cancer in the breasts or the thigh, and similar parts of the body, that can be removed completely; especially when it is small and newly begun.
However, when it is old and large, one should not approach it.
For I myself could not cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me who succeeded.
And the treatment for it, when it is located as we have said, is to proceed and relieve the patient from black cholera sometimes. Then, if you find it with clear fullness, a phlebotomy.
Then prepare the patient with a preparation where he is placed for the operation.
Then apply the hooks that fit it to the Cancer. Afterwards, hollow it out completely from all sides with the skin, according to your examination, until nothing of its roots remains: and let the blood run, and do not cut it quickly, rather squeeze the places, and wipe away all the thick blood with your hand, or with any instrument possible there.
However, if a large flow of blood from an artery or vein resists you: then cauterize the vein until the bleeding stops. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of dropsy.
Chapter LIII. We have already discussed in the division of the species of dropsy, and how the accumulation of water occurs: and the signs of each species, and its treatment with medicines.
And that which is treated with iron is only the species of ascites alone: and it is not applied with iron to the species of tympanitis: nor to the species of hyposarcae at all; for that is fatal.
When you have already treated this species of dropsy, tympanitis, with medicines, and your treatment has not benefited it, then consider. For if weakness has already followed the patient, or there is another infirmity with him besides dropsy, such as if there is a cough with him, or looseness of the bowels, and things similar to those: then beware of treating him with iron; for there is fear of it.
If however you see the patient of exuberant strength, and there is no illness with him besides dropsy alone: and he is not a child, nor an old man: then the method of operation in him is that you raise the patient standing between your hands, and a helper behind him will express his belly with his hand, propelling the water to the lower part of the chest.
Then take a pointed spatula, according to this form. Let it be of two pointed parts, long, with pointed ends like a spatula. However, there should be some degree of shortness gradually, so that you do not proceed with it during the operation all the way to the intestines, and injure him.
Then consider. For if the generation of dropsy is from the part of the intestines, then it is necessary that you extend the incision from the navel a distance of three fingers, downwards in a direct line above the pubic bone.
If, however, the generation of dropsy is due to liver disease, then your incision should be to the left of the navel by three fingers.
And if its generation is due to the spleen, then the incision should be on the right side; and it is also not necessary that the incision be on the side on which the sick person wishes to lie, so that the fluids do not flow to that weak place.
Then perforate the entire skin with an instrument. Then insert into that incision, and raise your hand with the spatula between the skin, and do so as if you were scraping it; and let the amount scraped off be the size of a fingernail, or approximately that.
Then perforate, doing so until the spatula reaches the empty space; and it is the place of the water. Then extract it, and insert into the hole an instrument of this shape. And it is similar to a cannula; it is made of silver, or bronze, or brass, smooth, polished; on its lower part is a small hole, and on its sides are three holes, two on one side, and one on the other; and the tip of it is cut, according to the cutting of a reed, as you see; on its upper end is a ring.
For when the instrument reaches the water, then it descends immediately upon the instrument, therefore evacuate from the water a medium amount in an hour.
Because if you evacuate from it more than is necessary, perhaps the sick person dies in an hour through the resolution of his animal spirit; or a syncope happens to him that approaches death.
But evacuate from it according to the quantity of its virtue, and according to what the dispositions of the sick person signify to you by the virtue of his pulse and by his condition.
Then withdraw the instrument, and retain the water.
This is because it is retained immediately because of the skin which retains the opening that is above the sifac: of which I spoke, that you should pierce it according to that method.
Then repeat the instrument on the following day, if you see the sick person tolerate it; and also draw out a small quantity from the water.
Do that for several days without fear, and guard yourself from error, until there remains little of the water.
However, if you fear over the sick person, and you remove much from the water: then treat him with burial in hot sand, and much sweat in a bath and sun, and let him endure thirst; and treat him with drying medicines, until he is healed; and cauterize over the stomach, and liver, and spleen, after the extraction of the water, according to what I related in what preceded.
Concerning the treatment of infants who are born in places where their urine passages are not perforated.
Chapter LV.
When any infant emerges from its mother's womb, and the opening of its urinary passage is not perforated, or is a narrow opening, or is in an improper place.
Therefore, it is necessary to provide assistance with its perforation immediately when it is born, using a very thin Spatumil, according to this form.
Then place a thin leaden nail in the opening, and tie it; and hold it for three days, or four.
When, therefore, it wishes to urinate, it is removed, and it urinates. Then return it.
And if you do not place the lead, it is possible that the urine that flows over the place will not allow it to close.
However, the one whose opening is constricted is treated with lead, as we have said, for many days, until it is widened.
However, those in whom the opening is in an inappropriate place are harmed. For this is why, of those who are born, the opening is at the end of the penis: therefore, they cannot urinate forward, until they elevate the penis with their hand upward. Nor is there generation for him, on account of this, because the sperm cannot reach the womb in a straight manner: and it is a very foul sickness.
And the method of operation in him is that the sick person is laid on his back. Then extend his penis with a forceful extension of your left hand, and cut the head of the penis from the place of the glans, with a sharp blade, or a sharp spatula, as is the incision of a reed, or as you shave something, so that its middle becomes similar to the head of the penis, and so that the opening falls in the middle, as is proper.
And beware of blood flow during your operation. For this often happens. Therefore, apply to it whatever stops the blood, and treat the wound until it heals.
Concerning Albucasis's descriptions of what happens to the foreskin and the head of the penis, and its corruption, blackness, and adhesion.
Chapter LVI.
These things, described by Albucasis, often happen to the penis, and there is a prominence of foul flesh.
And from it one is malignant, and another is not malignant.
Therefore, the non-malignant one should be suspended with a fine hook, and cut off, until the whole of it is cleaned. Then place upon it a poultice steeped in Egyptian ointment. Then treat it afterwards with the ointment dyafinicon, until it is healed.
If it is malignant, of foul colour, then it is necessary to administer a cautery to it after its incision and scraping.
If, however, the Albather is in the foreskin of an uncircumcised captive, and a part of the Albather is between the foreskin and a part of it outside, then it is necessary to remove the Albather which is inside first, until it is consolidated, then treat the outside; for when you treat both simultaneously, the foreskin is not safe from being pierced.
And similar occurrences also happen in the testicles, and in the foreskin, blackness and corruption. Therefore, it is necessary that you cauterize all that is already blackened and that wants to corrupt, or that is already corrupted.
Then apply a line over it afterward with honey which has already been mixed with the flour of pomegranate bark and the ground and sifted herbs.
Then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
If however, a flow of blood occurs, then apply the cautery with a lunar cautery, according to this form.
For the cautery is helpful for two dispositions simultaneously, namely for the flow of blood, and a wound when it becomes corrupted.
If the head of the shaft is corroded and destroyed entirely within an hour, then it is necessary to insert a lead cannula into the urethra, so that the sick man urinates over it.
However, in the adhesion of the foreskin to the head of the shaft, and this adhesion does not occur unless in one whose foreskin is healthy, and circumcision is not required upon him: and sometimes adhesion occurs due to a wound, or an abscess: therefore, it is necessary to scrape it with a blunt Spatumili, until the ligament is loosened, and the head of the shaft is freed from every part.
If it is difficult to distinguish them thoroughly, then it is necessary to scrape something from the head of the shaft, and from the foreskin. That is because the foreskin, when it is thin, is perhaps perforated quickly because of its thinness.
Then separate the foreskin and the head of the shaft with a thin linen cloth, which has already been soaked in cold water, so that they do not adhere again.
Then treat it with astringent wine, until it is consolidated.
Chapter LVII.
On the cleansing of infants, and the treatment of errors that befall them.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity, like other wounds; however, since it is an operation performed by our hand, of our own will, and its use is in boys specifically, it is necessary that we mark it with a better operation and a gentler path leading to health.
Therefore I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books, since they do not use it in their laws, and it is only what we have acquired through experiment.
This is because I have found most artisans and phlebotomists using cleansing, cutting with a razor and with scissors, and the experienced using cautery and ligation with thread, and incision with a nail.
And indeed I have already tested all these methods, and have not found a better cleansing than with scissors and ligation with thread.
Because in cleansing with a razor, the skin often escapes. Because the skin of the foreskin has two layers: therefore, perhaps the upper layer of skin is cut, and the lower layer remains; therefore, it is necessary to cut again in order to cut the other, and future pain will result. And with cleansing performed by cautery, that is, digital, which the surgeons use, the incision of the extremity of the male member is not secured; because perhaps it enters into its opening.
In cleansing with a cautery, perhaps the iron slips out and corrupts your operation.  And the skin of the boy is of short nature, and they are often born in such a way that they do not need cleansing; and I have seen that.
However, cleansing with scissors, and ligature with thread, proved a good experiment to me; because scissors are proportional to the incision: since the sharpness which is above is like the sharpness which is below. Therefore, when you press your hand, the two sharpnesses are proportioned, and they cut according to one operation at one time; and a compression is formed in a similar shape, containing the skin of the penis from every side, with which no error falls at all.
And the proper method of operation is, that you make the boy, especially if he is one of those who understand a little, realize that you do not do anything other than tie a thread to his penis and leave him until another day.
Then make him rejoice and be happy in every way that is possible for you, on account of what his intellect receives. Then have him stand between your prepared hands, and do not let him be seated; and conceal the scissors in your sleeve, or under your foot, so that the boy sees them not at all, nor anything of the instruments.
Then insert your hand into his penis, and blow on the skin, and pull it upwards, until the head of the penis emerges. Then cleanse it of whatever filth collects on it.
Then tie the marked place with a double thread. Afterwards tie a second ligature a little lower. Then hold with your index finger and thumb the area of the lower ligature with good retention, and cut between the two ligatures. Then lift the skin upwards quickly and remove the head of the rod. Then allow the blood to run slowly (for this is better) and place powder on top; and on the powder, a cloth on which is spread the yolk of a hard-boiled egg in rosewater, mixed with good fresh rose oil; and leave it on top until the next day; and treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
The shape of the scissors for cleansing is this.
Let the two ends be blunt and meeting; so that a nail fragment does not escape them; and let the length of the two sharpened parts be the same as the length of the two handles taken equally.
An error occurring in cleansing is: because perhaps the entire inner skin, or part of it, escapes at the incision.
Therefore, you should extend it from your hour with your finger before the suppuration of the place, and cut it in equal proportions.
If you cannot hold it with your finger, then pull it out with a hook and cut it.
If however three days have passed and that which is under the rod remains swollen, suppurated, then leave it until the boil subsides, and peel it off easily, and cut it as is prepared for you, and beware of the head of the rod. For that will not harm it.
Therefore, treat it with that which consolidates a wound from the powders we have described in the treatise on powders, and if it is cut from the skin beyond measure, and escapes to the upper parts, that will not harm it either.
Therefore treat it with that which we said, until it is healed.
Chapter LVIII.
Concerning the extraction of retained urine in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder occurs from an obstruction caused by a stone, or congealed blood, or pus, or growing flesh, and similar things to those which we have mentioned from the methods of treatment in the division.
And if the urine is not released, and you see that its retention is due to a stone that is already in the neck of the bladder, then it is necessary that he be weak and bent over on his knees. Then let his feet be placed upon his back, and let him stretch his belly from the upper part of his back, and let the weak man draw in his breath as much as possible. Thus, the stone is driven by the neck of the bladder to the rear, wherefore the urine is released.
If, however, it is not released with what we have said, and the matter is severe upon the sick man, then it is necessary to administer its extraction with an instrument, which is called a syringe, whose form is this: Let it be of silver, and let it be thin, smooth, concave like a bird's feather cannula in its fineness, long in the likeness of one and a half palms, to which there shall be a thin cannula at its head.
And the way of the urine's exit through it is, that you take a doubled thread, and tie to its extremity wool, or cotton, with a good ligature: and introduce the extremity of the thread into the lower part of the Syringe: and cut with scissors if any wool projects, but introduce into the cannula with force, like a lever.
Then anoint the syringe with oil, or with butter, and the white of an egg: and make the sick man sit upon a seat, and embrocate the bladder, and his penis with moist oils, and olive oil, and tepid water.
Then introduce the syringe into the penis with ease, until it reaches the root of the penis.
Then double it upwards from the navel's part.
Then drive the syringe into it, until it reaches the Bladder, and the sick person feels that it has reached the empty place: and this is not done in this order, unless because the passage, in which it goes, is in which is tortuousness.
Then extend the thread with wool gradually. For urine will follow the wool.
Then pull it out, and make the urine run through the urine passage: and repeat the syringe, and do not cease to do that, until the bladder is emptied, and the sick person finds relief.
Concerning the quality of bladder clysterization with a Clepsydra.
Chapter LIX.
When a sore occurs in the bladder, or blood is congealed in it, or pus is collected in it, and force is used to pour water and medicines into it, then it is done with the instrument called Clepsydra, and this is its form.
For it is deaf, that is, closed. And in it are three openings, two on one side, and one on the other side, as you see.
And the concave place, in which that which propels it is, should be according to the quantity that fills it without addition, until when something is drawn with it, humidities are drawn out: and when it is propelled, it is expelled far with it: according to how the clepsydra does, by which naphtha, that is, Greek fire, is thrown in naval battles.
Therefore, when you wish to project the humidities into the bladder, introduce the extremity of the clepsydra into the humidity, and draw with that which propels it upwards, for the humidity is drawn into the concavity of the clepsydra.
Then introduce its extremity into the tube, as we have described in the syringe. Then propel the humidity with that which propels it; for that humidity reaches the bladder immediately, so that the weak person feels it.
Form of a slender clyster with which the bladder is clysterized.
Let it be made of silver or brass; its head should be higher, similar to a small alembic, and below it a sinus into which a ligature should fall.
Then take a ram's bladder, and place in it the humidity with which you wish to clyster the bladder. Then tie it between the two sinuses with a strong ligature with a doubled thread: and heat that humidity a little over the fire.
Then introduce the end of the clyster into the tube, afterwards grasp your hand over the bladder with the humidity with a decent constriction, until the sick person feels that the humidity has now reached the bladder.
If however you do not have a bladder present, then take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it, and pierce it throughout its entire circumference. Then introduce a strong thread into those holes, as they revolve. Then aggregate the circle, as a vessel is aggregated: after you place in it what you wish of the humidities, and oils, and waters.
Then tie it to the instrument, and do with it as you did with the bladder by pressing with your hand, until the humidity reaches the bladder.
Chapter LX.
On the extraction of the Stone.
We have already spoken in the division of the species of Stone, and its treatment with medicines; and I have explained the difference between a stone generated in the kidneys, and the bladder, and the signs of each: and I have discussed the stone in which the operation is by hand and incision; and this is the stone generated in the bladder properly; and that which is fixed in the urethra; and I am relating it with clear explanation and brevity.
Therefore, I say that a stone generated in the bladder happens most frequently in children.
And from its signs is that the urine issues forth from the bladder similar to water in its subtlety; and white sand appears in it, and the infirm person rubs their urethra, and suffers with it, and frequently hangs down. Then it rises, and the anus discharges with it in many of them.
And the cure is easy for children, up to the point when they reach fourteen years. And it is difficult in the elderly. However, in young people it is intermediate, in that which is between those things; and the cure is easier for someone whose stone is larger; and if it is small, the cure is conversely difficult.
Therefore, when we arrive at the treatment, it is necessary first to cleanse the sick person with an enema, which will remove all the waste that is in their intestines; for it sometimes prevents the discovery of the stone during the examination.
Then let the sick person be taken with their feet, and shaken, and moved downwards: so that the stone descends to the depth of the bladder, or leaps from a high place sometimes.
Then have him sit between your hands, prepared, and let his hands be under his thighs, so that the entire bladder is inclined downwards.
Then examine him and touch him externally.
If therefore you feel a stone in the space, then hasten immediately with the incision upon it.
But if it does not fall under your touch at all, then it is necessary that you wipe the index finger with the oil of your left hand, if the sick person is a child; or the middle finger if he is a full youth; and insert it into his anus, and search for the stone until it stands under your finger; and turn it gradually towards the neck of the bladder.
Then press upon it with your finger, and drive it outwards against the place which you wish to cut; and command the attendant to press the bladder with his hand, and command another attendant to extend the testicles with his right hand upwards; and with his other hand to remove the skin which is beneath the testicles on the side of the place where the incision is.
Then insert the scalpel, the shape of which is this. And make a cut in the space between the anus and the testicles, and not in the middle, towards the left buttock; and let the incision be over the stone, and your finger be in the anus; and let the cut be transverse, so that the incision be wide on the outside and narrow on the inside, according to the amount that it is possible that the stone egress from it be not larger.
For perhaps the finger, which is in the anus, compresses the stone at the incision, and it emerges without difficulty.
And know that if the stone has angles and edges, therefore its exit becomes difficult because of that.
And if it is smooth, like a gland, and round: and its exit becomes easy.
Therefore, to the one that has angles and edges, add in the fissure.
If it does not emerge thus, then it is necessary to devise something above it: either to bind upon it with a well-made instrument, whose end is like the Moon, which binds upon the stone, and does not escape from it; or to introduce under it a slender instrument with a curved end.
If however you cannot with it, then enlarge the hole a little. If something from the blood overcomes you, cut it with a zegi.
If however the stones are more than one, then drive the largest one first towards the bladder's opening. Then cut upon it. Afterwards, drive the small one after that; and similarly do if there are more than two.
If it is very large, then it is ignorance to cut upon it with a large incision. Because it happens to the infirm one of two things, either that he dies or that a constant dripping of urine happens to him:  because the place does not entirely consolidate.
But administer its expulsion, until it comes out; or apply ingenuity in its fracture with scissors, until you extract it piece by piece.
When you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with frankincense, and aloe, and dragon's blood; and bind it, and place upon it a cloth steeped in oil and wine, or rose-oil, and cold water, so that a hot abscess may be seated.
Then lay the infirm person on their back, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day. When you have therefore loosened it, embrocate the place with water and much oil.
Then treat it with the ointment dyafinicon and basilicon ointment, until it is healed.
However, if in the wound a hot abscess is added, and corrosions, and similar things: such as if blood is congealed in the bladder, and urine is prevented from it; and that is known from the expulsion of blood with the urine. Therefore, insert your finger into the wound, and extract that blood. For if it remains, it provokes corruption of the bladder, and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt; and apply of every kind with what is suitable from healing, until it is healed.
And it is necessary in the hours of the entire treatment, that you bind the two thighs, and the buttocks, for the sake of the medicaments which are placed on the area.
If a small stone is in the urethra, and is fixed in it, and prevents the exit of urine: then treat it with what I describe before you reach the stage of incision. For many times you will be spared from incision by this treatment, for I have already proven it.
And it is to take a perforator of iron, whose form is this, of triangular extremity, sharp, fixed in wood.
Then take a thread, and tie the urethra under the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then insert the perforating iron into the urethra with ease, until the perforator reaches the stone itself: and rotate the perforator with your hand on the stone gradually: and strive to pierce it, until you make it penetrate from the other side.
For the urine is immediately discharged.
Then press your hand on that which remained of the stone from the outside of the urethra. For it indeed breaks, and it goes out with the urine, and the sick person is healed.
If, however, this treatment is not prepared for you, because of something that prevents it: then tie a thread beneath the stone, and another thread above the stone. Then cut above the stone in the urethra itself, between the two ligatures. Then extract it. Afterwards, loosen the ligature, and cleanse the congealed blood that forms in the wound.
And a ligature of thread beneath the stone is not necessary, unless so that it does not return to the bladder; and another ligature above it, unless so that when the thread is loosened after the stone's expulsion, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound; and for that reason you must do this when you tie the thread above, to raise the skin upwards, so that it returns to its proper position and covers the wound, as we have said.
Chapter LXI.
Concerning the extraction of the Stone in women.
Little stone is generated in women.
If, however, a stone happens to any of them, then the cure is difficult, and it is forbidden because of many methods.
One of them is: that the woman is perhaps a virgin.
And the second is, because you do not find a woman who reveals herself to a physician, when she is chaste, or from those having husbands.
The third is, because you do not find a woman well knowing this art, especially the operation with the hand.
And the fourth is the distance from the place of the stone: wherefore it needs a deep incision, and in that is fear.
If necessity provokes to that, then it is necessary to accept a female physician, well knowing: and few are found.
If, however, you conceal it from her, then seek a chaste, skillful physician, and present a female midwife well-versed in women's matters, or a woman who touches upon this part of the art: make her present, and especially to her, to do all that you prescribe to her from investigation of the stone.
First of all, that it should be seen if the woman is a virgin: then it is necessary that he insert his finger into her anus, and seek the stone.
If therefore he finds it, he should confine it under his finger, and then command the midwife to insert her finger into the vagina of the afflicted woman, and inquire concerning the stone, after she places her left hand upon the bladder, and compresses it with a good compression.
If therefore she finds the stone, then it is necessary that she move it gradually from the orifice of the bladder downwards with all her strength, until she reaches it at the root of the thigh.
Then she shall cut upon it at the opposing midpoint of the vulva at the root of the thigh, from whatever side it is prepared for her, and feels the stone in that part: and her finger shall not be removed from the stone, confining it below; and the incision shall be small at first. Then she shall insert a rod upon that incision.
Then she shall insert a rod upon that incision. If therefore she feels the stone, then she shall add to the incision according to the proportionate quantity that the stone emerges from it.
And know that the species of stone are many. For from these are the small, and the large, the smooth and the rough, the long, and the round with branches.
Know therefore its species, so that it may be signified to you through this concerning that which you wish.
If therefore blood overcomes you, then add in the place pounded zegi, and hold it one hour, until the blood is separated.
Then return to your operation, until the stone departs: and do as you prepare yourself with instruments, which I have said in the extraction of stones in men, so that you may aid through them in your operation.
If, however, a flood of blood overcomes you, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery that has been cut: then place powder on the place, and bind it with bandages in a suitable constriction; and do not change it; and leave the stone, and do not extract it. For perhaps the weak woman will die. Then treat the wound.
When therefore the sharpness of the blood subsides after days, and the place becomes putrid, then return to your operation, until the stone departs.
Chapter LXII.
Concerning the incision of a watery hernia.
A watery hernia is not present unless there is a collection of moisture in a white sifac, which is under the skin of the testicle containing the egg; and it is called sifac.
And sometimes it is in its own membrane, which nature prepared for it in part of the egg, until it becomes putrid; which is another egg, and there is the skin of the testicle between the white sifac, which we mentioned; and that does not happen except rarely.
And the generation of this hernia is indeed from the weakness that happens to the testicles; wherefore this matter is poured out to them. And sometimes it happens from a blow to the testicles.
And this moisture has many colours. For either its colour inclines to yellowness, or it is blood-red, or foul, black, or watery white, and there is also that which is more.
And the signs by which it is known where the aggregation of water is, are these. For if it is in the white sifac that we mentioned, then the abscess is round, somewhat the length of an egg; and the testicle does not appear, because the moisture encompasses it on every side.
And if the moisture is in its own membrane, then the abscess is round with the part of an egg, and on account of this the man estimates that it is another egg.
And if the moisture is between the skin of the testicles and the white sifac, then it falls under the sense.
Therefore, when you seek knowledge of the color of the moisture, then test the color of the moisture in the abscess with the square instrument, whose shape preceded. Therefore, according to that which emerges in the trace of the instrument, judge about that which is between it.
Therefore, when we come to treatment with iron, then it is necessary that you command the sick person that he be bled, if it is possible for him, and you see his body full.
Then let him be laid on his back upon a slightly raised object, and place many cloths beneath him.
Then sit you on his left side, and command the attendant to sit on his right side, who should extend his rod to one of the two sides of the skin of the testicles, and to the part of the navel of the abdomen.
Then take a broad spatula, and cut the skin of the testicles from the middle lengthwise, until near the pubic bone: and let the incision be according to the straightness of an equidistant line that divides the skin of the testicles, until it reaches the white sifac which it contains: and scrape it and observe, or beware from this, that you cut it: and let your scraping with which you scrape it, be from the part that hangs with the egg more: and carefully examine it with the scraping according to the amount that is possible for you.
Then pierce the sifac full of water with a large hole, and extract all the water.
Then separate the two edges of the incision with hooks, and extend the sifac upward, and do not touch the skin of the testicles which encompasses it: and cut the sifac however it is possible for you to cut it, either with its totality or in fragments, especially its thin side. For if you do not carefully examine it, there is no security in its incision, lest the water return.
If, however, the egg protrudes externally from its skin during your operation: then when you have completed the incision of the sifac, return it. Then bring together the two edges of the scrotal skin with sutures.
Then treat it like the other wounds until it heals.
If you find a corrupted egg from another blackness, then you ought to tie the vessels, which are the suspensory ligaments, fearing a flow of blood.
Then cut off the testicle with the suspensory ligament, and remove the egg; afterwards, treat it as we have said.
If, however, water is collected in two parts, then know that there are two branches: therefore cut the other part as you did the other equally.
And if it is equal to you, so that there is one operation, then do it; afterwards wipe away the blood. And insert into the sections wool soaked in oil, or in rose oil; and place outside another wool which you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread it over the two testicles and the navel; and place over it doubled cloths, and they are cushions; and bind them over it with a bandage, which has six according to this form.
Then take two cloths, and sew them together, and fill them with dyed wool, according to this likeness; and let the extremities by which it is pressed be made of linen, or damp wool; and let them be joined together with a constriction in the lumbar region of the breeches; and one extremity shall take over the anus up to the back, and the second shall take to the side of the crest; and two extremities over the two thighs below, and two other extremities over the two groins, and let the entire constriction be joined in the lumbar region of the breeches.
And sometimes this branch is artificially treated with a cautery in the place of the incision with iron: and it is that you take a thin, knife-like cautery, and cut with it the skin of the testicles; and it is heated, as we have described; until when it is revealed a white matter containing water; then take the cautery, according to this form.
It resembles the Greek letter.
Then pierce it with it, and let it be heated until all the moisture comes out.
Then stretch the sifac with hooks, and scrape it with a heated thin cautery, and cut it as much as possible, until you eradicate all of it.
And be careful, from the egg, that the fire does not touch it.
And know that this operation is safer, and farther from blood flow, and better than the operation with incision.
If, however, it creates opposition for you at the hour of incision, or a hot abscess at the hour of cautery, or blood flow, or anything else: then it is necessary to cure that which creates opposition for you from it with the cure which suits it, as was previously stated.
If, however, the patient is fearful and does not expect this operation, because of its horror: then administer the perforation according to what I describe it: and it is so.
When it is certified to you by that which we have said about signs, that the branch is watery: then it is necessary that you make the patient sit on a raised seat.
Then perforate the abscess in its lower part with a broad spatula: and place the perforation along the length of the body, and let the opening be wide, until all the water runs out.
Then place a poultice over the wounded area, and bandage it, and allow it to run the remaining water; then treat it until the wound is healed.
If, however, there is opposition to the egress of water at the time you perforate it: and it is not unless because a white membrane causes opposition in the wound's orifice, and prevents the exit of water: then it is necessary that you introduce a pen into the wound, or an instrument with which the water of hydrops is extracted, whose form preceded: or add a little to the wound's operation.
And know that the water returns, and gathers again after six months, or approximately those. When therefore it gathers again, then let it be perforated according to the same method, and the infirm person will pass their days.
Chapter LXIII.
Concerning the treatment of fleshy tumors.
Know that in the incision on this tumor, there is fear of leading to destruction in many dispositions. Therefore, it seems to me that its removal is salvation from it.
And I am recalling the operation on it, and the appearance of the abscess.
I say therefore, that many abscesses occur in the bodies from which the testicles are composed; and that happens from many causes, namely.
Or from an acute, or acrid, excess that is poured onto the testicles. Or from a blow.
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it; and the abscess is serious.
And perhaps it is a stony abscess, whose color is dark, and no sensation is in it.
