And sometimes it arises from the knotting of arteries and their inflation, as its previous mention has already shown, or from the inflation of veins.
Therefore, what arises from them from the inflation of the arteries is recognized by the arrangement of the abscess, when you compress it with your fingers. And you should not touch it completely.
However, that which arises from the inflation of veins does not spread from it anything upon compression with the fingers.
And now it is possible that a section be made on this kind, and on the other flesh: and it is like cutting the skin of the testicles. Then raise the egg upwards, and extract it with the white sifach: and release the suspensory from the vessels, and tie the vessels: and cut the suspensory after you have freed it from every part from the parts of the egg.
If therefore the egg is consolidated with those born flesh, then it is necessary that you pull the egg and cut it.
And if there is adhesion between some of the siphaket, or in that which is between the vessels: then it is necessary that you free the entire adhesion and cut it with a round incision.
If however there is a birth of flesh in the place of adhesion which is behind, then it is necessary that you cut the whole of it, and extract the egg, as we have said.
When therefore your operation is completed, then fill the wound with wool infused in rose oil and wine; then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter LXIII.
Concerning the treatment of Ramicis, which is with the Vite.
Vitis is an abscess enveloped by a certain wrapping, similar to a bunch of grapes, with softening of the testicles: and it causes difficulty in movement and exercise, and walking, for the infirm.
And in the treatment of this illness there is fear, because of that which preceded it, remembered.
But it is necessary, as we say, that the operation be performed in it according to what the first ones did.
I say therefore, that it is necessary to make the sick person sit upon a raised seat. Then push down the suspensory ligament of the testicles. Then hold the skin of the testicles with your fingers, together with the vessels that are near the penis; and let another servant hold them, and stretch them with a forceful extension.
Then cut with a broad, sharp scalpel, making a transverse incision directly over the vessels, until the vessels are exposed.
Then scrape it from all sides, as we told you in the extraction of the arteries that are in the temples.
Then insert a needle with double thread into them, and tie it in the first of the places where the veins occur, and tie it again in the last of them. Then cut them in the middle, straight along the length of the body, and extract that which is collected from corrupt, putrid moisture.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of the other wounds, in which the force of generating pus, so that the vessels that nourish one testicle may fall. For there is no evil through it.
However, if it happens to all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be extracted along with the vessels, so that the egg is not deprived of nourishment due to the incision, and it may waste away or dry up, and there is no benefit with them.
Chapter LXV.
Concerning the treatment of intestinal rupture.
The outcome of this rupture is from a cut, which happens in a dilated sifach over the abdomen in the groin area from a wound of the abdomen.
Why do the intestines descend from that rupture to one testicle. And this rupture is either from the sifach, or its dilation.
And these two types occur from many causes: either from a blow, or a jump, or a shout, or the lifting of a heavy object, and similar things.
And the sign, when it is from the dilation of the sifach. It is that which happens gradually over a long time, and does not happen suddenly; and it is an abscess equal towards the depth; because the sifach compresses the intestine.
And the sign of it when it is from a section of the sifach, because it causes a sudden, severe pain from the beginning; and it is a different abscess, appearing under the skin nearby; and that is because of the exit of the intestine, and its course towards the exterior of the sifach.
And sometimes it emerges with the intestine through the zirbum; and this is called a 'Ramex intestinalis' and 'zirbia'; and sometimes it occurs with windiness.
And sometimes faeces run into the intestine and are retained there; and with it is the harm of the infirm person, because it causes mild pain, and especially when pressed.
And in the treatment of these types of illnesses, there is fear with iron; therefore, one must be cautious of the cases in it.
And the method of operation is, that you should command the infirm person to reduce the intestine with their own hand into the interior cavity, if it obeys the return. Then he is to be placed supine on his back between your hands, and his legs are to be raised.
Then extend the skin which follows the skin upwards, and cut the skin of the testicles completely along its length. Then fasten hooks into the two lips of the incision, according to what you need to open the incision with them; and let the incision be according to the amount that it is possible for the egg to emerge from it.
Then scrape off the siphak, and what is beneath the skin of the testicles, until when the white, hard siphak is revealed on all sides; then insert your index finger into that which follows the testicle, into that which is between the white siphak, which is beneath the skin of the egg, and the second siphak, and separate with it the adhesion which is on the posterior part of the egg.
Then, with your right hand, move the skin of the testicles inwards; and with your left hand, extend the white siphak upward; and lift the egg with the siphak towards the incision; and instruct the assistant to extend the egg upwards; and you separate the adhesion that is behind, completing the separation.
And examine with your fingers, that there is nothing there from the intestine wrapped in the hard white siphak. If however you find anything of it: then push it towards the lower abdomen.
Then take a needle with thick thread, made of linen from ten threads; and insert it near the last siphak, that is, the tunic, which is under the skin of the testicles that follows the incision; then cut the ends that protrude from the thread, until there are four threads. Then arrange some of them over some, with a figure that is in the form of a cross; and tie with them the alsifach, of which we spoke, because it is under the skin of the testicles with a strong ligament, until no vessels which nourish them can send anything to them; so that no hot abscess may occur from it. And place again a ligature second, outside the first ligature, distant from it less than two fingers. And after these two ligatures, leave from the sifach, which is under the skin of the testicles, a quantity the size of a finger; and cut the rest entirely around, and remove the egg from it.
Then cut in the lower part of the skin of the testicles, the incision from which blood and pus runs, as we have narrated in what was previously said.
Then apply wool soaked in oil, and place it on the wound; and apply the ligature which we have described. And sometimes the white sifach that we mentioned is cauterized, after its incision, fearing a flow of blood. For it often happens. And leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
If, however, its falling is delayed, then it should be soaked with warm water; and place upon it that which we mentioned, until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with the other treatments for wounds, until it is healed.
Chapter LXVI.
Of the windy rupture.
In this rupture, I have not seen anyone who dared to use iron for its treatment.
And indeed the first ones said that it occurs as we have described in the rupture which is with the vine. That is, tie the vessels after the incision over them with ease from below; then cut in the middle, and finally treat it with that which opens it, until the vessels fall away; and treat the wound, as we have said, until it heals.
Chapter LXVII.
Concerning the rupture which occurs in the groin.
When a rupture occurs in the groin, as we have said, and the area protrudes, and nothing from the intestines descends to the testicles; and if anything does descend, it is little, and returns at all hours; but if time continues with it, a fissure is added in the scrotum, until the intestines descend into the scrotum.
And that occurs from the stretching of the scrotum, which is in the groin, as we have said. That is because the scrotum is stretched; then the area softens, and the place becomes suppurated, and protrudes.
And the treatment for it is with a cautery, as its previous description stated.
And sometimes it is treated with iron, in this way: and it is that the sick person lies on their back between your hands. Then cut the place of the suppurated and protruding rupture, with a cut across the width, according to the quantity of three fingers.
Then draw the scrotum and whatever is under the skin, until when the white scrotum is revealed, which is under the skin that follows it.
Then take a rod, and place it upon the protruding place of the scrotum, and press it to the depth of the belly.
Then sew the two protruding places on the extremity of the rod from the scrotum, and connect one of them to the other with sutures; then pull out the extremity of the rod.
And do not cut the scrotum at all, nor stretch the egg, nor anything else, just as I have made you know in the treatment of intestinal colic.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds. When therefore you cut the threads, cleanse it, and restore the wound, until it is consolidated.
For the scrotum contracts, and no prominence of rupture is added.
And the cautery is better in this place, because it is closer to health.
Chapter LXVIII.
Concerning the softening of the skin of the testicles.
Many times the skin of the testicles softens in some men, on account of which the flesh between them softens, and its appearance is spoiled.
Therefore it is necessary for whoever is asked to treat it, that he cause the sick man to lie upon his back, and that he cut all the skin that has softened over the natural skin.
Then he shall join the two lips with sutures; and if he wishes, first sew up the excess of softened skin with three or four sutures, and make the suture firm.
Then cut between the sutures.
Then treat the remaining wounds with care, until they heal and the threads fall out.
Chapter LXIX.
Concerning Castration.
Know that castration is excommunicated under our law; and because of that, it would be permissible for me to not mention it in this book of mine; and its mention is not necessary except for two methods.
One of which is that it should be within the physician's knowledge when questioned about it; and so that he knows the treatment for it should it happen.
And the other method is that we often need the castration of certain animals, for our benefit through it, such as rams, boars, and cats, and similar animals.
I say, therefore, that castration is done according to two methods. Or by attrition. Or by fissure and section.
In that which is done by attrition, the way of the operation is: that we make the animal sit in warm water until its testicles are softened and relaxed and hang down; then rub them with your hand until they are dissolved and no longer appear under the touch.
In castration by fissure and section, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament, and cut above each testicle with one cut, until when two testicles proceed; then cut them after you have flayed each, and do not leave anything on them from sifaket except a subtle sifac that is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than that which is done by attrition, because attrition perhaps leaves something of the testicles, wherefore the animal desires intercourse.
Then treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
Concerning the treatment of a hermaphrodite, there are two methods.
A hermaphrodite is from males in two ways. One of them is that there appears in it, at the interval which follows, or in the skin of the testicles, in the space between the two testicles, a figure like a woman's vulva, in which are hairs; and sometimes urine flows from that which is in the skin of the testicles.
However, in women, there is one kind, and it is a woman's vulva above the pubis, just as very small male testicles, protruding outward; of which one is like a man's penis, and two like two testicles.
And the treatment of three kinds, two from men, and one kind from women, is: because it is necessary to cut away the added flesh, until the pressure of it escapes. Then treat them with the treatment of other wounds.
In the second kind of men, however, from which urine flows, which is in the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor is there any treatment at all.
Chapter LXXI.
Concerning the incision of the Tentigo, and the protruding flesh in women's vulvas.
Tentigo perhaps arises upon a natural condition, until its appearance is disfigured and ugly; and sometimes in some women, it magnifies to such an extent that it expands like in men and even reaches the point of intercourse.
Therefore, you should hold the excess Tentigo with your hand, or with a hook, and cut it; and do not proceed too deeply in the incision, especially into the deep root, so that bleeding does not occur.
Then treat it with the treatment for wounds until it heals.
However, the flesh that arises is flesh that grows within the opening of the womb until it fills it; and perhaps it protrudes outward, resembling a tail; and because of this, some earlier writers call the ailment "tailed disease. Therefore, it is necessary to cut it, as we cut Tentigo, and treat it until it heals.
Concerning the treatment of Alratica, which is the unperforated vulva of a woman.
Chapter LXXII.
Alratica is, as it were, a woman's vulva that is not perforated, or has a small opening. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is from illness that preceded it: and indeed it arises either from added growing flesh, or from a thin sifach, or a thick one; and it occurs either deep within the womb, or on its sides, or on its upper, or lower part; and it prevents intercourse and impregnation, and childbirth, and perhaps it prevents menstruation.
And its knowledge is achieved through the obstetrician's examination, if there is an apparent nearby blockage.
If, however, it is not apparent, then seek it with your fingers, or with a probe.
If however the blockage is from a thin sifach, and is close to the two lips: then hurry, and break it.
And it is, that you place cushions on the two lips.
Then place your index fingers of both hands together, and let the woman be on her back with her legs spread apart. Then extend the two lips with strength, until the thin sifach is broken, and the blockage is opened.
Then take wool, and soak it in oil, and place it on the place: and let the woman do this every day, so that the place does not solidify again.
If however the sifach is thick, dense: then it is necessary that you cut it with a broad spatula similar to a myrtle leaf.
If, however, the blockage is born of flesh, then suspend it with hooks, and cut it: and let there be medicines with you that stop the flow of blood without causing a bite, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense mixed with the white of an egg.
Then administer a wide lead cannula, so that the wound does not quickly solidify, hold it for a few days: and administer a linen plaster, then treat the rest with the usual treatment, until it is healed.
And sometimes other flesh-births arise within the womb: therefore it is necessary that they be cut in this way, for as long as it is not a cancerous abscess; for a cancerous abscess that is in the womb, do not use any opposition with iron at all.
Chapter LXXIII.
Concerning the treatment of hemorrhoids, Athehelil, and red Bothor in the vulvas of women.
Hemorrhoids indeed are an opening of the veins' orifices, until much blood runs from them constantly.
When hemorrhoids become chronic, they become thelil; and I have already discussed their species and signs in the division.  Here I will state which of them receive treatment, and which do not.
Therefore I say that hemorrhoids which are in the orifice of the womb, and do not appear to the senses, then there is no treatment with iron for them.
However, those of them that are in the orifice of the womb, over which the senses fall, are those that are treated.
Therefore, it is necessary to put the woman into a cold room. Then, with forceps or a rough cloth, extend the thelil and cut them from their roots.
Then, sprinkle over them, when the blood flows from the incisions, some of the powders without mordication: such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense, and similar to them.
Then let her feet be pressed against the wall for one hour. Afterwards, lay her in cold water.
If the flow of blood persists, then have her sit in a decoction of sumac, and pomegranate bark, and gallnuts, and similar things.
Then take barley flour, and mix it with honey and vinegar, and make a plaster with it on her back.
If the blood is thus cut off, it is good; and if not, apply cupping vessels to the back and breasts, without scarification.
Then take wool, and immerse it in the juice of tarathit, or the juice of arnogloss, or the juice of alaulat branches, that is, aglanter, Pontic wine, and apply it to the affected area.
When the abscess therefore subsides, then treat the area with ointments until it is healed.
Albarth is red, similar to the heads of alhaasse, and has a rough appearance.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut away what appears from it, according to what I said in the thelil equally, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter LXXIV.
Concerning the perforation of the exit of the womb.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the womb, such as cancer, and stony abscess, and ulcers, and tumors, and boils, and herpes, and carbuncles, and fistulas, and hemorrhoids, and fissures, and thelil, and a hot abscess.
And I have already discussed all these illnesses and their kinds, and their signs, and their cures in the divisions: it is necessary to discuss in this treatise the hot abscess that occurs in the womb, when it is from the abscesses that collect pus, and you wish to know how its opening is made with iron.
Therefore it is necessary to consider. Then if the pain of the abscess in its beginning is acute with its percussion, and inflammation, and fever; and you see it reddened; if the sensation falls upon it, then do not hasten with its perforation; and treat it with that which helps with maturation, until when the acuteness of the symptoms subsides.
Then it is necessary that you make the woman sit on a seat that lowers two seats, and lean back, or be supported on her back, and bring her feet to the lower part of her belly; and let her thighs be spread, and let her place her arms under her knees, and tie each with a suitable bandage to it.
Then let the midwife sit on the right side, and administer the instrument with which the opening of the womb is opened.
And it is necessary when you wish to administer this instrument, that you fix with the Radio the depth of the woman's womb: so that that which enters from the instrument be not more than the depth of the womb, and injure the weak one.
However, if the instrument is more than the depth, then it is necessary that you place cushions on the covering of the depth of the womb: so that the entry of the entire instrument into the depth of the womb is prevented; and it is necessary that it place the whirling, which runs on the instrument, from the upper side; and that the minister hold the instrument, and that he revolve the whirling until the depth of the womb is opened.
When therefore the exit appears, and is touched by the hand, and is smooth and soft, then it is necessary that you separate it at its head with a broad spatula.
When therefore the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary that you place on the wound a lint soaked in rose oil, or green oil, in which there is some astringency; and place the lint, and withdraw the section into the depth of the womb.
And place externally on the womb, and on the pecten, clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
Then it should be treated after two days with an ointment suitable for it, until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and wound are washed, so that it is cleansed with water and honey; then with water in which the root of lily or long aristolochia has been boiled; then return to the ointment.
If the wound is hidden internally in the womb, then it is necessary that you refrain from treating it with iron, rather, treat it with what we said in the division.
Chapter LXX.
Instructions for Midwives, on how to treat living embryos that have emerged in a non-natural form.
It is necessary for the midwife to first know the form of a natural birth.
Therefore, the signs of it are that when you see a woman pressing downward, and she desires to smell the air, and she is relieved of what is above her in childbirth, and the embryo's exit is hastened: then know that this birth is according to the natural form, and its exit is upon her head, and the afterbirth with it: or hanging, or hanging from the navel.
Therefore, when you see these signs, then it is necessary that her belly be compressed, so that the embryo descends quickly.
For when it descends upon her head, the afterbirth descends with it, and she is cleansed of those superfluous things by a good cleansing.
But whatever is beyond this birth is unnatural, blameworthy.
For sometimes the fetus emerges on its feet, and its body emerges before its head, and its feet and hands together, or one foot; or its head and hands emerge; or its belly emerges; and perhaps it turns upon its neck, and similar forms that are blameworthy.
Therefore, the midwife must be prudent, subtle in all these matters, and beware of error and deception; and I am showing every form from among them, and how the nature is in it, and what is signified by it, and stand upon it.
When the fetus emerges on its head in its natural emergence, and it becomes violent upon the woman, and the birth becomes difficult for her, and you see her strength already weakened, then make her sit on a seat, or let women hold her; and vaporize her vulva with decocted fenugreek and with moist oils.
Then let the midwife insert a small spatula between her fingers, and split the afterbirth with it, and cut it with her fingernail until that which is in it from moisture runs out; and press the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If, however, it does not descend, then the woman must be given an enema of fenugreek mucilage, with sesame oil.
Then, after the enema, instruct her to compress herself; and make her sneeze with a condiment, and hold her mouth and nose for one hour. For the fetus will emerge immediately.
If two hands of the fetus emerge first, you must gently and gradually return them. If, however, it does not yield at the entrance, then place the woman on a seat, and elevate her feet upward; then shake the seat on the ground; and the woman must be held so that she does not fall during the shaking.
If, therefore, the hands do not emerge, and the fetus dies, then cut them, and remove the remainder of the fetus; and tie its hand with a cloth, and extract it with equality; for it will emerge.
The exit of the embryo is on its feet. When the fetus emerges on its feet, then it is necessary to push them upward; then turn the fetus gradually; afterwards, straighten it.
Then take one of its feet and extend it gradually.
When, therefore, they emerge as far as the hips, then order him to compress it, and make it sneeze with condiments; for it will emerge, if God wills.
And if it does not emerge with what we have described, then reduce the fetus gradually until you place it in its natural form. For then it will emerge with easy exit.
If it is obstructed with everything that we have described, then take from marshmallow mucilage, and sesame oil, and dissolved gum, and pound the whole in a mortar with good pounding; then smear the woman's vagina and the lower part of her belly with it. Then make her sit in warm water until she reaches the hypochondria.
When therefore you see that her lower parts are now warmed, then make a poultice of myrrh, and apply it. When, therefore, the poultice has held for one hour, then make her sit on a seat. Then make her sneeze; and bind her mouth and nose; and press the lower part of her belly with a gentle pressure. For the pus will immediately come out.
The exit of the fetus upon the knees and hands. When it emerges in this manner, then gently insert her hands. Then the woman will lie on her back, and her feet will hang down: and she will lie on the bed, and fold her hands. Then gently press upon her abdomen. Then pull out what is under her feet from the pillows, until it is prepared at the lower part. If the fetus does not emerge, then take her feet and move them with a forceful motion. Then gently press what is above the hypochondria, until the fetus ascends upwards. Then let her insert her hands, and gently straighten the fetus; and instruct the woman to bear down, until the fetus emerges.
The exit of the fetus hanging laterally from the hands. His hand be applied in the reduction. If however he cannot reduce it, then lift the woman, and make her walk.
If truly she cannot walk, then make her lie on the bed, and shake her legs with a violent shaking.
Then administer the liniment that you prepared from the mucilages.
If however she receives it from childbirth, then make her sit upon a seat. Then strive in the reduction of the hand, and adjust the fetus according to its natural form, and make the woman sneeze, and command her to compress herself, until it emerges.
The exit of the fetus is upon her neck, with outstretched hands, and its face is toward her mother's back. The midwife must hold the fetus's hand, then gently turn it around, and adjust it with ease.
When it is adjusted, then place upon her vulva what we have described of oil and mucilages.
Then command her to move gradually. When you have done that, then make her sit on a seat, and command her to lean to her right side. Then command her to compress herself, and make her sneeze with condiments.
The exit of the prepared fetus on the side. When it descends to the orifice of the womb in this manner with the afterbirth, then cleave the afterbirth with your fingernail, until it is evacuated of moisture.
If therefore it hangs over the face of the fetus and its neck, then cut it from the navel, so that it is not retained, and dies.
Then push the fetus inwards until you place it as it should be, and command the woman to compress herself several times, for it will exit with ease.
The exit of twins, or many fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three or four are born. However, that is unusual, and rarely happens.
Five, however, is unnatural, and they do not live at all: and although they multiply, they are contained by a single afterbirth; and they are separated by a membrane, distinct from each other, fastened to the navel of each of them.
And sometimes they emerge like a single fetus emerges by a natural, easy exit; and they emerge by that praiseworthy exit, as we have narrated.
If some of them are difficult at birth, and arrive at the unnatural figures we mentioned, then treat them as we have described: and let the midwife be skillful, and do what she does with ease and deliberation, and devise a method according to any figure, in conjunction with what is prepared for her by the ingenuity leading to salvation.
A reminder of what is formed in the womb from embryos, and are miscarried.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more, up to ten, are formed in the womb; and it has been verified by me that a woman miscarried seven, and another fifteen, all formed.
Which was done by the measurement of the glorious wise man.
Therefore, all this should be in the knowledge of the midwife. For perhaps something similar to that will come to her; therefore, let her oppose it with what is necessary.
Chapter LXXVI.
On the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you treat a dead fetus with what I have said in the division of medication, and it does not emerge, and you are forced to use an operation with your hand, then you should consider.
If, therefore, the woman is of sound constitution, and is not suffering from any of the accidents from which her death is feared: then it is necessary that the woman be laid on a bed, on her back, and her head be inclined downwards, and her legs raised; and the attendant should hold her by two parts, or be tied to the bed, so that her body is not pulled during the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the os uteri with moist oils, with the mucilage of marshmallow, and fenugreek, and linseed.
Then let the midwife anoint her hand with these oils and mucilages; then let her introduce it into the uterus with ease. Then let her seek with it the place in which to fix the hooks from the fetus, and the suitable locations for that.
Therefore consider then, if the fetus is prepared to descend upon its head: then fix the hooks in its eyes, or in its neck, or in its mouth, or in its palate, or under its chin, or in its fork, or in the locations near the ribs, and under the hypochondria.
If however the descent is upon its feet, then in its pubis and its middle ribs.
And it is necessary that she hold the hook with her right hand, and place its curve in that which is between the fingers of her left hand; and introduce it with the hand with ease, and fix the hook in any of those places which we said, until it reaches the empty thing.
Then let her fix in opposition to it another hook, or a third, if necessary: so that there is equal contraction and it does not decline to a side.
Then let it be extended with equal extension, and let not the extension be only according to straightness, but rather let it be moved to every part, so that its exit may be alleviated; just as it is done with a tooth during its extraction.
And it is necessary in that which is between them, that the extension be softened. If a part of it is retained, then it is necessary that the midwife anoint some fingers with oil, and introduce them from the side, so that she may turn with them what is retained.
If, therefore, a part of the fetus emerges, then the hooks are to be shifted to another place which is higher: do this until the entire fetus emerges.
If, therefore, the hand emerges before the other parts, and it is not possible to return it, because of its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it. Then pull it out until when the whole emerges, cut it either at the ankle joint, or at the elbow: and so it is necessary that you do for the other hand, and for the two feet.
If, however, the fetus's head is large, or constriction occurs in its exit, or water has accumulated in its head: then it is necessary that the midwife insert thorny spatulas between her fingers, and split the head with it, and extract the water; or break it with the instrument called Almisdac, whose form comes with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter that follows this one.
Similarly, she should do this if the fetus is of a large-headed nature, then it is necessary that she sever the head; or extract it with a misdach, as we have said. Then pull out the bones with forceps.
If the head emerges and is constricted at the fork, then it should be split until the moisture in the chest is discharged.
If, however, it is not complicated, nor yields to exit, then cut the head into fragments, according to what is possible for you.
If the lower belly has an abscess or contains dropsy, then it is necessary to pierce it until moisture runs from it.
If the fetus is on its feet, then its extraction is easy, and its direction towards the mouth of the womb is gentle.
If, however, it is constricted at the belly or chest, then it is necessary to extract it with a cloth that you have wrapped around your hand, and to split the belly or chest until what is within them flows out.
If the remaining limbs are removed, and the head returns, then the midwife should insert her left hand. If, therefore, the mouth of the womb is open, then she should insert her hand deep into the womb, and seek the head with it, and pull it with her fingers until the mouth of the womb; then she should insert a single hook, or two hooks, into it, and pull it out with it.
If, however, the mouth of the womb is constricted, due to a hot abscess that has occurred there, then it is not necessary to work on it, rather it is necessary to administer the effusion of moist, fatty things, and apply plasters, and sit in waters that soften and moisten.
If the fetus is on its side, then it is possible to make it equal.  Then administer what we have said in the case of a living fetus.
If this is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces and removed.
And it is necessary that you leave nothing of the afterbirth inside at all.
When you have completed your operation, then administer what we have said about hot abscesses that occur in the wombs.
If a flow of blood occurs, then it should descend into astringent waters, and administer the rest of the treatment.
And indeed I have already seen a woman, in whose womb a dead fetus was: then she conceived again above it, afterwards another fetus died also. Therefore, after a long time, an abscess occurred in her navel, and it swelled until it opened: and it began to discharge pus.
Therefore, I was called to treat her, and I medicated her for a long time: and the wound did not heal.
Therefore, I placed upon it some strong-attracting plasters: and the bone emerged from the place. Then several days passed, and another bone emerged. Therefore, I was amazed by this, since there is no bone in the belly.
Therefore, I thought that they were from the bones of a dead fetus.
Then I investigated the wound, and extracted many bones, the woman being in her best condition: and she thus lived for a long time, and emitted a little pus from the place.
And I did not establish this as extraneous, unless because of the knowledge and assistance contained in it, which the skillful physician provides with a hand in the treatment.
Chapter LXXVII.
On the shapes of necessary instruments in the extraction of the fetus.
The form of the screw with which the socket opens.
Here below is the form of a book-binder's press, to which two similar screws are attached at the two ends of two pieces of wood. However, this screw must be finer than the press; and to make it, either ebony or boxwood is suitable.
And the width of each piece of wood should be about two fingers; and the edge of each of the two is about one finger; and the length of each of them is a palm and a half; and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two additions of the same kind of wood, which are already fixed to both, whose length is half a palm and a little more; and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the socket, so that it opens with them when you turn the two screws.
Another form similar to it, finer, and lighter.
Let it be made of ebony wood, or boxwood, according to the likeness of forceps. However, at its tip there are two additions, as you see here above: and the length of each addition from them is like a palm: and the width of each is two fingers.
Therefore, when you wish to open the womb with it, then make the woman sit on the bed, her feet hanging down, opening that which is between her thighs. Then introduce these two joined additions into the womb: and you are holding the lower end of the instrument between her hips. Then open your hand with the instrument, as you do with scissors, equally according to the amount you wish to open the mouth of the womb, until the midwife achieves what she wants.
The form of another dizziness, which the ancients called the form of the impelling force by which the fetus is propelled.
The Almisdac form, by which the large head is crushed: and it is likened to a pair of tongs, having teeth at the end, as you see.
And sometimes it is elongated, like tongs of this form, as you see, which have teeth like saw teeth, with which it is cut and crushed.
The form of a hook having a single spine.
The form of a hook having two spines.
The form of a spatula with two ends for severing the head.
The form of two spatulas, even wider ones.
All these instruments are used, and the more their types are multiplied and prepared by the craftsman, the quicker is his operation and the higher his reputation among men, on account of their quantity.
Therefore, do not disregard any instrument, so that it may be prepared for you. For their necessity is undeniable.
Chapter LXXVIII.
Concerning the extraction of the Secundina.
When the Secundina is retained during childbirth, then it is necessary to instruct the woman to hold her breath. Then make her sneeze with incantations, and grasp your hand over her mouth and nostrils. If therefore it emerges through this, it is good.
If not, then take a pot and pierce a hole in the lid. Put in it herbs which open the mother's womb, such as calamint, rue, dill, chamomile, seham, cassia wood, and centaury. Take all these herbs, or some of them, and immerse them in water, and place them on the fire.
Then place a reed tube over the hole in the pot's lid, and the other end in the mother's womb. And let her hold it until the vapor reaches the concavity of the womb.
Then make her sneeze, as we said. For the afterbirth will emerge with ease.
If however, after this treatment, it remains and does not emerge, then instruct the midwife to immerse her hand in sesame oil, or marshmallow mucilage. Then let her insert it into the vagina and search for the afterbirth with it. When therefore she finds it, then let her take it and extend it gradually.
If however it is attached deep within the uterus, then let her insert her hand, as we have described, until when she finds the afterbirth, and extract it gradually in a straight line, so that the uterus does not collapse with violent traction; rather, it should be moved easily to the right and left; then, add the amount of traction. For then it obeys, and is preserved, and escapes from adhesion.
If the os uteri is constricted, as we have already described: treat it with sneezing, and with a medication from a pot with herbs.
If however it does not emerge with all that we have described, then beware of tiring yourself out repeatedly trying to extract it.
But it is necessary that whatever emerges from the uterus through the os uteri, be tied to the woman's hip. Then administer an enema to her with the tetrapharmacon ointment. For it will indeed cause it to putrefy after a few days, and it will dissolve and come out.
However, when it putrefies, a foul odor rises from it to the stomach and head, and harms the weak one. Therefore, appropriate fumigations should be administered to it.
Some of the ancients, however, have already experienced the fumigation of nasturtium and dried figs.
The shape of the instrument with which to fumigate a woman in retention of the Secundinae and menses.
Let it be made of glass, similar to a flask, or of earthenware; and let its wide end be placed over the fire; and let the fumigation be placed on the hot embers; let her hold it until that fumigation is consumed; and again another.
Chapter LXXIX.
Concerning the treatment of an unperforated anus.
Many children are born with their anuses unperforated, which the subtle sifac has already clogged. Therefore, the midwife ought to pierce that sifac with her finger; and if not, she should pierce it with a sharp spatula; and she should beware lest the hand touches it.
Then place upon it wool soaked in wine and oil. Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
If however you fear that it will be clogged, then place a lead cannula in the opening for many days, and it should be removed when the child wishes to evacuate.
And blockage of the anus also occurs from the consolidation of a wound, or from an abscess. Therefore, that consolidation must be cut, then treated with what we have said about treatment and lead.
Chapter Eighty.
Concerning the treatment of fistulas that occur in the lower part.
Fistulas that occur in the lower part are swellings and enlargements that occur near the anus externally, or in a space of one part, and are either a single fistula or multiple.
When that swelling becomes chronic, it opens, and watery white moisture, or thin pus, runs from it.
And sometimes these fistulas are penetrating. Therefore, penetrating ones are recognized by what emerges with bowel movements and flatulence, when the patient uses bowel movements; and perhaps worms emerge from them.
And some of those fistulas, when they are penetrating, extend to the bladder and to the urethra. And some of them penetrate to the hip joint and to the tailbone.
However, one recognizes a fistula penetrating to the anus, even from one that does not penetrate, by inserting your index finger into the anus; and inserting a thin probe into the fistula, made of air or iron: when there is no twisting.
If there is twisting in it, then insert a thin probe of lead, or a hair from a horse's mane, into it until you feel the probe or hair in your finger.
If you do not feel it completely, nor anything comes out of the opening in terms of excretion, nor wind, nor worms as I said: then know that it is not penetrating; therefore hasten to treatment.
And when the fistula penetrates to the bladder or to the urinary meatus: then its sign is the passing of urine through it, and its prohibition from this, so that the place may be consolidated with medicines.
If however it penetrates to the hip joint or to the hip bone: then its sign is that the probe reaches there, if there is no twisting within to the bone; and the finding of pain in the surrounding area, and that pus emerges from it always; and that medicine does not work there, nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas, there is no cure at all; and their treatment is toil and vanity for him, by foolish physicians, upon whom it is formed.
However, in that which is not penetrating, and not ancient, healing is hoped for with a saw, according to what I tell you, and what I have already proved; and it is to lay the sick person on his back between your hands, and lift his legs above his hips, and his hips be inclined towards his belly. Then insert a lead or bronze probe, if there is twisting in the fistula, until you know where the probe reaches.
If however the sick person feels it near the anus, then it is necessary to insert your index finger into the anus.
If therefore you feel the probe already penetrated through itself, detected, besides what you feel between your finger, and between the fistula, or flesh: then you know certainly that it is penetrating; therefore do not work on it, because there is no healing for it, as we said.
They said, however, that in some people the flesh is healed, and that from the medication which is hoped to help it, it is that you cauterize a small cautery according to the width of the fistula, as previously mentioned, and insert it hot into the fistula, until it reaches around the anus. Then repeat it twice, or three times, until you know that all those added, filtered, flesh-like tissues, similar to the quill of a bird's feather, are now burned.
Then treat it with lint wrapped in butter, until those burned tissues come out; then treat it with ointments that generate flesh and consolidate it.
If therefore it is healed, it is good.
And if not, then it will never be healed with any other treatment.
And if the prolapse emerges, and does not reach your finger in the anus, and there is a thick layer of flesh between it and the prolapse, or if such is the case: and you see a fistula in that which follows the surface of the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you proceed cutting along the prolapse that is in the fistula until the incision reaches where the end of the prolapse reaches, and the prolapse is released and falls out.
Then cleanse those filtered tissues, which resemble the hollow of a bird's feather, so that nothing remains of them.
If, however, blood overcomes you and interferes between you and your operation so that you sever those tissues, then it is better, and more swiftly helpful, to cauterize it with fire or with a sharp medicine.
Because cauterization with fire brings about good habits, burning those added tissues, and cutting off the blood, and drying the moisture.
Then treat it with linen strips soaked in butter, or crushed herbs, until the place emits pus, and those burned tissues emerge in the pus.
Then treat the wound with consolidating ointments, which produce hard flesh: and such medicines possess astringency and drying properties.
For when it is restored, then it is already healed: and you do not fear a recurrence.
If, however, the fistula has already reached the depth of the anus, and extends from bodily superfluity: then insert your finger into the anus, and investigate with it.
If therefore you feel a tension, and between you and it there is a membrane of siphac or flesh, and it is close to penetration: then there is no operation on it, except according to the path of trust and hope.
For it is treated in one of three ways: either to cauterize it, as we said; or to cut it away until you reach its concavity, so that it is possible for you to insert ligatures, and a cure from nearby. And do not proceed much with the cutting, so that you do not sever the muscle containing the anus, and bring about an involuntary expulsion of faeces upon the sick person. Then treat it with what we said.
Perhaps it will be healed; or you will stretch out the membrane and cleanse it after cutting it away to near the anus, as we said, either with a tenaculum or with another instrument of sharp extremity. Then cleanse those filtered flesh which are in the incision, until where you can with inspection.
Then attempt the restoration of the entire wound with the incision, and the upper opening where it consolidates; and a fistula will remain open in the inner part of the anus, and it will be less burdensome upon the sick person.
And sometimes the fistula is sutured in this manner: and this is when you introduce a tenaculum into the fistula; and it is on the side of the anus, near the surface of the body with the skin and margin of the anus; then take a tenaculum with a perforated extremity, which is like an aleskef needle, that is, for preparing hides, according to this form. And insert into it a thread wrapped with five threads, and about it. Then insert the tenaculum with the thread into the fistula, until it reaches its concavity.
If therefore it penetrates into the inner margin of the anus nearby, then pull the thread out of that opening: so that you insert your finger into the anus, and pull out the end of the thread, and join the two ends together; and tighten each, and leave it for one, or two days; and the more the thread penetrates into the flesh, tighten it well, until those fleshy parts between the two ends of the thread are cut and fall away; then treat the wound until it consolidates and heals.
If however the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate in whichever way is prepared for you, unless it is very deep. For you do not have the ability to do that on account of the arm, lest you harm it.
Then do with it as we said, until it is healed.
Shape the sharp spatula with which the fistula is split, whose curve is very sharp, and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not harm what is unnecessary, as you see.
Chapter LXXXI.
On the suturing of hemorrhoids, from which blood flows from their incision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two kinds. For they are either within the anus, like sacs of coal or plums, or like bunches of grapes themselves. And from them are small ones and large ones, and blood flows from them always, and there is one, and there are many. And some are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are mostly few in moisture, from which yellow water, or a little blood runs with constant flow, and they are according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is within the anus is to instruct the sick person to sit: and to express it, until it emerges from the anus, and the athers appear to you. Therefore, also suspend them with hooks, or hold them with your nail. Then cut them at their roots.
However, if the hooks do not hold on them, because of their moisture and their softening: then take them with a rough cloth, or with a piece of doubled woolen cloth, and pull them out with your fingers. Then cut them, and after the incision sprinkle some hot powders upon them, so that they will act as a cautery in their place. Or cauterize them according to what was said previously in the chapter on cauterization. Then treat them with the rest of the treatment, until they are healed.
If the anus does not yield to the expulsion, then inject the sick person with an enema containing a small amount of irritant, so that it washes out what is in the anus, and it will quickly yield to expulsion when the patient strains.
If, however, the piles are external to the anus, then their removal is easy and simple: you may grasp them with your fingernail or a forceps, and cut them off. Then treat them with what we have said, until they are healed.
And whoever recoils from incision with iron, then it is necessary that you apply a suture to them according to this method. Take a wrapped thread, and insert it into the needle. Then pull the pile upward, and make it pierce with the needle at its root on the other side; and wrap the two ends of the thread, at the lower part of the needle, and let them be latitudinal; and tighten the pile with a strong constriction. Then tie the thread, and remove the needle.
Do this with all the piles, and leave one of them; and do not compress it, lest blood run from it.
Then place on the anus a cloth soaked in rose or cedar oil, and place it over the anus; and command the sick person to rest. Then leave it until it falls.
When therefore the piles fall, treat them with ointments and other remedies until they are healed.
Concerning the treatment of Fissures. For they often occur from the drying up of excrement and the constriction of nature.
When therefore they become chronic and medicine is of no avail, then it is necessary to scrape them with a pointed spatula, or with your fingernail, until they become moist, and the upper crust that prevents their healing is removed. Then treat them until they heal properly.
If however they do not heal, then scrape them again with a more forceful blade than the first, until they bleed profusely and become raw; then treat them until they are healed.
On the treatment of inverted and non-inverted Almirmar, and of dry Athelil and Formicae.
Chapter LXXXII.
Know that Almirmar is nothing but a round swelling the color of the body, similar to the head of a nail; and it forms throughout the body, especially in the lower parts of the feet and toes; and it causes pain during walking.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut what is around the nail, and hold it with shears or a hook, and remove it from its root.
Then treat it; and if you wish, cauterize it according to what has been said in its chapter.
From dry athelil, however, one is a small, rough, filtered, round elevation above the surface of the body. And they often occur on the hands of children.
And its treatment is, that you stretch the athelil, and cut it; or bind it with a silk thread; or with a hair, until it falls. And if you wish, cauterize it according to what has been said with fire, or with a sharp medicine; and now inverted athelil are eradicated, and not inverted with the instrument that I will describe.
A formica is also a small, filtered, thick elevation on the surface of the body, going deep into it: and when it encounters cold, a sensation similar to an ant's bite is felt in it, and occurs throughout the body; and most of it occurs in the hands.
And its treatment is, that you take a cannula from a goose's quill, or a strong vulture's quill; and place that cannula over the Formica, until the circle of the cannula encompasses its parts. Then rotate your hand with the cannula, until you sever that which is around the Formica; and you place it deep into the flesh, and eradicate the Formica from its root.
And if you wish, do it with a cannula of bronze or iron, according to this form.
Let the upper part of the cannula be at a height drawing the enclosed dumb, so that the grasping and turning of it and itself might be relieved.
And if you wish, cauterize the Formica according to what is previously stated in the cauterization of Athelil in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter LXXXIII.
Concerning the forms of instruments that are administered for enemas in diseases of the anus, and for loosening the bowels, and for colic.
Sometimes an enema is made from silver, or from bronze, or from cast or hammered copper.
And sometimes it is made from these instruments small and large, according to those in which they are administered.
Therefore, that which is administered in the treatment of very young children should be small; and those whose anuses are weak or painful, so too their enemas.
Large enema.
Its tube, that is, the upper end where the bag is tied, should be wide according to this manner; and let it have a division so that the bag can be tied as you see, and its lower end which is introduced into the anus should be closed, that is, not open, smooth, very slender, drawn to thinness; and on one side of it let there be two openings, and on the other side one opening.
And let the width of the opening be according to the thickness of the rays, and a little thicker; and let the bag in which the medicines are placed be of animal bladder, or of parchment, which should be in the form of a small asofrat; whose root should be one palm.
And let it be pierced with many holes, as it is rolled, and let the space between each hole be the thickness of a finger. Then insert strong thread into those holes, made from ten threads, or thereabouts, and tie the bag to it, like an asofrat. When therefore the bag is placed in it, it should be tied at the head of the clyster between the two separating constrictions with a strong tightening. Then clysterize with it.
And this is the shape of the bags here depicted, and the holes.
However, the quality of clysterizing with it is: to fill the bag, or bladder, with medicine, which should be warmed according to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is not too hot, or too cold.
Then the patient should lie on their back, and raise their legs upwards; and place under them a 'inthaan', that is, a hide upon which people lie, or a linen cloth.
Then anoint the tip of the clyster with oil, or with egg white, or with the mucilage of fenugreek, or similar things. Then introduce it into the anus with ease. Then let the attendant grasp the bag with both hands together, and squeeze it forcefully, until all that is in the clyster is emptied into the intestine.
Then remove the clyster, and let the patient raise their legs towards the wall.
If the medicine compels him towards death, then let him hold on as long as possible. And if he sleeps with it all night, it is more useful in its assistance.
If, however, you administer clysters for diseases of loose bowels, and his death is imminent, then administer the clyster again, until he is healed.
If there are hemorrhoids or ulcerating abscesses in the anus, then let the clyster be very thin, mild, from the clysters with which children are clysterized.
And this be the form of the thin clyster above which is tight skin.
Chapter LXXXIV.
I have already said in the division on the treatment of wounds that it was fitting for the book, and I will here say the treatment of them with the hand and with medicines according to the complement.
Therefore, I say that wounds are diversified according to that by which wounds are made, and according to the place upon which the wound falls.
The things, therefore, by which wounds are frequently made are like the throwing of a stone; or the incision of a sword or knife; or a blow with a lance, or wood, or arrow; and similar things from many objects.
And the wounds that are made according to the places of the body are, like a wound that falls upon the head, or neck, or chest, or belly, or liver; and similar ones from those limbs.
And I am narrating the treatments of some wounds, so that I may set forth that reasoning and rule concerning the remaining wounds.
And I will begin with simple wounds of the head, since the recounting of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
I say therefore. When a simple wound occurs on the head, and there is no fracture of the bone, then consider if it is from a thrown stone, or similar things, and has merely broken the skin; and is a large wound, and you fear a bad outcome from a hot abscess: then hasten, and immediately perform a cephalic phlebotomy; and do not delay it; and draw from his blood according to the measure of his strength; if nothing is preventing phlebotomy, especially if the blood issuing from the wound is small in quantity. And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded part. And let the sick person beware of fullness, and wine, and heavy foods.
If, however, anything prevents lessening, then lessen from food, and let him not approach meats, nor wines.
And if a hot abscess occurs on the wound, place on it a poultice steeped in rose oil alone, or with wine in which there is astringency.
If, however, you are certain that it is free from a hot abscess, then place on the wound, if it is recent with its own blood, and the air has not altered it, the powder which I will describe shortly: and if the air has already altered it, then place certain ointments upon it, until it is healed.
If, however, the wound is large, or is from a sword cut, and similar things: and its edges do not come together by means of pads, then bring them together with sutures, according to what I will describe concerning the sutures of abdominal wounds.
If, however, the wound is already exposed to the bone, and hangs, and its retention is not possible except in a small support: then cut it. Then treat the wound with remedies, and restore hard flesh in place of the skin, and prescribe for the sick person that they be nourished with foods containing bulk, such as heads of grain and cooked wheat, until it is like porridge, and similar things.
If you find putrefaction in the skin, and it does not adhere to the bone, then cut away the entire putrefied area and discard it; then treat it.
If, however, in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood flows: and it is not stopped by medicines, then investigate the artery. If therefore you find it not severed, then sever it with a spatula, or tie it. If necessity compels it when that which we have said is not helpful, then cauterize it until the blood is stopped.
If, however, these wounds are small, simple, then their treatment is easy, slight; so that you pulverize this powder over them, because it consolidates them before the air changes them.
Method of the flesh-forming powder. Take one part of frankincense, and two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of quicklime: grind the whole thoroughly, and sift it: and fill the wound with it, and bind it with a good bandage until it adheres well to it, and clots with the blood.
Then bind it over with bandages, and leave it, and do not loosen it while it remains so that it does not fester. For medicine is not removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes quicklime alone does this, when you do not have frankincense and dragon's blood present, and the latter does the same operation in many large wounds, when it is appropriate to place it on them and bind them, and are fresh wounds with their blood, which the air has not altered.
If, however, the air has already altered them by some change, especially when it is summer, then the intention of applying this powder is not appropriate: therefore treat it with what we have said, so that you place certain digestive ointments on it; or place on it poultices of barley flour, made with water and honey, until they bring forth pus. Then treat them with the remaining treatment, until they are healed.
If, however, a fracture occurs with the wound in the bone, and it is small, then extract it with a forceps: and I will now state the treatment of fractures of the head in what follows.
Of wounds of the neck.
If, however, a wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference between it in treatment, and a wound of the head when it is simple.
If, however, the nerve of the neck, or artery, has already been cut: then if it is a nerve, there is no skill in it, except that its treatment be avoided with anything that causes intense astringency, such as ziniar, and zegi, and similar things. Because they harm the nerves, and contract them.
And do not put anything cold on the wound at all: because the substance of the nerves is cold, and its continuation is with the brain, which is higher than the limbs: and let that which is used for treatment be from medicines mild, such as lime washed with fresh water, infused sometimes with oil, or with rose oil, and tutty, when it is also washed with fresh water: and the rest of the mineral stones, in this manner, and moist ointments.
If however the wound is large, then administer a suture: or constrict its lips with poultices. Then treat it until it is healed.
If however the wound has depth, or there occurs a sinus in its lower part, in which pus is already collected, then pierce it in the more depressed place in it.
If indeed an artery has been cut in the wound, and its blood flow has ensued: then separate, or cut it, and tie it: or cauterize it, if necessity provokes it to that.
If however the windpipe's cartilages have been cut, or entirely, and the surrounding tissues are safe: then bring together the skin's edges with a suture over the windpipe's cartilage, and do not touch the windpipe, rather make it even and restore it to its natural shape. Then bind it with a suitable constriction, and leave it for a few days. Then treat it with ointments that possess astringency and desiccation, until it is healed.
If however the wound has already made an impression on the bone, then investigate it: then if there are fragments of bone within it, extract all of them, or as many as you can: and what cannot be removed, leave it until the wound rots, and its exit is separated and alleviated.
If the wound is recent, then powder it with the powder I have described. If however bone is seen in the wound, and a fistula is formed, then treat it with what is mentioned in its chapter.
Of a wound in the shoulder blades.
If there is a blow from a lance or a knife, and you see its depth, then consider. If therefore wind issues from the wound, when the sick person gasps from it, then know that the wound is mortal.
If it does not have depth, and is fresh with its own blood: then do not place powder in it from the first hour, nor bind it, so that blood is not retained in its depth and returns to the heart and kills the sick person; but put attracting ointment on it, and loosen it twice a day.
If, however, ointment is not present for you, then place an old cloth in the opening of the wound, so that it sucks what emerges from it, from the moisture; and make the sick person sleep on the wound, so that what gathers in it runs out.
If, however, three days or more have already passed since the wound, and no spasm or bad tremor of the heart or constriction in breathing occurs to the sick person, and you see the rest of their condition good, then know that the wound is safe. Therefore, treat it with bandages and other remedies until it is healed.
If, however, its healing is excused, and it constantly opens up, then know that it has already become a fistula. Therefore treat it according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from a sword or knife incision, and is simple on the surface of the chest or back, then treat it with the preceding sutures, if it is large; or with powder, if it is small.
If, however, it has already made an impression on the bone, and chipped fragments from it: then examine the wound, and hasten to remove those fragments, if they are separated. If, however, they are not separated, then leave them until the wound is suppurated, for the extraction of them will be easier.
However, for other accidental wounds in the other limbs, the judgment in their treatment is the judgment we have stated.
Concerning wounds of the abdomen, and the exit of the intestines, and their expulsion.
A rupture occurring in the abdomen is great, middle, and small.
From a great one therefore sometimes the intestine, or a number of intestines, emerges.
Therefore, their introduction, and their stitching is more forceful and difficult.
And a small rupture also becomes difficult in another way. Therefore, it follows that their reduction is more difficult if you do not hasten their introduction to their place immediately, since it is inflated, opened, and enlarged; therefore, its entry becomes difficult.
Because of this therefore, a better rupture of the middle disruptions occurs, since with it the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is difficult in these two kinds.
Know that the suturing of the belly is according to four methods.
Two common sutures agree in the suturing of the belly, and in the suturing of the remaining wounds of the body; and two other sutures are properly shared with the other wounds. However, they are more proper with the suturing of the belly; and I narrate each one to you with exposition and declaration.
I say therefore, that when the wound is small, and something from the intestine emerges from it, and it becomes difficult to reduce it, then that is due to one of two methods. Either because of the smallness of the rupture, as we said: or because there is inflation in the intestine due to cold air.
When therefore this is so, then it is necessary to warm it, so that you immerse a sponge, or a damp cloth, in just lukewarm water. Or boil it in water with squinant, and cyperus, and spike, and embrocate the intestine with it, until the inflation is resolved.
Sometimes wine in which there is astringency does it; and it is better than just water in resolving the inflation.
When therefore you see the inflation already resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which mallow or marsh mallow has been boiled. For by it the entrance of it is alleviated with small thing.
If however its return is excused after this treatment, then make a small incision in the rupture with the instrument with which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first treatment is better when prepared than the incision: and there is no forcing to cut after necessity and instrument failure.
Let one part of it be twisted, sharp, and the other part not sharp, and the slender extremity not be with the slenderness of a spatula, but rather somewhat blunter: and it is that which resembles a caul, as you see.
When therefore the wound is enlarged, and the intestine enters, then it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural form to its own proper place, if possible over it; because it is better.
When, however, the rupture is large in the lower part of the belly, then it is necessary that you make the sick person lie on his back, and raise his legs higher than his head. And if it is in the upper part of the belly, then place his head and chest higher than his lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two parts of the belly, then place your intent and your consideration always in this, that the part in which the wound is be higher than the other part.
And thus it is necessary that you administer it in large wounds, and in medium-sized wounds.
In small wounds, however, place it according to what is prepared for you.
Then have a pious attendant present between your hands, who shall hold the entire rupture with his hand, and bring together its lips. Then, uncover from it to him who follows in the stitching something after something: and this is the common manner of one stitching.
And it is that you take a needle, or a number of needles according to the quantity of the wound's width. Then, release from the extremity of the rupture a quantity equal to the ring finger, that is, of the physician's: and fasten one needle beyond that, into which you introduce thread at both ends of the skin, with both ends of the thread being beneath the skin from within, until you make it penetrate from the other side, and you have now brought together the two ends of the skin, and the two ends of the thread: and four stitches are made.
Then tighten with the doubled thread in the circumference of the needle several times from both sides together, until the two lips of the wound are brought together with a fitting closure.
Then release the thickness of a finger also, and fasten another needle in them: afterwards, repeat it with the thread, as you did with the first needle. Therefore do not cease to do that with whatever needles you need, until you complete the closure of the entire wound.
And let the needles be of medium thickness between thick and thin: because very thin needles pierce the flesh very quickly, and thick ones are difficult to penetrate the skin; therefore, they should be of medium thickness and thinness.
And it is not fitting that the needle be fixed in the two close ends of the skin too much, so that the flesh is not pierced quickly and the wound is opened before its consolidation, and also that it is not drawn too far with the suture, so that the wound is not prevented from consolidating.
Then cut the ends of the needles, so that they do not harm the patient during his sleep, and place cushions of cloth on every side to hold the ends of the needles, and leave them until you know that the wound has already healed.
And this type of suture with needles is indeed more suitable for small wounds. Because one, or two, or about that many needles suffice in suturing them.
However, the second common suture is to join together the four ends: two ends of the skin and two ends of the si[...?] in one turn, with a needle in which a thread is wrapped equal in fineness and thickness.
Then, when you have pierced these four ends with the needle, return the needle from the side from which you began, so that the drawn thread falls from the upper part of the wound, so that the suture is like the suture of a cloth in which cloths are tied, and put between each suture a quantity less than the thickness of a little finger.
However, this suture is known to men.
And with this suture I treated a wound that had occurred to a man in his abdomen, who had been wounded with a knife, and the wound rupture was greater than one palm: and about two palms of the middle intestine had come out, and there was a rupture in the middle of the abdomen.
Therefore I returned it after his intestine had remained outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
Therefore the wound was consolidated in a space of about fifteen days: and I treated him, and he was healed; and he lived after that for many years, continuing in his pursuits.
And the physicians had judged that it would not be healed.
And it was miraculous that I did not treat it with ointment. Because I was in a place where no ointment, nor any medicine, could be found. Therefore I placed an old linen cloth over the wound twice a day, and diligently washed it with honey water, until it was healed.
I will however describe two proper kinds of suture, according to the account of the text.
And it is, that you begin with a sharp suture: and you insert the needle from the outer to the inner part.
When therefore the needle penetrates the skin, and going into the muscle along the length and breadth of the whole body: leave the end thus in the side in which you inserted the needle, and make it penetrate the other end from the inner part to the outer at the other end.
When therefore you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate according to this in the same end thus from the outer part to the inner: and leave the end thus, which is in this side, and make the needle penetrate the other end from the inner part to the outer: and make it penetrate with your penetration, as you make it penetrate in the thus end, which is on its side, until it penetrates into the whole of it.
Then begin also from this same side, and sew it with the end, which is from thus on the other side, and withdraw the needle from the skin which it has penetrated.
Then return it into that skin, and sew the end thus, which is on the other side, with this end: and draw it from the skin which is on its side, and do that once, and again: and do it alternately, until you have sewn the entire exterior according to that example.
And the sum of this suture is that it should suture the skin as leatherworkers sew skins: so that you sew it alternately from one side, from one end of the skin; and that you leave it alternately from the other side, until you complete it.
The second species of proper suture, which Galen said.
His speech recounts that one should sew it according to the likeness in which certain physicians sew them, so that they gather every part to its suitable counterpart by nature: and they apply the thus end to its other end, and the other end to its other end. And that is done according to what I tell you.
You should place the needle into the nearby end, on the outside, and cause it to penetrate to the inside in that sole; and leave the two ends out. Then, turn the needle, and cause it to penetrate from the outside to the inside in the two ends of sifac, that is, on both sides.
Then turn both, also causing both to penetrate from the inside to the outside, into the two other ends of mirach, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suturing is better than common, easy suturing, and is a suturing whose remembrance preceded.
This is therefore also Galen's speech.
And some said. The suturing of the belly is rectified with two needles. That is, introduce one thread in each of them; and begin with the introduction of the needle, from where it is with you, and make it penetrate to the other side, according to the suturing of those who stitch hides equally.
And know that when there is a rupture in the middle of the belly, then its suturing is more difficult than that of the other parts of the belly.
The judgment of the treatment of a wound is the judgment of other wounds.
That is, when you obtain a fresh wound with its own blood, before the air alters it, and you reduce the intestine, and you suture it, and you adapt it, then place it over the incarnating powder.
If the air has already altered the wound, then place upon it some ointments which are present to you, until they cause pus; and the threads fall out, and the wound consolidates, and mirach. Then treat it as is the treatment of other wounds, until it is healed.
However, if you do not have the medicines present, then place immediately upon it, from the moment it begins to emit pus, old cotton: and change it twice a day, as I taught you, until it is healed.
For you do not need another medicine in many dispositions, when wounds are simple.
However, if you fear that the wound communicates with the principal limbs in the injury, then it is necessary that you immerse soft wool in oil of tempered warmth, or in rose oil, and place it around the places that are between the groin and under the armpits.
If, however, he feels pain, or prickings, or putrefaction in his intestines (for this often happens), then administer an enema to him with astringent, black, lukewarm wine; especially if the putrefaction has already reached the intestine and become a penetrating exit right to its cavity.
And know that that which is from the large intestine is of easier healing; and that which is from those of the small intestine, of more difficult healing.
However, the intestine called the Jejunum does not receive healing from a wound that happens in it at all; and this is because of its multitude of veins, and their size, and because of the subtlety of its body, and the proximity of its nature to the nerves.
If, however, that which proceeds from the wound is a zirbum, and you obtain it fresh, reduce it according to your reduction, by which you reduce the intestines equally.
If, however, a space has passed it and it has already become green or black, then it is necessary to tie it with thread above the place that has blackened from it, so that a flow of blood does not occur. For in a zirbum are veins and arteries.
Then cut what is beyond that ligature, and place the two ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside of it, so that the traction of it may be alleviated for you, and its extraction, in the event of a zirbum, and the emission of the wound's pus.
A recollection of the wound that occurred in the Intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the Intestine, and it is small, then it is possible that it may be restored in some people.  Therefore, because I have seen someone who was wounded in his belly with a spear thrust, and there was a wound on the right side of the stomach, and it was an old wound, and it became a fistula, and waste and wind came out of it.
Therefore, I applied treatment until I was not confident in its healing; nor did I cease to refine it until he was healed, and the place was consolidated.
After I saw the place already consolidated, I feared for the sick person, that a harmful accident might befall him in his belly; and no harmful accident befell him from that. And he remained in better dispositions, eating and drinking, and copulating, and entered the bath, and was exercised in his daily routines.
And now some experimenters have said that when a wound occurs in the Intestine, and it is small, then it should be bound in this manner. And it is so that you take ants of large heads. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, and place one ant of those having an open mouth upon the two lips of the wound. When therefore it has seized upon it, and contracted its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres, and does not loosen.
Then place another ant near the first, and do not cease to do that with ant after ant according to the quantity of the whole wound.
Then reduce it, and sew the wound. For those heads remain attached to the intestine until the intestine is nourished and healed, and no harm comes to the sick person at all.
And now it is possible that the intestine be tied even with a fine thread: which is cleaned from the animal's intestine, attached to it after it is introduced into the needle.
And it is to take the extremity of this thread from the intestine, and it is to be greatly scraped. Then tie a fine linen thread wrapped around its extremity. Then introduce that thread into the needle, and sew the intestine with it. Then return it to the rupture.
And this method of sewing with ants and intestine is only according to the way of trust and hope.
