Know that a wound that is large, wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines: then there is no remedy but subtlety, nor is its healing complete.
Concerning the treatment of Zuchemen and Fistulas.
Know that any wound, or abscess, when it grows old and becomes an ulcer, and does not heal, and continually discharges pus, and does not close: then it is universally called a fistula, wherever it may be on a limb, and we call it Zuchemen.
And a fistula, in truth, is a filtered, hard, white swelling: with which there is no pain, which has the concavity of a bird's feather: and for that reason some call it Risati, that is, feather.
And in some cases it is moist, emitting pus. And perhaps the moisture is opened in some cases. And sometimes this moisture is plentiful, and sometimes little; and it is thick, and sometimes thin.
And I have already stated in the division, when I spoke of wounds, that every ulcer that does not heal, and flesh does not grow on it, is not so except for one of nine causes.
Of which one is, either because of the scarcity of blood in the body, or because of its bad quality; or because between it and the edges of it there is hard flesh, preventing the growth of flesh; or because there is much filth and dirt; or because the ulcer itself is putrid, and the matter given to it is of bad quality; or because the medicine is unsuitable for its treatment; or because the corruption that falls in the region is a kind of plague, or due to a property in the region, as happens in Caesaraugusta, in which the digestion of illnesses is difficult, and the healing of abscesses by nature is delayed; or because there is one bone, or a number of bones, in it.
And I have already shown all these causes and their signs, and their cures with medicines in the division: and it is necessary here to make you know the cure of Fistula and Zuken, which is done by the way of operation with the hand.
Know therefore, that Fistula occurs in all members of the body. For among them are fistulas that reach large veins and arteries, or nerves, or sinews, or the intestine, or the bladder, or to any of the ribs, or to any of the vertebrae of the back, or to any of the joined joints, such as the hand and foot, and among them are those that go towards the vicinity of the main member and similar ones.
Therefore, whichever of these fistulas is of this kind, is from illnesses difficult to heal, and does not receive cure, and dealing with it is laborious and ignorant.
If, however, there is a fistula whose depth does not reach these places I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its cure, and wish to arrive at knowledge of it, then take a probe of air or iron, if the fistula tends in a straight line, and investigate it with it.
If, however, there is a tortuosity in the fistula, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead. Because lead, with the gentleness of its body, is lightened and curves around the tortuosity.
If the fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to indicate over it with a probe. Therefore, irrigate from its openings.
For the moisture with which you irrigate it, goes towards other openings and flows from them.
Then, inquire meticulously in what way it is possible for you: to know whether there is a bone, or a nerve, and the cavity of the fistula is distant or close; or whether the fistula is one with many openings, and stand over the entire thing with all your strength; with your inquiry, with which you question the sick person, and the discovery of pain when you press with your hand on the place, and similar signs and causes of accidents of the abscess.
When therefore you have stood over all that position truly, then proceed to treatment with confidence: and it is as though you consider then, whether the fistula is a proximate apparent one, or in a safe place distant from a joint, or from a nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places which I have told you: then cut the fistula as was previously mentioned in my narration, and remove that which is within it by filtration, and the corrupted flesh, which is born within its opening from flesh, or pustules, and similar things; and treat it until it is healed.
If however the fistula is of distant depth, and is straight: then it is necessary that you cut it in depth as much as is possible for you. Then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh.
Then apply strips wrapped in sharp medicines: and insert them into the cavity of the fistula which you have grasped with the iron; do this with it several times, until the sharp medicine corrodes all that remains in the cavity of the fistula from corruption.
Then restore it with ointments that generate healthy flesh, until it is healed.
If however it is not healed by that, then cauterize it according to what we have described.
If therefore the cause of the fistula is bone, and it is ascertained by you, then search for it, and cut it out according to what I have told you: if nothing prevents you from a vein, or nerve, or principal limb, as we said.
If therefore bone is revealed to you, and there is something of corruption and blackness in it, then scrape it until its entire corruption is removed. Then treat it with that which causes it to flesh over, until it is healed.
If it does not heal, and emits pus as it did: then know that you have not reached the complete eradication of the corruption.
Therefore, expose it again, and thoroughly make a scraping and cleansing of it with all your strength.
Then restore it. If therefore it heals, it is good; and if not, then know that that corruption is in a remote depth of the body, which you have not reached. Therefore, you have no skill in it, except to leave it to nature.
If the small bone is corrupted, and it is possible for you, draw it out with fine forceps suitable for it.
If, however, there are many bones, then carefully extract them all, and do not leave any of them with your strength.
If, however, something from the humors resists you, and there is no fear or error in cutting them: then cut those humors and flesh, if there are any there, and free the bones.
If, however, you are not prepared to remove the bones from your time, then put over the wound something to rot it: and leave it, until what is around those bones rots from flesh and humors.
And guard the wound so that it does not heal. And constrict the section you cut in your operation. Indeed, put over it, if you fear, a poultice steeped in ground sulfur mixed with oil; or a poultice steeped in Egyptian ointment, or some other green ointment.
For when those humors, namely, rot and are separated from the bones, the removal and extraction of them is made easier.
However, if there is one large bone, such as the bone of the leg or the bone of the hip, and similar to them, and that which is corrupted from it is a large part: then scrape it with the utmost scraping until that blackness and corruption are removed. Then restore the wound.
If however that which is corrupted in it is a large part, and the corruption has already reached the bone marrow, then it is necessary to saw it and cut it completely up to the place where the corruption has reached: then treat it until it is consolidated.
And I tell you a story, which happened to a man in his leg: so that you might set it as an example and aid in your operation.
There was a young man, about thirty years of age, in whom pain arose in his leg, from a cause originating within the body, that is, from the internal parts, until the matter reached the leg, and it became a large abscess: and there was no external cause for it.
Therefore, time passed with him and with the error of the physicians, until the abscess was opened, and many matters poured out with it. And his treatment was delayed, until the leg became fistulous, and many openings were made in it, which all emitted pus and bodily humors.
Therefore, a multitude of physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no skillful practitioner of the art among them, until I was called, and I saw his leg, and the matters that flowed from those openings with great force; indeed, the entire body of the man was already decaying, and his complexion was yellowish.
Therefore, I inserted a probe into one of the openings, and the probe reached the bone.
Then I examined all the openings, and found them connecting to each other from all parts of the leg.
Therefore, I worked diligently and cut over one of those openings, until I exposed a portion of the bone; and I found it corrupted; which was already corroded, and blackened, and had rotted and was perforated, until it had penetrated to the marrow.
Therefore I removed what I had exposed, and prepared it from the corrupted bone.
And I judged that there was nothing in the bone except for that corruption, which I cut away and removed. And I had already eradicated it.
Then I applied a restoration to the wound with consolidating medicines for a long time. And it was not consolidated.
Then I tried again, and I found a second area of bone above the first discovery; and I found continuous corruption with the bone, and I cut away what appeared to me to be corruption from that area too.
Then I attempted its restoration. And it was not restored.
Therefore, I again discovered it, and I did not cease cutting the bone piece by piece, and attempting its restoration; and it was not restored until I cut away about a palm's breadth from the bone and pulled it out with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines, and it was quickly consolidated and healed; and this repetition in its operation and section was not necessary, except for the disposition of the patient's weakness, and his little tolerance, and my fear for him of death, since he was experiencing fainting spells in every hour from the excess of evacuations.
Therefore, he was healed with complete healing; and hard flesh grew in the place of the bone, and a good disposition was made in his body, and his strength returned; and he returned to his previous condition; and no harm was caused to him by walking at all.
However, if a protruding bone in the body has already been fractured, then it is necessary to saw it, in this manner: you should take a ligament and tighten it at the end of the protruding bone, and instruct someone to pull it upwards; and place another thicker wool ligament over the first ligament. Then tie it over the flesh that is under the bone; and stretch its end so that you pull the flesh downwards; and you expose the flesh over the area you wish to saw so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
Place wood or a board beneath the bone properly below, because when you do that, the saw is not prevented from cutting any corrupt flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be over the corrupted area for a short time, so that there is no corruption in the cavity of the bone, and it does not appear to the senses in its appearance and necessitates sawing in another way.
If the bone is corrupted and not protruding, but rather its part continues with another part, and the corruption is in its middle or in a part of it, then expose all the flesh from all sides. Then place wood below.
Afterwards, saw it from the first part where the corruption is, until the sawing continues from the other part; and let the sawing be along the length from the corruption for a short time, as we have said.
If the corruption is in the joint, cut the corrupted joint itself, and saw the bone where it continues on the other side.
If, however, the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no remedy for it except abrasion.
If the corruption is in the fingernail of the hand, or in the fingernail of the foot, then the matter is very difficult. But it is necessary that you inquire how the corruption appears to you. And abrade it, and cleanse it according to whatever disposition is possible for you, and with whatever remedy rectifies it for you: when it does not cause you opposition from a vein or a nerve.
And know that there are many knives and saws for cutting these bones, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness and thinness, and their size and smallness, and their hardness and their rareness. Therefore it is necessary that you prepare an instrument suitable for each type of operation.
And know that these operations will show you the type of instrument you need, when you have long study with them. And knowledge of the methods of this art is a witness to the methods of illnesses: for then you will find within yourself that which suits it from instruments for every illness.
And I am showing you, at the end of this chapter, the number of instruments, which will give examples of what you will cut, and the test by which you will try them on others.

And it is not suitable, unless the heads of the joints are rubbed with it, when they are corrupted, or the bone is large and great.

Shape of the other, slender razor.

Shape of a small, slender, tenuous razor.
This is similar to a saw, and its end is like a saw; it is shown in many places by reason of the cutting of bones.
Shape of the razor suitable for the cutting of that which is perforated in the bones.
Its end is triangular, with sharp edges.
It is made of Indian [material]. And similarly, all that we have said must be made from razors, and knives, and saws.
Concerning the incision of extremities, and the sawing of bones.
Extremities putrefy either from an external or internal cause.
Therefore, if you have treated the putrefaction with medicinal remedies and the treatment does not help, and you see the putrefaction spreading in the limb and nothing counters it, then you must cut off that limb, up to where the putrefaction reaches, so that the afflicted person is freed from death or from the greater harm caused by the loss of the limb.
And a sign of this is that the limb darkens until it stinks; that fire has burned it, or that putrefaction follows the darkening, until that putrefaction is cut away, reaching the part immediately adjoining it, and spreading throughout the whole body.
Therefore, proceed with its excision, and similarly if the cause of the putrefaction is from the sting of certain venomous creatures, such as the sea scorpion, viper, and adder, and the like.
If the putrefaction or injury is in the extremity of a finger, then cut off the finger, and do not give space to the putrefaction so that it spreads to the rest of the hand.
And similarly, if it occurs in the hand, then cut it off from the root of the forearm, and do not give space to the putrefaction until it reaches the forearm.
And if it invades it, then cut the arm at the elbow in its joint.
If, however, the putrefaction passes, and you see it approximately at the elbow, then in that is the death of the sick person. And administering beyond that from treatment, is a matter of strength.
And similarly do with the foot, when putrefaction invades the toe, then cut it at one of the joints; and if it reaches the sole of the foot, then cut off the entire foot. If, however, it ascends to the knee, then cut the leg at the joint of the knee.
If, however, the putrefaction has already reached above the knee, then there is no skill in it, except for dismissal, and leaving the sick person to death.
And the method of cutting or sawing the limb is to tie the ligament below the part you wish to cut, and to tie another ligament above the place. And let the attendant pull one ligament downwards, and let the attendant pull the other ligament upwards. And you should separate the flesh that is between the two ligaments, with a broad scalpel, until all the flesh is exposed. Then it is to be cut or sawn.
And it is necessary that you place linen from all sides, and that the saw does not touch the healthy part; and that additional injury, and a hot abscess, may befall the sick person.
If, however, internal bleeding occurs while you are operating, then cauterize the spot quickly; or place on it certain powders that stop the blood. Then return to your treatment, until it is complete.
Then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it heals.
And I tell you what happened to a man in his foot, this same happening that I tell you.
That is, because it happened in his foot a blackness, with a burning resembling the burning of fire: and that corruption first happened in his toe, until it began the whole foot.
Therefore the man hastened himself, after he saw the corruption occurring in the limb with its intensity, which he found from the pain and its burning: and it afflicted him at the joint, and he was healed.
And after a long time had passed, that same corruption happened to him in the index finger of his hand.
He therefore came to me, and I attempted to recover that superfluity with what I placed upon his hand from medicines after the cleansing of his body: and the superfluity was not repelled, and it began to proceed to another finger: until the corruption began in the hand.
Therefore he called me to cut his hand: and I did not want that, hoping for a repulsion of that superfluity from me: and I also feared for his death at the cutting of his hand. Because the man's strength was above the fall.
Therefore, after he despaired of me, he returned to his own land: and it came to me from him, that he hurried, and cut his entire hand: and he was healed.
And I did not bring forth this account, unless that it be an aid concerning that which falls from the kind of this sickness: and that it be a signification by which it is signified, and according to which it is done.
Concerning the treatment of sinuses, and the quality of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in certain fleshy members, and the space of the abscess is prolonged, until it forms a collection of pus: then it bursts, or is perforated, and that which is in it, of pus, exits, and a hollow place remains, as if it were a vessel: and the skin which is over it, like a cloth which has already become thin: and it does not reach the ultimate in its corruption, nor does it press on the bone, nor on the nerve, nor on the ligament: it deserves to be called a Sinus: and is not called a Fistula: however, if its space is prolonged, until the corruption reaches one of these members, then it is called a Fistula, or zuken.
And its treatment is that you should cut that skin all around, as it is revolved: and especially when it has become thin, and is like a cloth: and you are confident that it does not adhere to the place, because of its corruption.
However, if you hope that the skin adheres, and that corruption does not result from that final adhesion: and if there is thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with an enema: and consider it to be a Sinus.
Then if it is large, and the pus that is emitted from it is of foul odor: then irrigate it with Egyptian medicine, which is vinegar, and oil, and ziniar, taking equal parts, and mixed in a vessel: and boil it over the fire, until the medicine turns red: and it reaches the consistency of honey.
Then take from it as much as you need: and dissolve it with water and honey, and irrigate the Sinus with it: and tighten its opening, and leave the medicine in it for the amount of two hours.
Then draw it out with pressure: do this for so many days, until the Sinus is cleansed, and the stench is removed.
And sometimes it is irrigated with ash water, when this medicine is not available to you: and you should take the ash of vine wood, or ash from the holm oak wood, and pour water over it. Then strain it, and irrigate with it, until you are confident that the Sinus is now washed clean.
If the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine, nor the ash water, then irrigate it with water and honey, to which a little crushed ziniar has been added. Or irrigate it with honey and wine mixed together, because the nature of honey is to cleanse and wash away, and wine causes the Sinus to adhere, especially when the wine contains an excess of astringency and dryness.
When you do this several times, and are confident that the corruption of the Sinus is now removed, then irrigate it with that which causes flesh to grow there.
And take from the diafinicon plaster, dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine, or irrigate it with certain medicines and other ointments, whose properties I have written in the treatise on ointments.
If the opening of the Sinus is narrow, so that the clyster tube cannot enter: then widen it slightly with iron, or place a linen cloth wrapped in Egyptian ointment, or in basil ointment, on it until it is widened.
And similarly, if the opening is also wide, then bring the lips of it together with sutures, and leave an amount in which the clyster can enter without addition.
And similarly, if the opening from which pus flows is elevated upwards, then cut it in the lower part of it, so that the pus flows downwards; because when pus is confined in the depth of the Sinus, it prevents flesh from growing there.
If you cannot cut the sinus approximately below the place you wish, then try to prepare the member in such a way that pus flows from it readily, according to your preparation. Try to keep no pus at all in it, and take strengthening medicines suitable for the purpose you wish. And spread them over a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such size that it covers the entire sinus. Then cut with scissors a hole in the direction of the sinus opening, a little larger than the sinus opening. Then spread mild ointments on another cloth, according to the size of the hole, and place it over the first, a distance from the sinus opening, to allow the pus to escape from it.  And do not remove the large cloth, unless after some days, one at a time.
However, the one over the sinus opening, that is the one you remove every hour, so that what collects in it from the pus may exit; and so that you may know whether that which flows from it, the matter, is much or little, or digested or undigested.
And with this, again consider the location of the sinus itself, whether its owner feels pain in it, or not, and whether there is an abscess there, or not.
Therefore, when your treatment proceeds thus, and you see the matter that exits through the sinus opening, then know that the sinus is now adhering, or approaching adhesion.  Then, therefore, unbind it in the morning, or on the third day; and when you unbind the wound, then change to another cloth, and renew the ointment.
However, if after a long time, a thin matter exits from the sinus, then do not despair of its adhesion, for its healing is approaching, since there are some people who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds, and there are those who act the opposite of this.
If, however, undigested pus exits from the sinus after many days, then know that the sinus does not adhere.
If, therefore, the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and its matter prolonged, then add to the drying medicines; but let the physicians see to it that the medicines are moist in their substance and dry in their virtue, such as diaphanitic ointment, when superfluity of addition of zegi is added to it.
And the most ultimate remedy for its cure is to take diaphanitic ointment and dissolve it with rose oil, and sprinkle over it equal old wine in its substance.
Then mix it with that, and use it.
And it does a similar action to this, when diaphanitic ointment is not present to you, honey, when you boil it until it thickens, and use it. Or take myrrh, and aloes, and frankincense: grind the whole, and pulverize it over honey while it is over the fire. Then line a cloth over it, and bind it over the sinus; and line with the honey that you boiled until it thickened: and pulverize spices over it, and put over it the cloth, and bind it.
And sometimes long aristolochia, and the roots of the celestial lily, and is lily, and the flour of herb, and centaury are administered in it. Use them simple, or aggregated, grind them, and sift them: and pulverize them over the honey which I mentioned, and use it.
If the sinus persists and is not cured by this treatment, then know that there is corruption deep within it: or it is already making an impression on the bones, or on the other hard members, or ligaments: then treat it with the treatment of Fistulas, according to what has been mentioned before.
Concerning the treatment of whitlow and a worn nail, and the incision of a swollen finger, and the section.
A whitlow is an abscess that arises beneath the nail of the thumb of the hand, or foot: and perhaps arises in the other fingers.
When, therefore, its condition prolongs itself and its treatment is neglected, and the abscess becomes a hot abscess, or it is corrupted and emits pus, until it corrodes the root of the nail: and perhaps corrupts it entirely, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, until there is a foul odor from it: and the end of the finger becomes broad, and its color becomes dark.
Therefore, when you treat it, with what we have said in the division, and your treatment does not help, then it is necessary to cut with an iron the entire excess that remains from the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For the cautery is very helpful.
If however the bone is healthy, and the nail is also healthy, and the superfluous nail additions now press the flesh inwards, and begin to prick it and injure it, then it is necessary to place a thin radium under the angle of the nail that pricks the flesh, and drive it upwards: and cut that flesh easily, and place upon what remains of the flesh burning, corrosive medicines, until all of it is removed. Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
If however corruption is already pressing on the bone, then it is necessary to cut that bone and extract it: for it will never be healed as long as corrupted bone remains in it.
If you see corrosion and corruption advancing into the finger, then cut it at one of the joints, according to the remembrance of its previous description. Then treat it until it is healed.
If, however, a nail suffers attrition or a blow, and severe pain occurs in it, then it is necessary to first bleed the patient. Then cut the nail with a sharp spatula in a transverse cut from above to below.
And be careful that the cut does not reach the flesh under the nail, for you will cause severe pain to the patient thereby, and it will be the cause of the birth of additional flesh in that place.
Then treat the place with that which soothes pain.
The extra finger, which is born in some human hands, is perhaps entirely fleshy; and perhaps some have bone in them, and perhaps there is a nail on it; and some are born at the root of the joint, or its birth is in some of the knots of the finger. Therefore, the one born in some knots of the finger does not move; and the one born near the joint of the finger, perhaps moves.
Therefore, the one that is fleshy among them, its incision is easy. That is, to cut it at its root with a broad spatula.
However, the treatment of the one whose birth is at the root of the joint is difficult. Therefore, abstain from its incision.
However, in the one that is born on the finger at the last knot, then it is necessary to cut first its flesh with a round incision up to the bone. Then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for it. Then treat it until it is healed.
However, the consolidation that occurs in the fingers next to each other often happens either from the birth of the person, that is, it is born with it, or from the consolidation of a wound, or from the burning of fire, and similar things.
Therefore, it is necessary to separate that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural shape. Then place strips of cloth, or bandages soaked in rose oil, between them, so that they do not consolidate quickly, and separate them; or place a thin sheet of lead between them, until the area is consolidated as it should be.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs between certain fingers and the palm, then cut that consolidation, according to what is prepared and suitable for the shape of the limb.
Concerning the incision of a defect.
Ulcers are swollen, thick veins filled with melancholic humors that occur in many parts of the body; and their effect is most often seen in the legs, especially in the legs of those who travel, work, and carry heavy loads.
Therefore, it is necessary to administer a purging of the body of black bile several times with a strong purge.
Then phlebotomize the patient's basilica vein.
The treatment of them with iron is according to two methods. One of them is that they should be cut, and the black blood extracted. And the other method is that the vein should be raised and the entire vein extracted.
However, the incision of it is according to this method. First, soak the leg in much hot water until the thick, foul blood is dissolved. Then bind the patient's leg from the upper part of the thigh, down to the lower part of the knee with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one, or two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then raise, that is, draw the blood in your hand from the lower part of the leg, up to the top, and from the upper part the phlebotomist should first treat the patient. Then cut the nail further down until an amount of blood sufficient and tolerated by the patient's strength emerges.
Then tie it; and instruct him to abstain from foods that produce black bile; and repeat the evacuation and phlebotomy when the veins are full and it harms the patient.
However, its elevation is according to this method. Shave the patient's leg if there are many hairs on it. Then immerse it in a bath; or embrocate it on his leg with warm water until it turns red and the veins bulge; or exercise it with vigorous exercise, if a bath is not available, until the limb is warmed.
Then cut the skin in a direct vein incision along its length: either at its end near the knee, or in its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and scrape the vein from every side until it appears to the senses; and at its appearance, you see it sloping from red to black. When it therefore emerges from the skin, you see it white like a string.
Then introduce a probe underneath it until, when it is raised and emerges from the skin, suspend it with a blunt, gentle hook.
Then make another incision near that incision, by three fingers' breadth. Then scrape the skin from the upper part of the vein until it appears. Then raise it with the probes as you did, and suspend it with another hook, as you did at first.
Then make another incision, or several incisions, if you need them. Then raise it, and cut it at the last incision near the heel.
Then pull it out to the incision that is above it.
And do that until you pull it out from the incision that is higher, or from all the higher incisions, until the whole thing comes out; then cut it.
If, however, it does not yield to you for pulling and raising, then introduce a needle with a strong doubled thread, and tie it, and pull it out: or introduce a probe under it, and turn your hand in every direction until it comes out. And beware, lest it be cut. For if it is cut, it becomes difficult for you to raise it greatly, and it enters into a harmful injury from it.
When therefore you have pulled it out completely, then place on the wound site wool soaked in wine and rose-oil, or oil, and treat it until it is healed.
If the vine is contracted, having a twisting in which it is wrapped around in parts, and is not, as we said, according to straightness, then it is necessary to cut along its entire twisting and its wrapping locations.
Then suspend it with hooks, until you have raised it completely: and beware at your cutting on it that you do not cut a vein, or wound it; for it will be difficult for you to raise or extract it. Therefore, preserve your prudence.
Form of the injury by which the vine is healed.
Form of the blind hook.
It does not have a curvature like the other hooks, nor is its end sharp so as not to wound a vein; and it is of thick, gentle flexibility, because if it were subtle, it would cut a vein with its subtlety; indeed, it has unnecessary thickness, as we have said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, such as Al-Andalus, and the land of the Arabs, and in arid, hot lands, of little softness, that is, in which neither grass nor tree grows.
And perhaps it is generated in other places of the body besides the legs.
And indeed its generation is from the putrefaction that occurs beneath the skin, just as happen within the bodies of serpents and worms ascarides, and worms generated between the skin and the flesh.
And a sign of the beginning of the occurrence of this Vein is that a violent inflammation happens in the leg. Then the place becomes blistered. Then the Vein begins to emerge from the place of that blistering, as if it were the root of a plant, or an animal.
Therefore, when its extremity appears from it, then it is necessary to wrap a piece of lead over it, whose weight is from one drachma keil to two drachmas, and tie it; and leave the lead hanging from the leg.
Whenever, therefore, something emerges from it to the exterior, wrap that in lead, and tie it. If it extends too far, then cut a part of it, and wrap the remainder. And do not cut it at the root before the whole of it emerges. For if you cut it, it will contract, and enter into the flesh, and cause an abscess and putrefaction in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it should be gently handled and pulled out gradually, until the last part of it emerges and nothing of it remains in the body.  And from this vein, in some people, there emerges what is five palms and ten in its length. And it has already come to me, that it emerged from a man of twenty palms.
If, however, it is incised during your treatment time, then insert a probe into its opening, and pierce it with a long opening, along with the body, until all the material within is evacuated; and treat the putrefaction of the place with medicines for several days. Then treat it with the treatment for abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the joint of the foot, or on the foot itself; and many openings occur to it, and a branch emerges from each opening.
Therefore treat it as we said in the division, and with what was previously stated.
Concerning the incision on worms generated under the skin, and it is called the Cattle Disease.
This disease is called in a certain land among us "Cattle Disease," because it often occurs in cattle, and is a small single worm that is born between the skin and the flesh.
And it travels throughout the body, ascending and descending, and its passage is noticeable to the senses from limb to limb, until it breaks through wherever it breaks through the skin, and emerges.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humours. As worms and snakes, that is, roundworms and threadworms, happen in the belly.
And it is feared because of its malice. Because when it travels through the body, and rises to the head, and reaches a place, then perhaps it bursts there, and emerges, and destroys the eye; and that happens many times.
Therefore, when you wish to cure it and extract it, then it is not done except at its movement and its appearance to the senses.
Therefore, it is necessary to tie that which is above it and that which is below it with a strong ligature. Then cut above it and extract it.
If it penetrates into the flesh, and you do not find it, then place a cautery on the place with fire until it dies.
And the greater fear is its corruption, by which it corrupts the eye, as we have said.
If however you see it having already reached the head near the eye, then tie below it the eyebrow with a good ligature. Then cut above it and extract it.
And it is necessary for the infirm person to use cleansing of their body, with medicines that dissolve bad putrid humours.
And let him beware of foods that generate putrefaction.
Concerning the incision above the ailment Nakir.
This ailment is called Nakir in our land: and it is a pain that occurs in certain limbs, then changes from limb to limb.
And I myself have indeed seen it, as I relate to you. I was called to a weak woman in certain villages. And she uncovered her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm's cord.
And after I remained an hour, I saw one swelling move with a wriggling motion, as a worm moves, ascending to her shoulder more quickly than possible, so that it is like quicksilver when it runs from place to place.
Therefore, the pain receded from that place and settled in the shoulder.
Then I sat for an hour, and it ran to the rest of the body, until it reached the other arm.
Then she told me that it revolved throughout her whole body, as I had seen.
I was therefore amazed at the speed of its change from limb to limb. And indeed, before that, I had not seen this illness with my own eyes in this way, in this manner. However, I have seen many who find pain changing from limb to limb, and I have not seen it with my own eyes, as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think it is otherwise than because the woman was one of those whose bodies are dry, whose veins are detected; hence, that windiness appears to the senses, changed.
And it is necessary that they not appear in this way in those who are delicate, and whose bodies are moist, whose veins are hidden.
Therefore, when you wish to cure it, and the patient feels that pain: then, if it is apparent to you by sight, as we said, then grasp above it, and below it quickly: and cut above it, until that constricted windiness comes out: and cauterize the place.
If however you do not see it with your own eyes, then cure it with shaking the body, and with what expels windiness, and soothes it with foul pills, and pills of seraphim, and similar things.
Of the extraction of Arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their kinds, and according to the places in which they fall from the body.
Their diversity is, indeed, because some of them have large points, and small: and concave, and blunt. And some of them have three angles. And some of them have tongues. And some of them have stars.
Those, however, which are according to the members on which they fall, are according to two ways. For either the members are from the principal concave members, such as the brain, and heart, and liver, and lungs, and kidneys, and intestines, and bladder, and similar to them. Therefore, when it falls into any of these members, and signs of death appear to you, which I shall relate to you later, then you ought to avoid the extraction of that arrow from them, for death follows him in whom it is, in many dispositions. And when these bad signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden deep within the member, then extract it, and treat the wound.
For the signs of the brain, when an arrow falls into it, and pierces the bone, and exits the membrane covering the brain, are: because there arise from it a violent headache, and scotoma, and dizziness, and redness in the eyes, and swelling, and redness of the tongue, and spasm, and confusion of intellect, and vomiting of bile. And perhaps blood exits from the nostrils, or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off, and the voice is destroyed. And white moisture like gruel emerges from the wound site. And from it exits something like the watery part of flesh.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then refrain from treatment, and remove the arrow if it does not emerge.
The signs of an arrow striking the heart are: that it is near the left breast; and one feels with it as if it were fixed in a hard thing, not in an empty thing. And perhaps the arrow's movement is similar to the movement of a pulse; and black blood flows from the wound. And that is followed by coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting. Therefore know that death descends undoubtedly.
And the sign of an arrow wounding the lung is the outflow of frothy blood from the wound; and the vessels that follow the neck, become suppurated, and the color of the sick person changes. And he exhales a large, deep breath; and he seeks the attraction of cold air.
If the arrow strikes the covering that is in the chest, then it is close to the small ribs; and a large breath occurs with severe pain, with sighing; and all the arm muscles move.
And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then perhaps undigested food comes out of the wound.
And if the arrow strikes the belly and is fixed in it, something of the excrement, or zirb, or the already ruptured intestine, comes out of the wound. Therefore, there is no hope of its cure, nor in the extraction of the arrow.
If the arrow strikes the bladder, and urine comes out, and something proceeds from it to the exterior, and the pain is stronger than the weakness, then know that it is fatal.
However, the remaining limbs, such as the face, neck, throat, shoulder, arm, backbones, collarbone, hip, leg, and similar limbs are saved according to many conditions, when the arrow does not strike an artery, or nerve and is not poisonous.
And I tell you what I have seen concerning these arrows, so that you may understand through that about your treatment.
Because an arrow struck a man in the tear duct of his eye, at the root of his nose. Therefore, I extracted it from the other side, under the fat of his ear, and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which struck him in the fat of his eye under the lower eyelid; and the arrow was already concealed, and I obtained only a small extremity of it, with which it adheres to its shaft; and it was a large arrow from the bows of the Turks, with soft iron, in which there are no ears.
Therefore, the Jew was healed, and no evil accident happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arabic arrow, and it is one that has ears. Therefore, I cut above it between the two guides; and it was already submerged in his throat. Therefore, I worked with it until I extracted it; and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from the man, which had fallen into his belly; and we thought that he would die from it.
After therefore thirty days had passed, and nothing had changed in his condition, I cut above the arrow; and I worked on it, and extracted it; and he was healed, and no evil accident befell him.
And I saw a man into whose back an arrow had fallen, and the wound above it had closed. After therefore seven years had passed, an arrow emerged from the root of his thigh.
And I saw a woman into whose belly an arrow had fallen, and the wound above it had closed, and the arrow remained within her; and nothing changed in her condition, and the woman found no harm in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man, into whose face an arrow had fallen: and the wound closed, and it remained, finding him little pain; and many similar things to these.
And I extracted an arrow from the king's chief man, which had fallen into the middle of his nose, and had already inclined somewhat to the right side: and the entire arrow was hidden.
Therefore I was called to treat him after the arrow's fall up to three days. Therefore I found the arrow wound very tight.
Therefore I searched for it with a slender probe, and did not feel it; and indeed he was finding a prick and pain under his right ear. Therefore I hoped that that prick was from the end of the arrow.
Therefore I plastered the place with a plaster in which there was attractive and digestive virtue, trusting in myself that the place would become an abscess and that the arrowhead would appear to me so that I could cut it out; and it did not happen in the place that an accident signified that the arrow had struck the place.
Therefore I persisted with the plaster over it for many days; and no accident happened, and the wound healed in the meantime; and he remained weak from its extraction for a space of days, until he felt the arrow one day inside his nose. And he told me that.
And he told me that. Therefore I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to that wound for many days, until it opened; and when I probed it, I felt a slender arrowhead adhering to the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound by means of that sharp medicine, until the arrowhead appeared to us visibly; and I spent approximately four months with him during this time.
Then, after the wound had enlarged, and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it, and to move it, and it did not yield to removal; then I did not cease to refine and devise ways about it, until I began one day, with forceps properly made, in the form that their shape will take in the conclusion of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And indeed the physicians judge that the cartilage of the nose is not restored. Therefore, I restored it, and the wound was consolidated, and the infirm person was healed with complete health; and nothing at all harmed him.
And I tell you the nature of the extraction of certain arrows: that you may set it as a rule and a meaning upon that which I have not said.
Since the parts of this art and their distinctions are not encompassed with the narrative, nor does the book encompass it, and the skillful craftsman compares the little with the much: and with that which is present, with that which is absent: and discovers a new operation, and a new instrument in the descent of extraneous things of this art when they happen.
I say therefore, that arrows do not emerge from the members in which they are fixed, except according to two methods. Or by pulling them out from the place from which they entered. Or by the contrary of another part.
And that which emerges from where it entered, or is an arrow proceeding in a fleshy place: and is pulled out, and emerges.
If it does not yield to emergence at the hour in which it falls, then it is necessary to leave it for several days, until the flesh which is around it putrefies: and its extraction is eased, and its emergence.
And similarly, if it is fixed in the bone, and does not yield to you for emergence, then leave it also for several days: and repeatedly remove it with traction and motion every day, until it emerges.
If it does not yield to you for emergence after days, then it is necessary to pierce around the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a fine drill, until the arrow's attachments are enlarged. Then pull it, and remove it.
If however the arrow is already hidden in a place of the body, and is concealed and hidden from sense: then seek it with probes. If however you feel it, then pull it with certain instruments that are suitable for its extraction.
If however you cannot get at it, because of the constriction of the wound, and because of the length of the arrow deep within: and there is no bone, nor nerve, nor vein there: then enlarge over it, until the wound is widened and it is possible to extract the arrow.
If however there are ears by which it is held, then free the flesh fixed in them from every side, by every means that you can, and by means, if it is not possible to free the flesh in the breaking of the ears and their twisting, until it escapes.
And when you administer the extraction of the arrow, wherever it is, then use the twisting of your hand, with forceps on all sides, until you make it escape: and with the utmost subtlety, so that the arrow is not broken, and its extraction and pulling become difficult for you.
If you cannot get at it within one hour, then leave it for a few days, until that which is around it rots. Then try again: for it will then be easier.  But if a flow of blood opposes you, then administer what we have said concerning treatment in its chapter: and guard your skill from cutting a vein, or nerve, or tendon.
And apply every means possible to the extraction of the arrow: and let it be with ease and tranquility, or with slowness and firmness, as I have told you.
And it is necessary, when you pull the arrow, that it be weak in the form in which the arrow was in the injury. Because it is more helpful. If, however, it is not possible for you, then administer what is possible for you from the figures.
However, if an arrow exits from the opposite side, either something already protrudes from it outwardly, or the tip of the arrow creates a bump on the skin near the surface, and you see it protruding; therefore, cut over it, and the incision should be according to the size that forceps can grasp.
Then pull it out; for it becomes easy to remove.
If, however, it remains in the bone, then twist your hand in a circular motion until the arrow is pressed into the bone and enlarges its own space; then pull it out.
And if not, leave it for a few days; then try again, until it comes out.
If the arrow's shaft is in it, then push it out with it.
If however the shaft has broken, and you wish to administer an impulsion, then insert a concave instrument into it, so that its concavity enters the arrow's tail; then push it out with it.
If however the arrow is hollow, then push it out with an instrument entering into it. For the arrow is eased out by it.
If the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary to cauterize all the flesh where the poison is, if it is possible for you. Then treat it with whatever is appropriate for it.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side; and it is close enough to what you touch with the probe, and it is possible for you to cut above it: then cut, and beware of cutting a vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then suture the wound, if you see fit, and treat it until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps with which arrows are extracted.
Let the extremities be similar to the beak of a bird, made of iron; when they grasp the arrow, they do not release it.
And from them are made many forms, large, and small, and medium, according to the quantity of the arrow's size, and its smallness, and the breadth of the wound, and its constriction.
The form of the pusher is concave.
And the form of the blunt pusher.
This is a blunt extremity like a ray, so that its entry into the concave arrow may be eased, and pushed with it.
Of the phlebotomy of veins.
The veins to be opened, of which custom runs in the body, are thirty.
Of these, sixteen are in the head: two pulsating veins, which are behind the ears, called the two Herbs; and two arteries, which are apparent in the two temples; and two veins which are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, which are called the Looking ones; and a prepared vein in the middle of the forehead; and the vein that is at the extremity of the nose. And two Guidegi, which are in the neck. And two veins that are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins which are in the lower lip. And these are the veins which are called Algecherich. And two veins which are under the tongue.
The veins, however, which are opened in the arms, are five veins in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalic, and it is from the wild side; and the common people call it the head vein. And Fusca. And there is the Median vein, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilic and a branch of the Cephalic; and the common people call it the body vein. And the Basilic, and it is situated on the domestic side; and it is also called Titillicaris. And the common people call it the belly vein. And the arm cord, and it is situated above the fold; and it is that in which phlebotomy is performed, and it is that which appears above the thumb with clear appearance.
And the Salvatella; and it is the vein that is between the ring finger and the little finger; and it has two branches.
And in the leg and foot there are three veins. One of them is that which is under the curve of the thumb on the wild side. And the second is the Saphena; and its place is at the heel on the domestic side. And the vein Nesse; and its place is at the heel on the wild side.
And in the other leg there are three veins similar to those.
Concerning the phlebotomy of two veins behind the ears.
Phlebotomies of the two veins behind the ears are helpful for chronic colds, and hemiplegia, and sahafati, and old, bad head ulcers.
And the quality of the phlebotomy is as I describe. And it is that the patient's head is shaved, and his nape is rubbed in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth, with good friction. Then the patient should tighten his neck with a bandage, until the two veins appear. And their locations are behind the ears in two depressed areas of the head. Therefore, seek them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark it with ink there.
Then take the phlebotomist's knife, and it is that which is called the Incidens. Then introduce it beneath the vein in the skin, until the phlebotomist reaches the opening. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upward, and cut the vein with the skin with an incisive incision. And let the length of the incision be the amount of two joined fingers, or roughly that: and make as much blood come out as you wish.
Then bind it with bandages, and leave it until it heals.
And already in the beginning of the book was the incision of those things, and the cauterization of them.
The phlebotomy of the two arteries which are in the two temples is of benefit for old-time hemicrania; and for crooked swelling, and constant ophthalmia; and for the discharge of excessive acute effusions to the eyes.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is according to what I tell you. Let the sick man hold his neck tightly, until two veins appear distinctly to the senses; and let their pulse be demonstrated beneath your finger. Then mark the spots with ink.
Then lift the skin above the vein upwards with your index finger, and insert the lancet underneath, and lift the vein upwards. And cut it, as you did in the other two veins; and cause blood to flow according to your need.
Then release the sick person's constriction, and place your finger on the vein for an hour. Afterwards, place a bandage and pads on it, and tighten it firmly over the constriction; and release it until it heals.
And already the remembering of them, and their incision, and their extraction were previously in the beginning of the book.
Indeed, the benefit of phlebotomy of the frontal vein, after phlebotomy of the cephalic vein, is for ancient diseases of the face; and for hemicrania, and redness; and foul ulcers.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is as I tell you. The infirm man tightens his neck with a bandage until the vein appears. Then take the instrument called a Fossorium.
Place the prominent point that is on the head of the Fossorium above the vein itself, and strike it with a comb, or with something else in its manner; and let the blood run according to the quantity you wish. Then loosen the wound of the infirm person, and bind it until it heals.
And sometimes it is opened with a broad phlebotom. However, it should not be a phlebotom with a sharp end, like the other phlebotoms, but rather with a broad end. And phlebotomize it across; because the bone is nearby; and perhaps the phlebotom will break in it when it is slender.
The benefit of the two veins which are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, is for the eye from scabies and redness; and for a boil in the eye, and ailments of the face.
And the quality of phlebotomy of them is that it constricts the weak neck with a bandage. Then phlebotomize them; and you stand over his head.
And let the phlebotomy be made transverse to the length slightly, with a small phlebotome, a little wide, for there is no flesh in the place. For if the phlebotome is of slender extremity, perhaps it breaks.
Then make the blood flow out as much as is necessary for you. And place a compress upon them, and bind them for one night, and loosen them.
Phlebotomy of the veins of the Nose is beneficial for acute fevers, and violent headaches, and ailments of the face, such as hemicrania, and redness that occurs on the face, especially when it is chronic.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that the weak man binds his neck. Then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a slender, long lancet, and insert it into the middle of his nostril, between that which divides the nose according to its straightness. Because the vein is not apparent to the senses there, and because blood begins to flow immediately.
And you should hold your hand with the lancet for a little while, and cause the blood to run according to your need. Then tie it with one bandage. For it is restored quickly.
Indeed, the benefit of Guidegi's phlebotomy is for the constriction of breathing, and the onset of leprosy, and melancholic illnesses, and impetigo and bad ulcers, and those that gnaw, that is, blistering herpes.
And the quality of phlebotomy of them is that the weak man should be tied under both his neck with a bandage, and the craftsman should stand over the head of the sick person, and the sick person should sit on a seat.
Then the phlebotomist should incise the vein to a length of ample phlebotomy for a short while. Then draw out a moderate amount of blood, or according to what you see to be necessary for it. Then do similarly with the other vein.
Afterwards, loosen the bandage, and bind the two veins with a moderate constriction, so that the sick person is not overly choked. And leave it until morning. For the wound is healed.
Concerning the opening of veins by Algeberich. The veins, according to Algeberich, are helpful in phlebotomy, because they contribute to the cure of sores in the mouth, and gum decay; and bad ulcers; and lip fissures, and bad ulcers, even those found on the nose and its surroundings.
And the method of phlebotomy of them is that you make the sick person sit before you, and bind their neck with a bandage. Then, roll back their lip, and look at the two veins, one on the right side of the lip, and the second on the left; and the appearance of both is by the blackness of both. And that is true, because around them are small, black veins. Therefore, cut each with a suitable incision.
However, if there is doubt upon you, and you do not know which of them it is, then direct yourself to the incision of the larger and more obvious of them.
And similarly do with the two veins which are on the upper lip; and custom runs very much with it; and cut the two veins which are on the lower lip.
Concerning phlebotomy of the veins under the tongue after phlebotomy of the cephalic veins. However, the benefit of phlebotomy of the two veins which are under the tongue is that phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is for the suffocations which are in the throat, and the ailment of the uvula, and the ailments of the mouth.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is that the sick person sit between your hands before the sun; and elevate his tongue, and look under his tongue at one side of it a vein, and at the other side of it a vein; and the colour of each of them slopes towards blackness.
Therefore phlebotomize each of them; and beware lest you extend your hand too far when phlebotomizing them. For under it are arteries, and perhaps a flow of blood will occur from those arteries.
The three veins, however, which are phlebotomized in the elbow, are those whose phlebotomy has become customary among all people.
And their phlebotomy is performed according to two methods. Either by piercing with a broad myrtle phlebotome, or an olive-shaped one, as narrow as a slope, or by cutting with a knife phlebotome, and it is Alnesil. And this is its form.
And from them are broad and narrow types, according to the width and narrowness of the veins. The form of the myrtle phlebotome should be broad, as you see. And it is suitable for opening concave, full, proceeding, apparent, thick veins, and those that contain thick, turbid blood.
And this phlebotome is of smaller width and finer extremities.
And it is suitable for the phlebotomy of thin veins, which contain thin, yellow blood.
The Basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, is a help in phlebotomy. Because it draws blood from illnesses located under the throat and neck, from what follows the chest, and the belly; and it is fitting for the phlebotomist, when performing phlebotomy on it, to be cautious and be aware of the fear from it, for an artery is beneath it. Therefore, if he errs, and adds to the puncture of the phlebotomy, he will cut that artery, and a flow of blood will occur. Therefore, it is necessary not to puncture it with the phlebotomist's lancet: rather, let it be incised with a lancet.
If the Basilic vein is not clearly visible, then it is necessary to seek another vein, and to switch to another, or to seek a branch of it; or to perform phlebotomy on the brachial vein instead. For it is indeed apparent, and cut it with the phlebotomist's lancet, as we said.
If, however, you wish to perform phlebotomy on it, then it is necessary before constriction of the arm, to touch the place, until you recognize the location of the pulse. Then mark the spot with ink, afterward the arm, and cut the vein with a concave incision using the phlebotomist's lancet, as we said. And be careful that the incision does not fall upon the artery's space.
And when you see, when you tighten the ligature, inflation in the place you marked with ink, then that inflation is the inflation of the artery: therefore avoid it.
If you see blood at the phlebotomy leap, like a child's urine, and it is thin, red blood: then know that it is from the blood of the artery. Then quickly place your finger over it, a long space, then remove your finger. If therefore blood is severed (for it is often severed), then bind the arm, and release it: and let the patient beware of its movement: and let it be over his fear.
And let him not move it for several days, until it is healed.
If however the blood is not severed, and it overcomes you, and there is no medicine present at your moment, then cut into the artery, if it appears to you. For its extremities contract, and the blood is severed.
Or take a piece of fig bark, and split it: take one half, and bind it over the site of the vein's constriction with a decent ligature, with bandages and dressings until the next day.
Then if the blood is severed, it is good. And if not, then treat it with that whose previous mention was of the place of powders for severing the flow of blood: and the severing of its blood is not difficult in many dispositions, because of the small size of the wound, and the possibility of a bandage from the arm.
The Fusca vein's phlebotomy is helpful, because it draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; because it is composed of a branch of the Basilica vein and a branch of the Cephalica vein, as we said.
And it befits the phlebotomist that she be cautious of her phlebotomy, for a nerve is under it. Therefore, if during the phlebotomist's puncture, a nerve is encountered, there occurs numbness in the patient and the healing becomes difficult, and perhaps it will not heal completely.
And this nerve often appears to the senses. However, if in some people it is hidden, and is subtle and not shown, then it is necessary that you place your phlebotomy, so as to avoid it with phlebotomy, with sectioning it with Alnesil, and avoid the nerve with your strength.
If the vein is between two nerves, then cut the vein lengthwise.
Concerning the cephalic vein. Phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is helpful, because it draws blood from the head and is beneficial for ailments of the eyes.
And it is necessary in this vein, properly, if you wish, that you should puncture it with a phlebotom of olive, or with a phlebotom of myrtle, since it is safer than all the veins, because there is no artery or nerve below it.
However, it is fitting for you in phlebotomy to avoid injuring the head of the muscle with the phlebotom and to seek a gentler place; and it does not harm it if it is not found on the first stroke, so that it should be repeatedly punctured with the phlebotomy several times.
However, perhaps it becomes suppurative in some people when not punctured on the first stroke, but that suppuration does not harm it in any way.
Concerning the usefulness of Phlebotomy, and the precautions to be observed in it. The quality of Phlebotomy, and its consequences arising from it, on which you ought to reflect.
First, you should know that phlebotomy is not performed except in the preservation of health, and its perseverance, and caution against the approach of illnesses.
And so that phlebotomy be performed on one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the Cephalic, and the Basilic, and the Median cubital. And that phlebotomy be performed at the beginning of spring on an empty stomach, in spring and summer; and in autumn and winter after eating, when signs of fullness appear.
And let phlebotomy be performed on the first, or third day, after three hours have passed of the day.
However, phlebotomy given in illnesses does not have a determined time; but when expediency and necessity call for it, in the night, or day, and in any hour, and in any time.
And children should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age. Nor should a pregnant woman be bled, except from the fourth to the seventh month. Nor should the elderly be bled, who have already passed sixty years.
Therefore, when someone wishes to be bled for whatever reason, it is necessary that he cleanse his intestines beforehand with a gentle clyster, if there is much retained stool in them, so that the veins during the phlebotomy do not draw putrid superfluities harmful to the principal members from the intestines.
Nor should a patient suffering from nausea, that is, satiety-induced nausea; nor one suffering from indigestion, nor one recovering from drunkenness, be bled until that is removed from them. Nor a sick person with dropsy.
And let phlebotomy be avoided again after colic, and vomiting, and diarrhea, and excessive intercourse, and labor, and exercise, and vigils, and fasting, and anything that dissolves the strength from the body or the animal.
Then consider the subtlety of the humors beforehand, if the blood is thick with foods and drinks and medicines, if it is possible for him.
Then let him enter a bath, if nothing prevents him; or let him exercise with some exercise, so that the blood may be refined.
And place his phlebotomy on his chest, about the third day, as we said: and try that his chest be emptied that day from all the bad animal accidents, such as anguish, and anger, and fear; and from all bodily accidents, such as labor, and pain, and excessive discharge, and those similar to them.
And let him have present in his seat things with whose use the custom of men runs through aromatics, and scents, and dancing, and things like them, in as great a quantity as possible.
Then let the phlebotomist sit on a cushion that is higher than the phlebotomist's cushion; afterward, let him pull out his arm, and let the phlebotomist rub it with his hand twice, or thrice. Then he should tie the ligament with a strap, and wrap it twice.
And let the constriction be moderate, because when the constriction is unequal or excessive in the constriction, it then hinders the flow of blood; or in its loosening, it also hinders the flow of blood.
Then, after the constriction, it is necessary that the phlebotomist rub his hands together until the veins swell and are apparent to the senses.
Then the phlebotomist should wipe the phlebotomized area with a little old oil.
Then he should place his left index finger gently over the vein itself, under the spot where he wants to perform the phlebotomy, so the vein does not flee and the incision is not missed. Because some veins are like a fleeing chord during phlebotomy; and some are full of airiness; and when you place the lancet on them, they contract under the lancet, and the phlebotomist is deceived, and the lancet does not open the vein; and if he does open it, it is narrow.  Therefore, it is necessary for the phlebotomist to be firm and careful in all these matters.
Then he should lay down the lancet. If therefore the vein is opened at once, then it is good. And if not, then repeat it another time under that spot a little, or above it with haste, if the place does not become inflamed.
If, however, it becomes inflamed, or the patient grows weak, then leave him for a day or two days; and do not tighten the bandage. For perhaps it will draw the hot inflammation. Nor let him enter the bath.
Then let him repeat the phlebotomy, if he wishes.
If the opening is small, or the blood flows subtly, and you fear that the desired amount of blood will not be released, then repeat the phlebotomy in the same opening according to the correct direction, and add a small amount in the procedure.
And do it quickly, before the place becomes inflamed, for an inflamed place occurs in many people with a small operation.
If, therefore, you see it already inflamed, then do not repeat the procedure on it at all, for it will not excuse anything for you. And place something of the dregs of oil upon it, for it alleviates the flow of blood. And it is better in this place than the oil itself, and than other oils.
And similarly administer the dregs of oil in all your phlebotomies, where phlebotomies are made on veins to prohibit the flow of blood: and they do it with alfarach and sagzenea tyriacs, when one of them is placed on the place.
For blood is refined and resolved when it is thick.
But if a large abscess occurs at the site of phlebotomy, for it often happens, especially to one who is not a phlebotomist except on that occasion, or if the opening of the vein is small, then hurry and place a sponge soaked in water and salt, warmed a little, upon the place; and bind it for one hour, for it will be resolved.
And it is necessary that you do this after the blood has exited from the vein itself, with its completion, or from another vein.
If some blackness or greenness remains in the place after a few days, it does no harm; and if you wish, place some dissolved aloe or myrrh, or something from the juice of mint, that is, of balm or similar to it, upon it.
And very often abscesses and prominences occur at the phlebotomy of the Basilica.
Therefore, place your hand upon it; then if you find softness upon your compression upon it, then that prominence is bad. Therefore, beware that you place upon it something of that which we have said. For perhaps blood of the artery will flow from it.
But make a plaster on it with that in which there is astringency, so that the place is hardened. Then treat it with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
And it is necessary that you extract from every man from his blood according to the quantity of his strength. And that which appears from the color prevailing over the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it go out until it turns red; and similarly if you see it thick, then let it go out until it becomes thin; and similarly if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for one who is full, strong, and needs bloodletting once, that the phlebotomy of the vein be enlarged and the phlebotomist be wide; and in one who is weak, it should be the opposite of that.
And it is necessary to extract it in stages, and that the opening be narrow; and better than what is administered in the phlebotomy of the vein is that it be a transverse section, not a puncture.
And this method of phlebotomy is safe from the flow of blood, and from the incision of the nerve; and it is more praiseworthy, and safer than phlebotomy by width and length.
And to whom it is accustomed to come upon phlebotomy syncope, it is necessary that he eat before phlebotomy with moderate bread soaked in pomegranate juice water; or secaniabin, if it is heated; and extract from him from the blood in three or four stages.
And if he is of a cold temperament, then he should take before phlebotomy bread soaked in rose syrup, or in syrup of honey aromatized with spices, or in fragrant aromatized syrup.
If syncope occurs during phlebotomy, and its cause is the excessive outflow of blood, then it is necessary that he drink water of meat, and a delicate fragrant syrup; and he should use aromatics with almug, and his chest should be lined with it; and he should use the rest which we said in the division in the chapter on syncope from evacuation.
However, whoever wants to evacuate their arm and discharge their blood a second time, it is necessary for him whose phlebotomy is for much evacuation, and whose strength is weak, that he discharge the blood gradually in proportion to his strength over consecutive days.
Whoever, however, wants to evacuate their arm and discharge their blood a second time, and their body is strong, then let them do it after seven or nine hours from their first phlebotomy.
Whoever wants to draw blood from their body to the opposite part to which it inclines, it is necessary that it be evacuated for them on the second or third day.
In whose body, however, there is much blood already heated and sharpened, and causing fever to occur, it is necessary that blood depart from it at once and a large quantity be drawn out; and the opening be enlarged until syncope occurs; after you have observed all the conditions of phlebotomy; and that you place your hand on their pulse during the flow of blood, so that death does not occur in place of syncope. For often that happens, when the phlebotomist is ignorant, and negligence falls.
And it is not appropriate when you want to release the arm and discharge blood a second time, when the opening of the vein has already closed and the exit of blood is difficult, to press upon it forcefully and quickly extend or flex it. For that is very bad; indeed, leave it, until it is bled a second time.
Or remove with sharpness of the phlebotomist that which has clotted from the blood in the opening of the vein, and place upon it some dissolved salt in water. And place upon it something from the tyriaca alfaroch, or sagzenea, and apply it with gentle pressure, until the blood exits.
If however the vein is already suppurated, then leave it, and do not touch it, until the abscess subsides.
If, however, necessity compels the release of blood a second time, and there is no excuse, then either phlebotomize it above that place, or in another arm in another vein.
However, the arm's vein is phlebotomized in the place of the Fusca and Basilica, when they are not found and are hidden, since it is composed of them.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that the infirm person should immerse their hand in warm water until the skin reddens easily, and the vein appears clearly.
Then, a slight constriction is applied over it with a ligature. Then phlebotomize across it gradually, not laterally, nor lengthwise; and let the opening be wide, and let your phlebotomy be over the joint of the hand for a short time.
If the flow of blood is hindered, then immerse the hand again in warm water, and allow the blood to flow into the water until you achieve your desired result.
However, if you are in the days of summer, then you may excuse yourself from re-immersion of the hand in warm water. And indeed, the flow of blood is greatly facilitated in warm water in winter time.
And phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all veins, because there is no pulse vein nor nerve beneath it.
Phlebotomy of the Saluatella vein of the right hand is beneficial for liver ailments.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that you should grasp the mahason of the hand with a ligature, or with your hand, after you immerse it in warm water, until the vein swells and is clearly visible. Then phlebotomize it transversely. And if you cut it completely, that will not harm it in any way.
And be careful not to injure your hand with the phlebotom. For beneath it are the nerves of the fingers bare of flesh.
Then again place the hand in warm water, and let the blood run into it. For if you do not return it to warm water, the blood clots in the vein's opening, and its flow is prevented.
When therefore you have extracted the necessary amount of blood, then place oil and salt over the vein: so that it does not solidify quickly.
And similarly it is necessary that you do with every constricted branch.
However, the benefit of phlebotomy from the left hand is: because it is beneficial for illnesses of the spleen: and similarly do in its phlebotomy, as you did in the second equally.
However, the benefit of saphenous phlebotomy is for illnesses that are in the lower part of the body, such as illnesses of the womb, and retention of menses, and ailments of the kidneys, and old ulcers of the hips and legs, and similar illnesses.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is that the sick person should place their foot in warm water: and make friction upon it, until the veins overflow: and tie above the joint of the foot with a strap. And indeed the vein's location is at the heel, appearing towards the big toe: and from it branch many branches on the sole of the foot.
Therefore, phlebotomize it in its larger branch, or at the heel in its confluence, because it is better and safer.
If one phlebotomizes it on the sole of the foot, then beware of the nerves that are beneath it on the sole of the foot.
And place your phlebotomy where you phlebotomize it transversely, as if you wish to cut it: and let the phlebotomist be skilled.
If, however, the flow of blood is hindered, then repeatedly immerse his foot in warm water, and let the blood run into it, until it is evacuated.
If the phlebotomist errs in the first attempt, then he should repeat the phlebotomy a little higher up, for that place is safe, and you have no fear of harm from it, when you beware of the nerves, as we have said.
And similarly with the saphena of the other foot equally.
The sciatic vein's location, as we have said, is at the heel on the wild side.
And the benefit of phlebotomy for it is, for the pain of the hip, when it is due to hot blood.
And the method of phlebotomy for it is that the patient should enter a warm bath and hurry; and he should bind his leg from the side of the hip up to above the heel by four fingers, with a long thin bandage: for it is not apparent except because of that.
When therefore it appears, then the phlebotomist should lance it according to whatever method is possible for you, or by cross-cutting; and it is better to either cut it with an incision or to cut it with a section. For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in many people.
For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in many people. If you do not find it, and it is not at all apparent to the senses, then the phlebotomist should lance some branch of it, and that is the one which appears on the back of the foot, and toward the ear; and beware of the nerves, and make the blood flow in the quantity you wish.
Then loosen the bandage, and place a skin over the phlebotomy site, and bind the place. For it heals quickly.
On Cupping, and the nature of its use.
Cupping instruments are made from horns, and from air, and from glass.
And cupping indeed is done in two ways. One with scarification, and the extraction of blood; and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping which is without scarification, is according to two methods. For it is either with fire. Or it is without fire.
And the cuppings that are administered with scarification and blood extraction are fourteen places on the body.
