Of which one is, the Alnocrati cupping, and it is at the back of the head; and the Alkehel, and it is the middle of the neck; and the Alhagdahim cuppings, and they are two sheets of the neck at two sides together; and the cuppings of the chin, and it is under the jaw in the lower mouth; and the cuppings of the two shoulder blades; and the cuppings of the Alachosos at the end of the tail; and the cuppings of the two armpits, and they are in the middle of the two arms; and the cuppings of the two thighs; and the cuppings of the two calves.
And cupping indeed does not draw blood from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and because of that no virtue is lost from them, as is the case with venesection. Nor should cupping, whether with fire or without fire, be administered in any illness resulting from fullness, until the whole body has been evacuated.
If necessity prompts cupping due to illness or custom, we administer it at any hour at the beginning of the month, at its end, and in the middle of it, and whenever it may be.
This is because some people, when blood multiplies in them to the point where it needs extraction by cupping, experience heaviness and throbbing in their head.
And some of them experience fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. And some of them experience itching in their face and forehead, and darkness and dullness in their eyes.
And some of them scratch the site of their cupping. And some of them have increased laughter. And some of them experience a taste of blood in their mouth; or the tongue is ulcerated, and they spit blood. And some of them have increased sleep. And some of them see in their dreams blood and redness, and the slain, and wounds, and things similar to these.
Therefore, when we see any of these things, and especially if it is in the third middle of the month, we then order the application of cupping, after two or three hours have passed during the day.
The benefit of cupping the nape of the neck is because it contributes to heaviness in the head and that which flows to the eyes. However, it is necessary that this be after the evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in place of a cephalic phlebotomy.
And it is not permitted to use it for one of cold disposition, or in whom there is a catarrh. For it brings him great harm.
And therefore it is not fitting that the elderly, and those with cold diseases of the head, use it.
But whoever is assiduous over it, oblivion is generated in him.
And therefore it is necessary to instruct the Wind-sucker to lower his hand slightly with the wind-sucker, fearing the generation of oblivion.
Wind-sucking, or Alkehel, is in place of the dark phlebotomy and the Basilica phlebotomy, and therefore it benefits asthma, and constriction of breathing, and fissure of the breathing instrument, and cough, and repletion.
And it is necessary that the Alkehel wind-sucker be impelled for a short while, because if it is placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
Wind-sucking of the alhagdahim benefits accidental pains in the head, and ophthalmia, and hemicrania, and perforation, and pain in the roots of the teeth: and it is in place of the Basilica phlebotomy.
And it is necessary to instruct the wind-sucker not to press his hand with the scarification, lest he strike an artery and cause a flow of blood.
Wind-sucking under the chin benefits ulcers in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar diseases that are in the mouth: and it takes the place of the alieherich phlebotomy, which is in the two lips.
Wind-sucking of the spatulas benefits alchafacham, which is caused by reflex and heat.
Wind-sucking of the inner parts of the two foci benefits the relief of the phlebotomy of the three veins, Basilica, and Fusca, and Cephalica, because it draws blood from all the small veins that are in the flesh, and those small veins draw from other larger veins until the drawing reaches the three veins.
And it is necessary to instruct the wind-sucker not to proceed much in the scarification, because the place is denuded of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
A cupping that is applied over the alchausos benefits hemorrhoids of the anus and lower ulcers.
And it is necessary to instruct the wind-sucker to have a large cupping, and to be made of air, because the place requires strong suction, and perhaps the small cuppings break; and a large scarification should be made.
Cuppings of the legs diminish noticeably, because they draw blood from the entire body, and benefit old pains in the kidneys, and the uterus, and the bladder; they provoke menstruation, and benefit bothor and ademenul; and they stand in place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins and alhorcobeni.
However, they weaken the body greatly, and cause fainting in many people.
And the benefit of alhorcobeni cuppings is close to the benefit of cuppings of the two legs.
The quality of cupping placement is to place the cupping initially empty and apply moderate suction, and do not prolong the placement of the cuppings, but place them quickly and remove them quickly so that the humors come to the place with equal arrival; and do not cease repeating this and continuing it until you see the place already red and inflamed, and the redness of blood appears; then, therefore, scarify, and repeat the suction gradually. Then consider in the disposition of the bodies.
For those who are of delicate flesh, with rare pores, you should scarify them once, no more, so that the place is not ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to enlarge the scarification slightly and deepen it a little: and to temper the suction with ease, speed, and gentle movement.
If there is thickness in the blood, then you should scarify it twice. In the first instance, a path is opened for the thinning and liquefaction of the blood; and in the second, for the thorough examination of the extraction of thick blood.
If, however, the blood is very foul, then let the scarification be done a third time, so that the ultimate result is achieved.
And finally, when we wish to extract blood gradually, or just a little, one scarification is enough for us. If we wish for a large extraction of blood, we scarify many times.
And if we measure that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that it be scarified with deep scarification, and the equal limit in scarification is the depth of the skin.
What is it that should be administered from oils at the application of Cupping, and what should the cupper beware of?
In one whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are narrow, it is necessary to anoint the cupping places with oils that are opening, soothing, and resolving: if it is in the summer season, such as alkeiri oil, or violet oil, or oil of sweet almonds, or oil of pumpkin seed.
And if it is in the winter season, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or sarbantine oil, and similar ones.
If however the superfluity is thick, cold, then the oil should be amaracin oil, or balm oil, or alben oil, or anise oil, and similar ones.
If however the cupper has large pores, tender flesh, then it is necessary that they be prohibited from oil, and it is necessary that they wash the cupping places after the cupping with rose water, or with cold water, or with soothing water, or with pumpkin water, or with purslane water, and similar ones.
However, he whose blood is of great moisture, should wash the cuppings with vinegar, or with myrtle water, and sumac, and similar things.
However, whose superfluities are thick, should wash their cuppings with old wine, or with sorrel water, or with anise decoction, or chamomile, and similar to them.
And it is necessary that cupping be avoided in a bath, or in the vicinity of a bath, indeed it is necessary that it be administered after exiting the bath for an hour, or for two hours.
And it is not necessary that anyone sleep after cupping.
What is it that cupping, or bloodletting, should be regulated before cupping?
And after it, one should consider first: if the cupped or bled person is choleric, and sharpness and inflammation overcome their blood, then it is necessary that they take refrigerants, such as pomegranate juice, and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and secaniabin, and iuleb, and similar to them.
And place their food chickens, and the meats of sheep sekebiget, and foods seasoned with agrimony, and similar to them.
And whose complexion is cold, it is necessary that they take in a drink syrup of honey, or of almiua, or alsecaniabin made of seeds, and take a fragrant wine, which is between old and new, and let it be prescribed that they use little food. And place their food chickens, and canebir, and sparrows, that is, small birds, and doves' young.
And it is necessary that the drink on the day of cupping and bleeding be more than the food, and it is necessary that in some people, in the hours of tyriaca alfaroch or a medicine of musk, that is, muscata, or alesselithe, be given in the drink before cupping or before or after bleeding, so that the main members are strengthened and the blood is refined. And it is not fitting that they take it in heated drink.
Concerning cupping without scarification. However, cupping without scarification is cupping which is placed upon the liver, and the spleen, and gout, and upon the belly, the navel, and the places of the kidneys, and the pouch of the hip, because these members do not bear scarification upon themselves.
And they are not sought out except either by the attachment of blood from a limb to a limb, such as when we place a cupping glass on the nipples in an illness of nosebleeds. And we use them to relieve a cold, viscous windiness in a limb, such as when we place a cupping glass on the belly and navel. For it itself thins the limb, and warms it, and removes the pain, because it resolved that windiness.
It is also placed upon the kidneys, when obstruction or a stone occurs in them. For by its power of suction, perhaps it opens the obstruction or removes the stone from its place.
And similarly it does when placed upon the liver and spleen, in case of windiness to be treated in either.
And these cupping glasses are administered empty with only the incision. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of lukewarm water in diseases of pleurisy; and that is, that the cupping glass be filled, and large, with warm water alone, or water in which herbs suitable to it have been boiled.
Form of cupping glass administered with fire.
Let its opening be the size of two open fingers, according to how we have formed it; and let its depth be half a palm; and let there be a small hole on its side, in a place approximately its midpoint, corresponding to the size where the needle enters it; it shall be made of brass, or of citron, with thick, smooth, even, and polished edges, so that it does not harm the limb at the point of placement; and let a transverse rod of brass or iron be in its center, where the candle with fire is placed.
And now this cupping glass is made large, larger than we have described, and a little smaller than that, according to the diseases and the age of the one who uses it.
For the cupping glasses of children and thin people are different from the cupping glasses of men and those with large bodies.
The method of placing this cupping glass with fire upon the limb is as follows: light a wick with fire from linen, or a small candle of wax; and place it over the middle of the transverse rod which is in the middle of the cupping glass; so that the fire is ignited approximately at the lower part of the cupping glass, so that the sick person's body is not burned.
Then it is placed upon the limb; and a finger should be over the hole that I have mentioned, until we hold the cupping glass; when needed, remove the finger, and vapor will come out through that hole, and the cupping glass will immediately be released.
Then light the wick according to its method, and repeat it, if you need it.
However, in a cupping glass administered in the sickness of pleurisy with water, there is no transverse rod, nor hole; and it is not administered, unless it is filled with water, and placed upon the limb only.
And this is its form.
And this cupping glass, the larger it is, so that it can hold much water, is better.
Concerning the suspension of bloodsuckers.
Bloodsuckers are not applied in multiple places, unless in parts of the body where the application of cupping is not possible, due to their small size, such as the lip, and gums, and the like. Or on a limb stripped of flesh, such as a finger, and nose, and the like.
And the quality of using them is: that you obtain leeches which are in fresh, clean water, free from decay.
Then let them be placed in fresh water, day and night, until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their bellies. Then the body should first be evacuated with phlebotomy, and with cupping. Then wipe the sick member until it turns red. Then place them upon it.
When they are filled, and fall, and suction of the place is possible with a cupping glass, then it is most useful; and if not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then with much water, and it is rubbed, and squeezed out.
If the course of blood after the fall of the bloodsuckers prolongs, and it is oozing: then immerse a linen cloth in cold water, and place it over it, until the oozing is stopped.
If the blood multiplies, then powder upon it crushed zegi, or gallnuts, and things similar to them from astringents, until the blood is stopped.
Or let the halves of shelled beans be placed on the area, and let them be left until the beans adhere to the place, for the blood is stopped.
And if a repetition of blood-letting is necessary, when possible, change them.
If, however, the leeches are prevented from hanging, then the place should be wiped with fresh blood. Or pierce the area with a needle until some blood comes out. Then they are placed, for when they feel some blood, they adhere immediately.
When therefore you want them to fall off, powder something of crushed aloe, or salt, or ashes, upon them: and they will fall off immediately.
This chapter is also very necessary in the art of Medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations of accidents in the bones.
Know, O children, that the foolish physicians and the common people boast about this chapter, and those who have never inspected the book of the ancients, nor read a letter from it.
And for this reason, this method is private among the sciences in our land. And I have never encountered any good in it at all, and I have not acquired that which I have acquired from it unless because of the length of reading the books of the ancients, and study exceeding the understanding of them, until I extracted the knowledge of it from them.
Then I adhered to experiment and study for the length of my life.
And now I have described to you from it in this chapter all that contains my knowledge: and upon which my experiment passed, after I have abbreviated it for you, and delivered it from the occupation of elongation, and abbreviated it by the end of abbreviation, and shown it at the end of declination: and I formed in it many forms from the forms of instruments that are administered in it, since it is by the addition of declination, as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter II.
It is necessary, as we say in the prologue of this chapter, to summarise the discourse and chapters, the understanding of which the craftsman must first grasp to stand upon their truth: namely so that you, and those who are ready, may know, or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when a fracture, or dislocation, or twisting, or fall occurs to someone, then it is necessary, that you should first bleed him, and release him, or both at the same time: if nothing prevents it, such as weakness of strength; or if the person to whom this has happened is a child, or an aged decrepit person; or if the time is of great heat, or great cold.
Then let him be content in his food, with cold vegetables, and the flesh of birds, and kids; and forbid wine, and coarse meats, and filling himself with food and all food that fills the veins with blood. Until when you are certain of the hot abscess, and do not fear the effusion of matter to the limb: then let the sick man return to his first regimen, with which his custom ran.
When, therefore, the broken bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the sick person be fed with nourishing foods, with much, coarse, heavy nourishment, in which there is viscosity: such as foods made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish; and strong wine, and things similar to them. For with this regimen, the coagulation of the fracture occurs more quickly and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in early old age and decrepit, cannot be joined and consolidated according to their nature always, on account of the drying up of their limbs: and those that are in them at the end of their softness are consolidated and joined, just as the bones of small children.
However, nature attaches something like glue, in which there is thickness, to the fractured bone from every side, with which it adheres and binds it, until they are glued together, and bound together, until it comes to the end of its strength and firmness, as it was at first, until nothing prevents it from its operations: and for this reason it is necessary that you put before the sick person nourishing foods, in which there are weight, and viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that fractures are of diverse kinds, according to the diversity of the limbs; for a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the skull; and a fracture of the breastbone is different from a fracture of the spine; and likewise all the remaining limbs are different from one another; and I have come with a recollection of every kind of fracture, each distinguished from the other in its own chapter. And the kinds of bone fractures are diversified even within themselves.
Because a fracture of it can be arched, besides which splinters occur in it. And sometimes its fracture occurs along the length of the bone: and its fractures are splintered, that is, splinters, and proceeding additions, as are in a reed when a knife is fitted to write with it. And not proceeding: and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption at the head; and there is a fracture with a small fissure.
And each kind has its own nature, or instrument, in its restoration, according to which its remembrance will come, distinct in its places.
And from that by which the fracture of the bone is known, there is its crookedness and its own prominence; and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you press it with your hand.
Therefore, when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor sound upon your touch when you touch the bone with agitation, nor does the sick person find great pain: then there is no fracture there, but rather it is possible that there is crookedness or a small fissure; therefore it is not necessary to move it with the hand and pressure at all, but rather place upon it from among the medicines, whose memory comes, after the moment that suits the place; then a subtle bandage is tied.
And know that when a place is curved and cleft in two, besides what struggles happen in it, nevertheless each part is removed from its equal; then it is necessary for you to begin your hour for its straightening, making it equal before a hot abscess happens to it.
If however a hot abscess happens to it, then leave it for a few days, until the abscess rests. Then make it equal according to whatever manner is possible by gentleness and skill.
And know that its restoration and its equalization are easier than of a bone in which struggles happen.
Then bind it, according to how its memory will come to you.
If however there are struggles in the bone, then the extension of this broken limb from two parts is necessary, if it is a hand or a foot: or with your hand, if the limb is small; or with two ropes, or with a rope and a hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb upon an equal place, according to its natural shape, until when the broken parts of the limb are extended; then strive to bring those additions back into their proper places with all skill, and in whatever manner you are able; and be careful, according to your strength, that no pain or injury befall the weak through your operation; and strive according to your strength to apply one of the two bones with its partner, according to the best forms.
And it is necessary at that hour to try and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see there anything different, rectify it and make it equal by the measure of your ability.
And beware of violent extension and strong compression, as many foolish people do. For many times they cause a hot abscess or contraction in the limb through their operation, as we have often seen it from their operations.
Then, after the equalization, and decent operation, and tightening of the limb, and rest, and tranquility, advise the sick person about it. And make him beware that he does not move it during his waking hours, and during his sleep, in his turning and his stirring, and during his excretion, and all his movements with all his strength. And that he be careful that the limb's position not be one that will be followed by pain. For when he feels pain or injury in the position of the limb, then he must change it to another position where he does not feel pain.
And be careful with him that his position be even and straight, so that twisting of the limb does not occur when it is restored.
And the quality of the fracture of the limb is as I describe.
Know that fractured limbs differ in their smallness, their size, and their shape.
Therefore, for those small ones, such as the arm, and finger, and toe, and similar to them, a wrapping of soft, thin cloth is necessary. However, for those that are large, such as the thigh and leg, a wide, firm wrapping is necessary.
Because a wide ligament connects a large limb and binds it on all sides with an equal tightness, so that no bruise enters it.
Then, after your evacuation, begin to apply the suitable liniment to it, on soft linen over the site of the fracture itself. Then begin to wrap the bandage around the fracture site three or four times, according to the limb's needs, and gently tighten your hand with the bandage.
Then proceed with it to the upper part from the fracture site, and tighten it less than the tightness you used on the fractured site. Then, move away from the fracture site with the bandage slightly, and gradually loosen the tightness until you have a large part from the healthy area.
Then take another strip and wrap it again around the fractured site in turns. Then proceed with the bandage to the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be in tightening the bandage and loosening it, according to what we said in the first wrapping above.
Then place between the bandages soft stuffing or cloth, with which the crookedness of the fracture is equalized, if there is crookedness in it; and if not, do not put anything in it.
Then wrap another strip over it. Afterwards, place over these bandages properly made strips immediately, if there is no swelling in the place, nor a hot abscess.
But if there is swelling or an abscess there, then place over it whatever sits the abscess and removes the swelling, and leave it for a few days. Then bind strips over it.
And the strips should be from the halves of broad reeds, shaved and shaped skillfully; or they should be from the wood from which sieves are made, which are made from pine or gerard, that is, palm branches, or calingi; and let the width of each strip be, according to this form, and this same figure.
However, it is necessary that the strip placed over the wound itself be somewhat thicker and wider than the other strips; and the length of the strip should be according to the size and smallness of the limb.
Then bind another strip over the strips, according to the first binding. Then tie over it with threads properly, according to what we said about the binding; and it should be that your binding is more tightly over the place of the fracture; and the further it is from the fracture, the looser the binding.
And it is necessary that the middle threads be of medium thickness and thinness, and be of soft flax.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the work of foolish men who place their threads from twisted flax cords, there is great error; because the binding falls away from them outside its proper quality.
And again, thin threads are not suitable, because you will not achieve through the binding with them what you wish.
And it should not be less than a finger's breadth between the splint and splint.
If the patient is injured by the splint ends after binding, in healthy areas, then place soft stuffing or dyed wool under them, until nothing is harmed by that.
However, when there is a fracture with a wound, and a rupture occurs in the skin, I will come with a remembrance of it in the second chapter, according to its peculiarity.
And know that not every broken limb needs to be bound with splints from the first day. This is because when a limb is large, you should not place splints upon it unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your safety concerning the outcome of a hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters, with which the ancients restored those that are placed upon fractures and dislocations.
A method of an abbreviated common plaster, restoring a fracture, suitable for many complexions, especially women and children, since it is from it that neither heat nor cold prevails.
And it is to take from the mill dust, and it is the flour dust that hangs on the walls of the mill by the movement of the millstone: and form it as it is, except that you sift it with egg white: and make its mixture not thick, nor thin. Then use it.
Another method of plaster, restoring fracture, dislocation, and twisting. Take of almess, laudanum, acacia, enula, and mugath, and juice of all,  a drachm X; myrrh and aloes, both a drachm V; and alethil a drachm XX.  Grind all these, and sift them, and mix them with alethil water; or with egg white, if the patient's complexion is heated. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, quickly restores broken bones, and benefits many people.
Another method of plaster, which also benefits fractures and twisting. Take of mugath, mess, and white althea, of all a drachm X; myrrh and aloes, both a drachm V; acacia a drachm VI; Armenian bole a drachm XX. Grind all these together thoroughly, sift them, and form a mixture with water or egg white, and administer.
A method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places; and it relieves pain that arises from them, and the splitting of bone, and fracture. Take clean wool, and immerse it in vinegar and decocted oil, and place it upon the affected area.
And in this plaster there is no virtue of restoration, but it is good for relieving a hot abscess, and it drives away pains properly.
A method of plaster that restores a broken bone. Take leaves of figwort and leaves of wild poppy; and grind them all, and make a plaster from both.
A method of a shortened plaster, administered in the restoration of a broken bone; and when you want the resolution of a remaining abscess. Take of the root of marshmallow, and camomile, and violet flowers, and herb flour, of each one part:  Grind them all and mix with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
If however it is inflamed, then make it with water of damp coriander, or with water, and use it.
A method of another stronger plaster for resolution, than this first, when a hard abscess occurs in the restoration of a limb. Take of marshmallow root, and flaxseed, and pomegranate bark, and melilot, and marsh-mallow, and violet flowers, and camomile, of each one part; grind them all and mix with willow water, or with sweet water, or with wine. All of that according to the warmth of the limb and the quietness of its heat.
Hippocrates, however, did not say in his book that anything should be placed upon a fractured limb during its restoration, except a preparation made from wax and oil; nothing else; and he stated that it should be of a consistency between thick and thin.
Galen, however, considered it appropriate to place upon a fractured limb during its restoration substances that contain the drying of heat, such as myrrh, and aloe, and frankincense, and similar ones to those that we have mentioned.
Method of the plaster that is beneficial for injury and pain. Take equal parts of mugwort, chickpeas, cut human hair (or bird's feathers), marsh-mallow, and salt: grind them, sift them, and mix them together; and make a plaster from it.
The amount of time the bandage should remain in place before being removed is as follows: if the patient experiences no pain, no itching, and the broken bone does not shift from its position, then do not remove it for many days. However, if the patient experiences intense itching, or disturbing pain, or swelling in the area, then quickly remove it at once, and take the plaster away from him: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, moistened: and immerse it in warm water, and wash the area with it until the itching subsides, and the pain ceases.
However, if the patient experiences intense itching, or disturbing pain, or swelling in the area, then quickly remove it at once, and take the plaster away from him: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, moistened: and immerse it in warm water, and wash the area with it until the itching subsides, and the pain ceases. Then leave the limb to rest for one hour. Then place upon it finely shredded wool, soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil; and bind it over it for one night, until you are certain of avoiding a hot abscess, and the limb's swelling subsides, and its pain is relieved.
Then apply a slender bandage, and a small plaster; and do not bind it with your first constriction, and make it slender with it, until it is healed.
If you see that the abscess, redness, pain, swelling, and all the other symptoms have completely disappeared, and you no longer need a plaster and bandage, then apply it again as you did at first, equally.
If none of the things we have mentioned happen to the limb, then do not loosen it unless after three, or four, or five, or seven days. And when it is left for twenty days, according to what appears to you from the loosening of the limb, as we have said, until when the fracture adheres and the flesh around it has coagulated, then add even more to the bandage than your initial bandage: and add also to the thickening of the weak food, in accordance with its previous mention.
However, if you see the fracture site already drying up and becoming more thin than necessary, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Therefore, bathe it with lukewarm water every time you loosen it, and let it be so for every three days, and slightly relieve the bandage.
Through this operation, then, nourishment flows to the limb, and it heals quickly.
However, that which foolish restorers do in the case of a fractured limb a second time, when it is not restored initially as it should be, and is restored crookedly, is an error of their operation and a great fear: and if it were correct, the ancients would have said it in their books, and would have operated with it.
And I have seen no trace of it in any of them at all, and it is better that it not be done.
Concerning the accidental making of a head wound.
The types of fractures are diverse, and its shapes are numerous; and its causes are distinct.
For there is a fracture caused by a blow with a sword; and the blow removes the bone entirely as far as it reaches the siphac, which is under the bone, just as an axe does to a log; and on account of this, this type of fracture is called Axialis.
Or the sword cuts a part of the bone, and cuts its surface, and the incision does not penetrate to its final point; and this type of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, a simple lifting.
And there is a wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture that is a crushing or abrasion; and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall on a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which now approaches the periosteum, which is under the bone, or is on the surface of the bone; and the wound of these two fractures is also either small or large.
And there is a fracture, which is hidden in the bone to the subtlety of a hair; and it is a small fissure, and because of that this type of fracture is called Capillaris.
And from among them is a fracture, which occurs from a fall or a blow of a stone, and similar things, which cause a lamina of the bone to enter its interior, and a cavity is made: just as happens with bronze cauldrons, when a blow strikes them, and a part of them enters the interior.
And this happens most often in the heads of moist bones, such as the heads of children.
And all these types of fractures are separated exertions, and I will come with a recollection of it all in its proper place.
And we recognize all these types of fractures by probing over them, and by investigating them with probes, and by removing the corrupted flesh that is over them.
The capillary type, however, is recognized by this, that it is detected over the bone, and is scraped off, and is painted over it with ink: for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, first consider the patient's symptoms: then, if you see from the symptoms that it signifies a clear sign of fear, such as vomiting of bile, and extension, and loss of understanding, and loss of voice, and fainting, and acute fever, and protruding eyes, and redness of them, and similar symptoms in various dispositions, undoubtedly it is not cured: and if you see the symptoms not alarming you, and you hope for recovery, then begin its treatment.
If a wounded person comes to you immediately after being wounded, and it is in the days of winter, then it is necessary that you strive in the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to every disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then it is necessary that you hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before that which is under the bone from the bandage is corrupted, and those symptoms which we have described occur.
If the fracture of the bone has already reached the bandage touching the brain, and is with splinters and tearing, then it is necessary that you cut off the broken and torn piece, according to what I tell you.
And it is to shave the head of the wounded person and uncover the bone in whatever way is possible for you, and according to the shape of the wound, and what is easy on the sick person.
If, during the uncovering of the bone, a flow of blood or a hot abscess occurs, then apply to it what is necessary. And it is to fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose-oil, and make the wound rest, until the abscess subsides, and you are secure from the flow of blood. Then begin in the treatment of the bone and its removal.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
One method indeed is to cut the bone with a scalpel of narrow blade, and this is its form.
Then administer another scalpel, a little wider than the first scalpel, and this is its form.
Then administer yet another scalpel, even wider than the second.
And the second method is that you have a number of different scalpels, some wider than others, and some shorter than others; and let the edges of their points be sharp, and let them be made of strong iron, or steel; and use ease in striking upon the scalpel, so that it does not strike the head and wound it.
If however the bone is strong, hard: then it is necessary that you pierce around it, before you administer the scalpels, with drills which are called non-deepening drills; and they are not called so, except because they do not pass through the limit of the fleshy bone to that which is behind it; because a drill has a round point above that which is under its sharp head, like an edge, and a small circle prevents sinking into, and passing through, the thickness of the bone.
And it befits you to take from these drills a multitude, each fitting the thickness of the bone, until you have a drill for every skull; whose sharp point's size in length and shortness should be according to the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three kinds of drills, or trepans.
The method of piercing around the fractured bone is: that you place the drill on the bone, and turn it with your fingers, until you know that the bone has already been pierced. Then change the drill to another place, and place that which is between every hole the thickness of a radius, or about that.
Then cut with the chisels that which is between every hole from the bone, and make it finer than you can above it from the point of lightness, as we said, until you eradicate the bone, either by your hand or with some other of the instruments that you have prepared for it, such as pliers and fine forceps.
And it is necessary that you guard against every precaution, lest the drill or chisel touch anything from the flesh.
When, therefore, you have encircled the bone by piercing, and it has been separated from the flesh, if it has been continuous with it, and you have extracted it, it is necessary to scrape and equalize the end of that which remains in the bone with another instrument similar to a chisel. However, it is necessary that it be thinner and more subtle than other chisels.
If anything remains of small bones, and splinters, then take them with ease, with that which is with you of the instruments. Then treat the wound after that with the lint and ointments that we have said.
And another method of operation is easy, utterly free from fear, which Galen said, and praised it with great praise, and this is his statement. It is necessary for you first, that you begin to uncover the part of the bone from the place where the fracture is more severe and more evident: until when you have uncovered that part, and have placed under it the end of this lenticular chisel, of which this is the shape.
And the lenticular part, smooth, does not cut anything: and its sharp part going in length on its two sides, as you see: so that the lenticular part rests on the bone, its sharp side facing the bone.
Then strike above the chisel on one side with a small mallet, until you cut the whole bone with ease, just as it turns: and you be secure from the cloth, that no accident may happen in it, which you fear entirely.
And if the surgeon were not more foolish than men, and more ignorant of this, nor were asleep, then if anything remained attached to the bone from the cloth in some places, he would cut it away from it with the end of his lenticular chisel, and free it from it with ease. It is freed from it without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the cloth, and that which is cut away from the bone is its surface, and there remain on it roughness and fine irregularities, then it is necessary that you scrape away that roughness, and remove those irregularities with the fine razors from which you have prepared a number of various sizes, so that it may be possible for you to apply those which are most suitable and straighter, according to that to which the operation leads you, and the fractured bone and its shape, in every appropriate place. And it is necessary that you apply in your judgment, with which you first scrape the bone, broader of those razors. Then use after it a finer one.
And it is necessary that you apply in your judgment, with which you first scrape the bone, broader of those razors. Then use after it a finer one. And do not cease to do that according to continuity, until you arrive at applying the thinnest and finest of them all.
In each of the remaining small fissures, and fine fracture, it is necessary that you administer care, according to that which leads to its straightening.
And it is a matter not hidden above all, to whomever in this art has any study, and has stood upon that which we have written, and shown in the great fractures.
If, however, the flap is retained when you remove the bone: then it is necessary to take a linen cloth, according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the wound opening. Then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth: and do it as easily as you can upon it, so that it is not burdened by this.
Then administer a broad bandage above it: and do not tighten it, unless by the amount that holds only the wound: and leave it with this care for one day, or two days, until you are secure from a hot abscess. Then loosen it, and apply after it medicines with which superfluous dryness is removed: such as lily roots, and herb flour, and frankincense powder, and birthwort, and similar to them: and it is that you make a powder from these medicines, which is pulverized on the wound as if they were dry.
And universally apply to it any medicine whose property is to cleanse, and not to bite. And beware, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment is clean: nor be there filth of ointment, nor dirt.
And beware, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment is clean: nor be there filth of ointment, nor dirt. Nor allow the virus to gather in it at all: because when virus gathers on the membrane of the brain, it corrupts it, and putrefies it, and great harm comes to the sick person afterwards: and this happens in the top of the head, when the mouth is freed from it, especially when its care is neglected, blackness on its surface.
Therefore, if the sick person experiences the misfortunes that we have mentioned, know that it is undoubtedly harmful.
And if blackness does not occur except from the medicine that is placed upon it, and it is in the power of that medicine to cause that blackness: then it is necessary that you take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and lines with them a cloth. Then place it upon the top of the head: afterwards treat it with the types of treatment that are suitable, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except if two of its upper parts are broken simultaneously, or one of them: because it is two bones. Since the lower part of it is cartilaginous, it does not break, and what happens to it is only abrasion, twisting, and crookedness.
If one of its two parts is broken, then it is necessary that you insert the little finger into the nostril, and straighten that fracture with your index finger and thumb from the outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape: and do it skillfully, with ease. And beware that your operation does not cause pain to the sick person.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it is necessary to straighten it with the edge of a radius bone, on which there is a small thickness.
If, however, the fracture is in two parts, then do the same: and hasten its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, at rest from a hot abscess.
Then introduce into the end of the nose a bandage of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part; or introduce two bandages if the fracture is in two parts; and let the bandage have a thickness of such quantity as to fill the nostril.
And some ancient restorers said that bandages should be soaked in butter and changed every day. However, that does not seem to me suitable; rather, the bandage should be soaked in egg white, mixed with mill dust. Then leave the bandage until the bone is strengthened and the cartilage hardened.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nose in place of a bandage, after a soft cloth has been wrapped around it; and this is done so that it holds the nasal fracture more firmly and does not prevent the weak person from breathing.
And this is not necessary. If you wish, do it; and if you wish, do the bandages.
If a hot abscess should occur in the nose again while you are treating it, plaster the nose with cerote, or with cot infused in rose oil, or a moderate amount of diaquilone.
If, however, a hot abscess does not occur, then it is necessary that you plaster it with sesame flour and powdered frankincense mixed with egg white. Then place soft lint over it, and do not bandage the nose at all.
If, however, the bones of the nose are fractured or broken in small pieces, then it is necessary that you cut around them and extract them using instruments that are suitable for them. Then stitch the wound, and treat it with that which causes flesh to grow and to heal with appropriate plasters or ointments.
If, however, a wound occurs between the nostrils, then it is necessary to treat it with lint and administer a lead cannula until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the lower jaw, when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken, and its fracture is not accompanied by a wound: then see if its fracture is only on the outside, and it is not broken in two, and is bent inwards; then its knowledge is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on its right side, it is necessary to insert the index finger of the left hand into the patient's mouth; similarly, if the fracture is on the left side of the jaw, then insert the index finger of the right hand; and with it push the curvature of the fracture from the inside out with your other hand from the inner side of the bone properly, and with it make it straight.
If the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary to apply reduction from both sides, according to the straightness, until its alignment is possible.
If, however, there has already been movement or breakage in the teeth, then tie those which you hope will remain with a gold, silver, or silk thread. Then place a poultice on the fractured jaw. Then place a doubled cloth over it, and put a large, properly made plaster over the cloth; or a piece of sole leather of equal length to the jaw.
Then bind it above, according to how its ligation is prepared for you, and its application is appropriate, until it is not broken; and instruct the sick person to use tranquility and quiet; and give him bland, easily swallowed food.
If, however, you estimate that something of the form has already been altered in any way, then hurry and loosen it on the third day. Then straighten what has been altered from it, and plaster it with mill dust, with egg white, or with sesame flour, after you have removed the poultice; and place soft wool over the plaster.
While therefore that plaster remains attached to it, and the position of the bone is not altered, leave it, do not loosen it, until it is healed; or bind the fracture. For this fracture is often bound for three weeks.
If in the meantime a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times for its sedation, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider. For if a piece is already separated from the bone, or from the surrounding tissue, then be careful in this, so that you remove those pieces of tissue with whatever suits you in removing them from the instruments.
If truly the opening of the wound is narrow, then widen it with a lancet according to your necessary quantity.
Then, when you have removed that tissue, and nothing remains of them, then suture the opening of the wound, if it is wide; and if not, place over it from plasters which are suitable for it, and cause it to heal, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the [surgical] forceps, when it breaks.
Indeed, the forceps is most often broken at the upper arm, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
Either it breaks and separates into two, apart from the tissues that may be in it; and it is easier to restore it.
Or, such that tissues are in the bone; and it is more difficult to restore it.
Or, such that it is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation in it, when the fracture is without a wound, is to have two assistants present, so that one holds the support that follows the fractured forceps, and the other extends the neck towards the part.
Then, adjust the fracture with your fingers, until its shape is as it should be; and there should be neither a prominence nor a concavity in it.
If you need more extension, then you should place under the patient's armpit a sphere of cloth or wool; and its size should be according to your need; and extend, and lift the forceps, and squeeze the sphere with your hand, until you make the fracture even, as it should be.
If you cannot pull the extremity of the forceps outwards, because it has reached a depth, then you should have the patient lie on their neck; and place a moderate-sized cushion under their shoulder, and let the assistant press on their shoulder downwards, until the bone of the forceps that is deep is lifted upwards; and then straighten the fracture, and make it even with your fingers.
If, however, you feel a splinter from the forceps, and it moves, then you should cut over it, and extract the splinter with ease.
If, however, it is retained in the bone, then make an incision in it with one of the knives you have prepared for that, after you have placed under the forceps the instrument that preserves the sifach; and the instrument is of wood or iron. And this is its form.
It resembles a spoon that has no concavity.
And let its width be according to what you need of it from the size of the bone and its smallness. Its length, however, also according to what is possible for you in the operation.
And let it have two ends, as you see: and one end be wide, and the other narrow.
If, however, the wound that you cut when you extracted the bone splinter is wide, and you are certain it will avoid a hot abscess: then bring the two edges of the wound together with a suture.
And if the rupture is small, or you fear a hot abscess: then fill the wound with cloths and lint according to the size of the wound's incision.
If a hot abscess occurs, then soak a cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and place it over it.
A constriction of the bone, when it is without a wound and without incision, is such that you should apply a plaster made from mill flour and egg white to it. And place a soft bandage over it. Then place a sphere under its projection, if you need that. Then take a hemostatic device, that is, a wheel held by the Saracens, which is very long, and let its width be one palm, or approximately that. Then place a doubled pad over the bandage and plaster. Then take a stick from a thin board, whose width is three fingers, and in length similarly. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the stick in the place of the alhimemati that falls upon the fracture and the place. Then place the alhimemati over the fracture as it revolves, and wrap it around its neck, and under its healthy and diseased tissues; and reduce it over the entire area, and as you see that the constriction holds the fracture properly: and that, which is not hidden from you, and all, so that the stick does not move from the broken bone. And for this it is necessary that you examine the sick person every day. Therefore, whenever the ligament softens, and you see the stick already removed, then straighten it and tighten the ligament.
Then place the sick person's head on his back, and place a small pillow under his head at night, so that his help rises from his side with it. Therefore, the fracture of the arm-pit is raised by raising the shoulder.
Or bind his arm to his neck, and do not loosen the bandage if no accident occurs in the place from itching or from an abscess, until twelve days.
Then renew the plaster, if you see his face towards it. And add to the constriction, and leave him until it is restored and congealed.
And indeed, the fracture of the arm-pit is bound and strengthened in most cases within twenty-eight days; and sometimes in some people it happens within a shorter time.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured shoulder-blade.
The shoulder-blade is rarely broken, and its break is in a wide part of it: and only its edges are broken from it.
Therefore, when a part of it is broken, and it is broken in its middle, and that is not recognised except by touch: then, according to the nature of the fracture, try to prepare it. And restore it to its natural shape by all the means of skill that are possible to you.
Then put on the place flour of mill with the white of an egg, and soft lint, and put on top a cushion made of folded cloth. Then place on it a broad splint of thin board, according to the size of the entire shoulder-blade, or a little larger.
If however there is a hollow under the splint in any part of the shoulder blade, then make that hollow even with soft lint, until the splint descends evenly.
Then bind it from above with a long bandage, with a suitable constriction, and firmness of the splint, until it is not removed from its place.
And consider the bandage every day. Whenever, therefore, the bandage softens, tighten it, and make the splint even if it moves from its place; and let the sick person lie on their healthy side.
And the shoulder blade indeed is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When, therefore, this number is completed, then loosen the bandage; and you will be secure. For it is from bones whose juncture is not feared, nor their separation.
If, however, a fragment from a bone protrudes and pierces under the skin, then split over it and remove it. And do what we said regarding the fracture of the fork from the suppuration of a hot abscess, if anything happens from it.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured chest.
The chest is sometimes broken in its middle, and that rarely happens. Its extremities are indeed broken and shattered very often by its accidents.
When its middle breaks, leaning downwards, a severe pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing occur; and perhaps he vomits blood, and a hollow in the fractured bone is apparent and not hidden from sight.  Its restoration is that the sick person is laid supine on their back, and a pillow is placed between their shoulder blades.
Then press his shoulders, and grasp the ribs from both sides with your hands; and adjust them in their alignment, according to all the ways possible and fitting, until the bone's shape returns to its proper form.
Then place a plaster and linen bandage on it, and put a thin tablet of willow, or chalking, or similar lightweight material on top, after wrapping it in cloth. Then adjust the binding over the broken bone so that it does not shift; and wrap the bandage around the back in a circular manner several times, and tighten it properly.
Then examine the bandage every hour, and whenever it softens, tighten it; and if necessity prompts its loosening due to itching, or a corrosion that occurs in the area, or pain, or an abscess: then quickly loosen it, and remove the plaster, and correct what has happened with the course of its treatment.
Then replace the plaster, if you see fit; and apply a bandage to it until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of ribs, when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in the thick parts which follow the spine, and from their extremities at the front. And attrition does not occur in them unless because they are cartilaginous. And its knowledge is not hidden from the sense in examination with the fingers.
And its restoration is done so that the fractured portion is made even with the fingers, as much as possible, until the shape is equal, according to what is necessary. Then apply plasters, and bind the fractured bone with splints, if it needs them.
And if the rib fracture is slanted inward, then a violent pain and stabbing occur in the patient, like the stabbing that occurs in one with pleurisy. Because the bone pierces the membrane, and difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting of much blood also befall him; and this is difficult to cure.
And the ancients have been ingenious in it with many devices; and some of them say that you should give the patient foods that cause inflammation and windiness, so that the belly is inflamed and distended, and the fracture is driven outwards.
We, however, abhor that a quick cause be made for a hot abscess, if it does not occur. If however it has already occurred, then add to it.
And some of them said: let a windy substance be put over the place, then be sucked with force, and this is similar to reasoning. However, it is feared that the windy substance will draw superfluities to the place, because of the disposition of weakness.
And some of them said: the place must be covered with wool that has already been submerged in hot oil; and pillows must be placed between the ribs, until it is filled; so that there is an even binding when you wrap it around the roundness.
Then the sick person should be treated with the treatment for pleurisy, from food and medicine.
If a violent matter, which the sick person cannot tolerate, constricts him, and the mouth is pricked by a wounding prick, and we fear for the sick person, then it is necessary to cut over the place and uncover the broken rib. Then let us place upon it the instrument that preserves the sifac, whose description preceded: and let us cut the bone with ease, and cut it.
Then let us bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with sutures; and let us treat it with plasters until it is healed.
If in the meantime a hot abscess occurs, then hurry, and pour pillows into rose oil, and place it on the spot; and let the sick person be treated with whatever is suitable for the abscess.
Meanwhile, let him lie on the side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured spine of the back and neck.
A fracture sometimes occurs in the bones of the neck, and attrition rarely happens to them; and similarly to the vertebrae of the back as well. Therefore, when this happens to someone, and you wish to know whether it will heal or not: then consider. For if you see his hands already softened, and numb, and dead; and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor contract them; and when you contract them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and he does not feel pain in them: then know that it does not heal, and in most cases is mortal.
But if he moves them, and feels the contraction with the fingers and the prick: then know that the bone of the neck is sound, and that the sick person will be healed with treatment.
If the same thing happens to the vertebrae of the back, and you want to know if it will heal or not: then consider his feet.  Then if you see that they are softened, and the same thing happens to them as happened to the hands; then, when he lies on his back, wind and excrement come out involuntarily; and when he lies on his stomach, excrement comes out involuntarily; and when he lies on his back, and he wants to urinate, and cannot: then know that it is mortal. Therefore, do not labor in his treatment.
But if nothing of that happens to him, the condition is less severe.
And the treatment for what arises from that is, to try to reduce the heat of the abscess: thus, that you place on the vertebra crushed rose oil alone, or with the yolks of roasted eggs.
Place that on it several times a day, until the hot abscess subsides: then place one of the strengthening, drying plasters on the area, and bind it with a bandage; and order the sick person to use rest and tranquility. And let him not sleep on the part where he finds pain, until he is healed.
If it happens that the bone is abraded or something has already separated from it, then it is necessary to cut through the skin and remove it. Then bring together the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with stitches. Then treat it with some healing ointment until it is healed.
If, however, the last bone of the tail breaks, and it is the end of the tail, then it is necessary to insert the thumb of your left hand into the anus, and straighten the broken bone with the other hand, as much as possible, and by a second straightening.
Then place it on top of it, and remove it; and treat the wound with the previous treatment, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the hip.
Chapter XI.
The bones of the hips rarely break. If they do break, then their fracture is not complete unless there is splintering at their extremities; and they split lengthwise, and are deflected inwards; and the patient experiences pain and a puncture-like sensation in the area, and the leg that breaks becomes numb.
And its restoration is to extend your hand over it, until you stand over the fracture in the way it is shaped.
If the fracture is only at its extremities, then make that fracture even, according to the alignment prepared for you, until it is similar to its natural shape.
However, if the fracture is lengthwise, or already deviates inwards, then have the patient lie on their stomach, until its restoration is prepared. When you have therefore made it even, place a plaster over it; then place a splint of wood, or of leather, over it; and bind it tightly, such that you do not fear a change in the fracture, nor the removal of the splint.
And make the concavity of those things equal to that which fills it, until the binding takes it according to equality; and order the patient to sleep on their back, or on their healthy side.
If however a hot abscess occurs, then cease from its extension and its restoration until the hot abscess subsides; and place upon it that which settles it, according to what has preceded, then return to its restoration; and bind it as is necessary.
If however it happens in the bone, being shattered or broken, remove it.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is that which is between the elbow and the shoulder blade.
If therefore it is broken, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: that you take a piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form, which follows.
And two bandages are tied at the two ends.
Then it is hung from a high place, and the sick person sits on a seat. Then let him place his broken arm on the wood, until it reaches the tip of it, in whose middle the curve of the wood is attached.
Then hang something heavy above it, so that the attendant stretches it downwards. Then the physician should put the fracture together with his hands, until the fracture is restored, as it should be.
And another way is that the sick person is laid on their back: and suspend their hand from their neck with a bandage. Then command two attendants, that one of them hold that which is above the fracture with their hands, and that the other hold it below, and let each of them stretch it to their own side.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then bind under the fracture with a bandage, and above it with another bandage, and let each of the two attendants extend it to their two sides.
If therefore the fracture is near the extremity of the humerus, then it is necessary that one bandage be placed under the elbow, and another under the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary that a bandage be placed over that place, and over the elbow itself.
Then straighten the fracture with ease without effort, until when the fracture is straightened as it ought to be, and a good application is applied, then bind it, if a hot abscess does not occur.
If however a hot abscess occurs, then leave the binding until the seventh day. And place over it cut wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until when the abscess subsides; then bind it; and the manner of binding it is, that you place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster. Then let the arm be supported by the aid itself, and let him place his open hand on his shoulder.
And place a cloth, and bind the arm over the support, so that the arm stays in place like a star: if nothing prevents you from it, nor does anything from the fractured bone disturb you.
If you fear that something from the fractured bone will disturb you, then use splints; and it is, that you place a splint over the fracture itself, which is wider and stronger than the other splints. And place between each splint a width of a finger; and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on each side.
Then bind over the splints the bandage which I said at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is, that your bandage should be tighter over the place of the fracture. And the more the fracture is lengthened, the less the bandage should be tight.
If therefore you see the position of the splints and the bandage, as we have said, at the hour when you restore the limb immediately, then do so; and if you fear a hot abscess, then leave the bandage and the splints until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then consider the ligament for all three days, lest itching or swelling occur in the place, or lest food be prevented from reaching the limb, because of the disposition of excess fluid from the bandage. Therefore, correct all of that, according to what we have said.
If, however, you are confident that nothing will result from it, then do not loosen the bandage, except after three days, or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on his back, and his hand on his stomach; and place a mattress full of wool evenly beneath his assistance.
And consider at every hour of the night or day that the fractured limb's shape is not loosened, or the bandage softened. Therefore, correct all of that with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we have previously described, so that there be fine food at first, until the mouth begins to draw together; then it is necessary that you increase the thickness of his food.
For it is the custom of the leg and foot that they should draw together in forty days; therefore, it is necessary to loosen them, and administer a bath, and treat them with ointments that correct it.
If the fracture is grievous, bruised: then do not loosen the bandage and splints from it for up to fifty days, and up to two months.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called two forearm bones.
One of which is small; and it is the one that follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is the one that is situated below the small one, on the lower part.
Perhaps therefore only the larger forearm bone is broken, or the smaller one; and perhaps they are broken together.
When therefore the smaller forearm bone is broken higher up, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the forearm bone is broken lower down, its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when the two bones are broken at the same time.
If therefore the bone that is broken is the smaller forearm bone higher up, then it is fitting for the physician in its restoration to apply a small extension with ease, until he equalizes it.
If however the larger forearm bone is the one that is broken, then it is necessary that he apply a very strong bandage.
And it is necessary that he place the shape of the hand in its restoration, and its extended extension upon a cushion. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers, and let the lower joint be below all the fingers, or the rest; and let the sick person sit square upon himself, and let the cushion be before him, or in a straight line with him, in height, so that the sick person does not incur strain.
Then let the attendant extend the arm from the lower part, either with his hand or with a bandage. And another attendant extend it from above similarly.
Then the physician should adjust the bone until he restores it to the best form possible.
If there are splinters in the broken bone, then strive to restore each fragment to its place by your skill.
However, if a separated splinter appears in it, and it pierces the skin, and you have no hope of restoring it: then cut over it, and remove it according to the method we have described in what precedes.
If, however, the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter on it, therefore take the treatment of it from there.
If, however, a hot abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then line a cloth with a cerate made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be medium between thickness and thinness: and bind the cloth over it with a fine bandage, until the abscess subsides: then remove the cerate, and apply a plaster prepared from mill dust with egg white. Then place splints, and let the splint placed over the fracture itself be a little wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in many arrangements, whether the fracture be in one or two bones at once.
Then apply your bandage to the fracture site more firmly and forcefully, and somewhat more tightly to the upper or lower parts of the fracture: or apply a bandage softer than its previous one, according to what was mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths wrapped around the fracture be soft, moist, and not hard, rough. And let the thread with which it is tied be of linen, properly, medium between fine and coarse, as we have narrated.
And consider the limb and ligament after days. If anything occurs requiring rectification, such as itching that affects the limb, then it is necessary to bathe the limb in warm water until the itching ceases, and to release the limb from the bandage until it is at rest, then to reapply the bandage.
If the bandage is already softened, and the bone already removed, and similar to these: then rectify it all by your skill, and consider again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, due to the excessive tightness of the bandage, then it is necessary to soften it a little, and release it for a few days, until nourishment flows to it, then bind it again.
If, however, nothing of what we have said happens to the infirm person, then it is not necessary to loosen it, except after twenty days, or approximately these.
Then the patient's hand should be suspended from his neck. And let his neck be even, and let it be guarded by his skill from agitated movements. And have him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All that is according to the dispositions of complexions, and the dispositions of strength.
Concerning the restoration of fractured hands.
Know that fracture of the hand's ridges and the joints of the fingers rarely happens. And it does not happen to them unless from very frequent rubbing.
Therefore, when a fracture or rubbing of the sole occurs, then it is necessary that the sick person sit square, and a seat be before him, according to equality. Then place his hand upon it outstretched.
Then the attendant shall extend the broken bones, and the physician shall make them equal, until when they are joined with good union.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and linen, if a hot abscess does not occur. Then place a bandage over it according to the amount of the place, and you shall now have wrapped it in linen.
If however the fracture is towards the lower part, approximately the sole of the foot, then make something like a sphere from cloth, and instruct the sick person to press upon it with their broken foot; then bind it with a long cloth.
And let the bandage be of leather, in which there is softness, so that the leather is applied to the body of the foot; and bind it, according to what is necessary.
If however the fracture is outwards, then it is necessary that you place a bandage over it, and another bandage from below on the foot; and let the hand be open and straight. Then place a bandage, as if the hand is revolved; and stretch between the fingers with a ligament.
If a fracture occurs in any of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, let it be straightened as is necessary. Then bind it with the foot; and if you wish, place a straight, small bandage on it, so that the fracture is elevated and does not move.
If however the fracture is in the remaining fingers, such as the middle, and index, or ring, and little finger, then let it be straightened, and bound with the healthy finger that follows it; or let all of them be bound in accordance with their order; and it is better. Or place a small bandage on it, as we said in the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the disposition itself of the hot abscess; and apply with it what is necessary, when anything from it happens, according to the recurrence of its narration.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured hip.
The hip bone is frequently fractured, and is declared to the senses, because it turns towards the inner and posterior part.
And its restoration is done so that the ligament above the fracture is tightened, and another ligament below the fracture. And the sick person lies on their face.
Then let the attendant stretch all the ligaments on their side according to equality; and this, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
If however the fracture is near the root of the hip, then let the ligament be on the side of the pubis, so that the extension falls upwards; and another ligament below the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is near the knee, then let the ligament be near the knee, so that the extension is downwards.
Then the physician should equalize the bone with both their hands until they return it to the likeness of its natural form; and the bone is joined with a good junction.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and a bandage, if no abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess occurs there, then leave it for a few days until the hot abscess subsides, then return to your treatment.
However, its bandage should be like this. It is necessary to wrap a hard linen bandage over the fracture, that is, the binder; and double it twice, or three times; and let an excess remain of it. Then wrap the leg, until the heel reaches the root of the buttocks.
And insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee downwards, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of the two parts. Then wrap over the leg and thigh whatever excess remains of the bandage.
Then place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture itself, and place one splint over the shinbone.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the constriction is equal.
Then begin with a strong constriction from the middle over the fractured place with three or four turns. And the farther you extend with the bandage from the fracture site, the less tight and softer and gentler your constriction should be.
Then pull on the two ends of the thread, which you inserted between the thigh and leg. Therefore tie together the three splints that are above. Then proceed with the two ends of the thread to the lower parts, until you reach the ankle of the foot. And with both ends tighten also the ends of the splints on the other side, so that the bandage does not move from its place.
Then release the constriction on it, while it remains, so that no ulceration, or abscess, or swelling, and similar things, befall the limb. If anything of that sort happens. Therefore hasten with its removal, and rectify whatever happens from it completely, according to what we have said many times.
If however there is a bone fragment in the bone that pierces, then it is necessary to smooth it, if it is possible for you. And if not, split over it, and remove it: and treat it with what was previously remembered, until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is bound, besides which are added splints, as we have said in the case of the arm and forearm.
However, in the restoration of it thus fractured, lameness does not befall the sick person. And if it is restored alone, besides which the other leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that the one who has it limp always.
And know that the hip is bound in fifty days, or a little is added, or a little is subtracted: all this is according to the diversity of complexions and other dispositions.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured kneecap.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap rarely occurs, but it often suffers attrition.
If, however, a fracture does occur, then it is only either a fissure or a hole in its parts. And it is with a wound, and without a wound. And the whole of it stands with feeling.
And its restoration is such that what is disrupted of its parts is made equal with the fingers, until it is aggregated and joined as much as possible by means of equality and ease and fabrication.
Then place a plaster of sesame flour, or mill flour, with egg white: and place a round pad over it, if you need it: and bind it above with a suitable bandage.
Then consider all the dispositions we have described in other fractures, such as a hot abscess and similar things, so that you oppose every accident with what is suitable for it, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick and is called the Cruris, and the other thin, and is called the Focile.
And fractures of both types occur in the same way as in the two bones of the arm, and because of that, its restoration is done in the same way as the restoration of the arm, and the operation is the same.
If both bones are broken at the same time, the leg will be turned in all directions.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg will be turned towards the front.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it lower; and it is of those things that are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, and alignment, and the ligature of splints according to that equally.
However, if the fracture of the leg is a foul fracture, having much blood: let the extension be less, and gentler with your alleviation.
And in the leg indeed there is something from the operation surpassing the arm: and it is, that when you align the splints, and complete all your operation, then take two bandages from pinewood, that is administered in the making of roof-shingles that are laid under the boards; and they are made from the blades of a palm tree; and choose from those that have little thickness, and neither let them be small; and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee down.
Then wrap over each bandage with two wrappings according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side, and the other on the other side: and let them be from the knee down to the foot.
Then tie the two bandages in three places from the two ends and the middle. For when the constriction is prohibited, the leg deviates to the right and left, and stands in good posture.
And now indeed let a cradle be administered of wood according to the length of the leg, and let the leg be placed upon it, so that it may keep it from movement.
And very much indeed it is necessary, that you do it in a fracture when there is a wound properly with it.
