Then consider the leg every day, and strive with that utmost diligence from an abscess, or swelling, or any other remaining ones. If therefore anything of those happens, then oppose it with what is necessary, until it is healed, if God wills.
Of the fracture of the bones of the Foot and Toes.
A fracture of the heel bone does indeed not happen entirely.
However, fractures of the bones of the Foot sometimes happen, and fractures of the Toes also sometimes happen; but they do not happen to them except for abrasion in many circumstances.
If a fracture of the bones of the Foot does occur, and you see those bones elevated above each other, then let the sick person place their Foot on prepared ground, as if walking.
Then, stand you, and place your Foot on that which is elevated from those bones; afterwards, press it, or step on it; and make it even, until it returns to its proper place.
Then place a plaster, and linen over it; and place a small board under the sole of the Foot, which has two flat ends.
Then bind it with a suitable bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other things it needs.
When therefore three or four days have passed, then loosen the bandage. For you will find the bones straightened, whether they were broken or dislocated. The operation on them is therefore, as you see.
If any fingers are broken, then restore them and straighten them according to what I have told you concerning the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint along the length of the finger, and let it be somewhat wider than it; then place under the foot this board which I have told you, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
If however that which is broken of the bones is two, or three, or more, then place on each finger a splint of its own size, wrapped in linen cloth; and bind the board to the lower part of the foot; and let it stand upon one end of the board outside the sole of the foot, so as to hold it firmly.
And it befits you, that you not forget, to consider all that I have said to you concerning the remaining fractures from accidents that we have mentioned: and apply each accident with what is appropriate.
Concerning the fracture of a woman's vulva, and the breastbone, and a man's penis.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, then make her sit square. Then incline her slightly towards her own back, and let it be held back.
Then let the midwife fill her vulva with wool, until it fills it: and let there be a sphere-like form in the vulva from it.
Then let the woman be shaken, and raise her back gradually. For that wool will emerge, until it becomes like a sphere in front of the vulva's entrance, and the fractured opening returns.
Then place a cushion on her back. Therefore, when she wishes to urinate, remove the wool easily, then let her urinate: and return it according to the manner in which you first introduced it: then let her return to her original cushion.
Let her do that for seven days, or thereabouts, for the opening itself will be restored.
And if you wish, take an ewe's bladder, and bind a reed cannula over its opening, and introduce the entire bladder into her vulva. Then blow into the cannula with force, until the bladder inflates inside the vulva, for the fracture returns.
Then fill it with wool, and let it remain for some days, according to what we have described, until it is healed.
And when the rib bone of a man or woman breaks, then administer in its restoration, and its equalization, that which we have described in the hip bone.
And the rectum is not hidden in these nearby fractures, which rarely occur, especially for someone with some study or practice of it.
And understand my book well, for this fracture itself signifies for you in many dispositions the right way in its restoration and its tightening: understand therefore.
But when a man's penis breaks, take the throat of a goose and insert the penis into it. Then place over it a bandage of cloth, and let it be wrapped, and let it be left for three days, or about that time, and it will heal, if God wills.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured bone when it is with a wound.
It is fitting for the one who has a fracture with a wound, especially if it is large, such as the hip bone, or the auxiliary bone, or similar to it, to hurry and immediately bleed him: if the conditions of bloodletting attest to you, as we have previously stated.
If the wound flows blood, then it is necessary, that you hurry to the excision of it, so that you pulverize crushed zegi upon it: if another is not present to you.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day: and do not postpone it, if no abscess occurs.
If, however, a hot abscess occurs, then postpone its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. And do not approach him on the third and fourth days at all, for you would cause him to experience bad accidents.
If, however, the broken bone is prominent above the skin, uncovered: then it is necessary that you try to reduce it, and equalize it with your hands with ease and small extension.
If, however, its extension and equalization with your hands is not prepared: then reduce it with this instrument. And the instrument is made of iron, whose length is the measure of seven or eight fingers, and its width is according to the size of the wound. And for that, it is necessary for the physician to take from them three or four, according to the quantity he needs for that treatment in every type of fracture.
And let it be round, and let it have a small thickness, so that it is not doubled by compression upon it at the time of the operation; and let it have sharp ends, with a margin at their edges. And let its upper part be to the thickness of its middle part up to the lower parts.
And this is its form.
It is necessary in this operation that its pointed curved end be placed upon the projecting end of the bone; and drive it with it in one direction, until when the bone returns and becomes equal by some adjustment.
Therefore try to make the fractured ends equal to each other.
If its fractured ends are thin, and it does not receive the instrument with good reception, then it is necessary that you cut the end of that bone, until the instrument has power over it.
If, however, you cannot reduce them with that which we have described at all, then cut it with one of the knives that we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in any way that is possible for you.
Then scrape away that which remains on the bone from roughness and small pieces.
Therefore, when the bone is reduced, and the infirm person finds a very painful injury after its reduction, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Therefore, do that. For you confer great benefit on the infirm person when your restoration of the bone is completed.
Then dip a cloth into astringent, black wine. And properly if it is in summer. And do not place cauterized material on the wound, nor anything containing oil, so that putrefaction and corruption do not occur in it.
Then administer the splints at the hour of completion of your restoration of the bone. And leave the wound uncovered; thus, cut a hole with scissors in the dressings according to the size of the wound.
And carefully avoid binding the wound with the fracture. For often foolish physicians have done this, and have caused their patients either death or corruption or züch, that is, pus. And let your bandage be soft, gentle, different from other fractures' bandages.
If the wound is severe, or is a large wound, and you fear certain bad outcomes which we have mentioned, and find pain in the suspended area, then you should not place splints upon it. And make them wrappings from hard cloths in place of splints, and bandage it with them.
However, when a day or two has passed, and you see the wound beginning to generate pus, then remove the cloth you placed upon it with wine. Then administer dressings and ointments with which we are accustomed to treat wounds, such as tetrapharmacon ointment, and similar ones.
And it is fitting for you to loosen the bandage. And consider the wound every day morning and evening, until it is consolidated and healed.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that pus flows from it downwards with ease.
If many days pass over a wound and it does not consolidate, nor is pus separated from it, then know that there are small bone splinters there. Therefore, it is necessary that you probe the wound with probes. And however many of those splinters are separated, remove them and extract them. And those of them that are not separated, and pierce the limb, and cause pain to occur, try to cut them and remove them by every means possible to you.
If a wound suffers from zūchen, or ekileten, that is, festering, or another species of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary to apply to each of them the appropriate treatment from the previous discussion in its chapter.
And concerning that about which my discourse should stand, and to which you should apply your mind when a bone is fractured and protrudes from a limb, such as the hip bone and the shin bone, and similar large limb bones, it is that you should not presume to pull it back, for often death results from that, but rather leave it alone until it rots.
For perhaps it will fall out by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then, if it seems appropriate to you, treat the wound; and if not, leave it alone.
Concerning the treatment of a swelling at the site of a fracture.
This swelling often occurs at the site of healing from a fracture. Especially those near the joints, and it disfigures the shape of the limb from it. And perhaps it prevents the limb from its natural function.
Therefore, if the swelling is recent, administer to it medicines that have astringent properties, such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh, sarcocolla, acacia, and similar to them.
So that you take some of these, or all of them, and mix them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar, and place them on the swelling in linen, and bind them tightly over it.
And leave the bandage, and do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and apply another, until the swelling is removed.
Or bind over it a sheet of lead properly. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from the limbs.
If however the swelling has already turned to stone, and it is tight, and necessity urgently requires its removal: then cut over it from its upper part, and cut away the protruding excess; or scrape it with some razors until it is removed; and care for it until it heals.
Concerning the treatment when it is restored, and the limb remains after it delicate, not according to its nature.
When a fractured bone is restored, and the limb remaining after it is delicate, weak: then it is not so except for many reasons. For one is the multitude of loosening of the ligament and its ligation not as it should be; or because of an excess of constriction of the ligaments, until the nourishment is prohibited from running to the limb; or because of the multitude of superfluous embrocation at the wrong time; or because of the paucity of blood in the body of the infirm, and its weakness.
And the treatment of it is to feed the infirm person, and to make his body tender, until the blood is multiplied in him. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and gladness upon him.
Then place pitch upon the limb, so that the pitch will attract to it much nourishment. And diligently embrocate it with tepid water, until the nourishment flows, and it returns to its natural form.
Concerning the treatment of fractured bones when they are restored crookedly.
When, after the healing of a limb already restored, crookedness, and prominence of the fractured bone, or a knot, occur, and the form of the limb is disfigured, yet the limb is not prevented from its natural operation, then it is not necessary to heed the opinion of one who believes that the limb should be broken again.
And already there have been many foolish physicians and bone setters who have done that in our land.
And indeed this operation is greatly to be condemned, leading to death, or great fear, so that it might be deadly.
But if the limping and the knotting are recent, then it is necessary to bathe it with lukewarm water in which have been boiled softening herbs, such as the leaves of marsh mallow, and its root, and melilot, and similar ones. And let a plaster be applied with a softening plaster, such as a properly made diachylon.
Or let the mucilage of the marsh-mallow root be taken, and be beaten with hen's fat, or sesame oil: and let it be plastered with it.
Or let fat figs be taken and ground with pigeon dung and similar medicinal substances which are said to decrease consolidation.
And sometimes the knotting is loosened with diligent, long-continued, and gentle rubbing, which is done with moist hands. And let movement of the limb be administered to every part at all hours.
However, if twisting has already preceded, and become strong, and turned into stone, and the need has arisen for its cure with iron, then it is necessary that the above part of it be cut, and the continuity of the bone be loosened, and that what is superfluous from the knotting or bone be cut with slender surgical instruments: and let ease be administered to it with care and diligence.
Concerning the discourse on Dislocation.
Dislocation is the departure of any joint from its place. Therefore, it is prevented from its motion, and the figure of the limb is disfigured. And it causes severe pains and injuries to the afflicted.
Therefore, when any dislocation occurs, then it is necessary to hasten its restoration; nor should one delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the place becomes suppurated, and perhaps the reduction of the dislocation with it.
Therefore, it is not appropriate for it to be moved or extended during the time of its suppuration. Because it often causes spasms and painful injuries.
But when that occurs, then it is necessary to hasten to the bloodletting of the sick person. Then it should be left until the abscess rests.
Then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil. Then it should be reduced with ease.
And every limb should be treated with whatever restoration comes to its place.
I have indeed already arranged the chapters on dislocation, even as it preceded in fractures, from the upper part of the body down to its lower part.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws are seldom dislocated except rarely.
And the dislocation of both occurs according to one of two ways. Either they are moved from their places by a small displacement, and softened a little. Or they are dislocated completely, until they soften to the part of the chest, until the sick person's saliva runs, and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue becomes entangled with his speech.
When, however, its dislocation is small, then it returns in many cases by itself with little effort.
If, however, it is a complete, entire dislocation, then it is necessary that its reduction be administered quickly, and not delayed at all: and it is so that the attendant holds the sick person's head; and the physician inserts the thumb of one of his hands into the root of the dislocated jaw between his mouth, if the dislocation is on one side. Or he inserts his two thumbs together, if the dislocation is on two sides. And the remaining fingers of his hands shall be outside, with which he shall equalize.
And command the sick person to clean his jaws and free them to eat in every direction. And the physician shall equalize the jaw, and shall push the jaw until it returns to its place.
If its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated simultaneously, then administer vaporization with hot water and oil until the reduction of both is easy.  Do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
When therefore they return and are equalized, and the sick person's mouth is closed, and they are not softened, then place cushions of cloths, with cerate that is made of wax, and rose oil, upon them.
Then bind it with ease with soft bandages.
And let the sick person's sleep be on their back, and let their head be supported between two pillows, so that they do not move it to the right and left, nor attempt or be forced to chew anything.  Indeed, let their food be gentle drinking until, when the pains are removed, and the jaw is tied up, then let them eat what they wish, and let it be administered with ease.
Let them not labor over the operation of their mouth during eating, drinking, and yawning until the jaw is bound and healed.
If the reduction of the two jaws becomes difficult, when they are dislocated at some time and do not return to their places, then often there are fevers and persistent sweating from it, and perhaps the sick person's bowels are loosened, and perhaps they vomit pure bile.
Therefore, when you see this, then know that it is destruction, and often the one who experiences this dies within ten days.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the humerus.
The collarbone indeed does not dislocate from the inner side, because of its connection with the chest. And it sometimes dislocates outwards, and this is made clear to the senses.
And its restoration is that the infirm person lie on their back, and extend their arms. Then, grasp the place with your hand firmly, for it will return itself.
Then place a plaster and pads over it, and bind it.
However, its extremities that follow the elbow and continue with it do not dislocate, except rarely. If, however, it dislocates on any day, then it is necessary that it be reduced and made equal according to what we have said, and what is prepared for you.
Then place a plaster and pads over it, and a bandage. And tell the sick person to adhere to quiet and rest until he is healed.
And with this same treatment the humerus extremity is reduced when it is also separated from its place.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the Humerus.
Know that the Humerus is not dislocated except in three ways.
One of which is that it is dislocated to the side of the armpit, downward. The second is that it is dislocated to the side of the chest. And perhaps it is dislocated to the upper part of the Humerus; and that happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated to the back, because of the shoulder blade. And it is not dislocated to the front, because of the nerves.
And very often it is dislocated and comes out downwards towards the pubic area: especially in those whose flesh is scant. Because it exits quickly in them, and enters quickly.
However, in those whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite, namely because it exits with difficulty, and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some people through a blow or fall, and the humerus becomes inflamed with a hot abscess, and it is thought to be because it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, it is necessary that you test it, until you are certain about it, and then proceed with its treatment.
And dislocation is recognized when it is turned downwards towards the tickling: and distinguish between the dislocated humerus and the healthy humerus. For you find a clear difference among them, and you find a concavity in the head of the humerus, and under the tickling, upon touching the head of the humerus, as if it were an egg: and the weak person cannot bring the hand to their ear, nor move it in all directions.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest, or upwards, then you find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
And indeed, a simple reduction of this dislocation is done, when it is recent, or the patient is a weak child.
And its reduction is that the attendant should raise their hand upwards. Then place your thumbs beneath its tickling, and raise the joint with strength upwards to its place: and the attendant should raise their hand and extend it upwards. Then place it lower, for it will return quickly.
However, if it does not return through what we have said, and there have been many days since the dislocation occurred, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water, and an embrocation be administered that softens and soothes, such as if the roots of marshmallow, and fenugreek, and melilot are boiled in water, and administered.
Then the patient should lie on their back: and a sphere and wool should be placed beneath its tickling, which should be equal between softness and tightness.
Then the physician should place their heel on the sphere, and lift the shoulder's head with strength, and pull the patient's hand downwards.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method: and it is necessary to have present a man who is taller than the patient, and stand at the patient's side, and insert their arm under the patient's armpit, and lift the armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. And another attendant should pull the patient's hand downwards toward the patient's abdomen. If however the patient is light, then it is necessary to suspend something else with him, so that it weighs him down; for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also reduced according to another method: and it is necessary that a long piece of wood be fixed in the earth, whose head is round in shape, like the sleeve of a mortar, which is neither thick nor thin. Then, after soft cloths are placed over the head of the wood, the patient is placed beneath it, and stands along the length of the wood.
Then his hand should be extended downwards on one side, and his body extended on the other side with strength; for the joint returns to its place swiftly.
If his reduction is difficult by all the means we have described, then use this treatment: and it is to take a piece of wood whose length is the size of two arms, and its width the size of four fingers, and its thickness the size of two fingers; to which there should be a round head, so that its entrance into the depth of the hip may be eased, according to this form.
Then soft cloths are tied over the round head, so that the wood does not harm the patient.
Then place the wood under the patient's hip, so that the hand or arm is extended over the wood downwards; and bind the wood to the support and arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then place the arm upon a step of a ladder in width, and extend the hand downwards, and let the rest of the body be suspended from the other side. The joint enters immediately.
When therefore the entry is completed in whatever way possible, then it is necessary that you place a sphere of equal size made of wool under the hip. Then place the prepared plaster from the mill's powder with frankincense and egg white over the entire shoulder, as it revolves from above.
Then bind with a suitable bandage under the hip, and the ligament be revolved over the plaster from above, and let his hand be tied to his neck, and let it be released, nor should his hand move for seven days.
And it is necessary that little food be given to the weak one, until the limb is strengthened, because it is quicker for its healing.
Then a larger one should be placed after seven days, or five, and it should be exercised with movement. If therefore it is strengthened and does not soften, then it is now healed.
If however the joint is dislocated many times, due to the moisture that happens to it, or because of another cause, then it is necessary that a cautery with three openings be applied to it, according to what was previously said in the chapter on cautery.
But if all this is done, and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the joint does not strengthen, and the plaster and constriction are applied to it repeatedly, and it does not strengthen, and it falls, and cannot raise itself upwards, then know that its nerves that are to the head of the humerus are already cut, or stretched, or softened: and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
However, the reduction of a dislocation that occurs towards the breast and nipple, or to the back, is done with pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns, and the remaining constriction and care are applied to it until it is healed.
If, after healing, heaviness occurs in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the infirm person should use a bath repeatedly until that heaviness is eased and it returns to its original nature.
On the treatment of elbow dislocation.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty, and similarly returns with difficulty; indeed, it is dislocated in all directions, and especially forward or backward.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you, since it is apparent to the eye and to the touch, regardless of the form in which it is dislocated. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the sound one, that is clearly evident to you. For the joint is hollowed out, and cannot double the arm, nor touch its own shoulder with it.
And you ought to hurry and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot abscess occurs to it.
For if a hot abscess occurs to it, its reduction becomes difficult, and perhaps it will not be healed completely, especially when it is towards the back. For it is worse than all other types of dislocation, and causes them more severe pain: and very often death descends with it.
And its restoration, when it is of those that can be reduced, is that his hand is to be extended with two hands; and his arm be extended, and the physician's hands be above the elbow and below it, and he should push the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or indeed, the dislocation is towards the front: then it returns thus, that he double his hand with his hand, until he strikes with the root of his palm the elbow which is dislocated.
If the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer a vigorous, very strong extension.  And it is that two attendants should extend the arm, and two attendants should also hold him so that he is not moved during the extension.
Then the arm is to be turned in every direction after a long doubled cloth, or a wide bandage, is wrapped around his hand.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, let him anoint it with oil, so that it may be of those things which aid in the lubrication of the joint with ease. Then he should drive the joint with a forceful impulse until it returns.
And after its reduction, it is necessary that a plaster containing astringency and desiccation, along with egg white, be placed upon it; and a suitable bandage be applied. And let the arm be suspended from the patient's neck, and leave it for a few days.
Then it should be released. If therefore the joint is strengthened in its place, then loosen the bandage from it and leave it.
And if you see that the joint is not properly bound, then reapply the plaster and bandage, and leave it for a few days until it is bound. Then release it.
If however there occurs a heaviness to it after the reduction of the joint, and the condition is in motion: then administer moistening in bathing, and gentle friction, and compression, until it is lined, or place upon the joint the fat of a ram's rump. Then bandage it, and leave it upon it by day and by night. Then remove it, and immerse it in a bath. When therefore it sweats, then rub the joint with moderate friction. Then apply it [the fat] again over it once, and twice, and thrice with each bath entry, until it is soothed.
And if you wish, place upon it a moist, heated cow dung with butter, and bind it upon it.
Do that several times. For thus it is soothed, and returns to its proper condition.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of Almahassem.
The joint between the arm and hand, the Mahassem, is frequently dislocated.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. In contrast to other joints. However, speed is necessary in reducing its dislocation within the hour in which it is dislocated, before the place becomes suppurated: or a hot abscess occurs in it.
And the reduction of the dislocation in it is that you place the weakened hand's Mahassem on a table, and the attendant extends his hand; and the physician places his palm upon the prominence of the joint, and propels it until it returns.
However, it is necessary to consider, if the palm has already receded to the back of the hand: then the infirm person should place the back of his hand on the table at the extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding toward the back of the hand, then the position of his hand should be internally on the table, that is, the inner part of his hand should be placed on the table, so that the physician's hand falls upon the very prominence of the joint.
If therefore it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then bind it with a soothing plaster for the abscess: and leave it, and do not repeat it.
For if it does not tolerate it, nor can you succeed in its reduction after days pass, but the joint remains according to its twisting: and it does not harm the infirm person anything except that the hand softens and cannot grasp anything: then know that the nerves have been severed, or lacerated.
Therefore, there is no remedy for it, except to be cauterized near its injury. And perhaps it does not benefit it at all.
Therefore, when the Almahassem does not return, then place upon it the plaster, which we have recounted. Then it should be bound, and left for five days; afterwards, it should be loosened and left, and the hand should be exercised.
However, if its movement is deprived, and something serious happens to it, then soothe it with warm water and friction several times, until it is soothed.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the Fingers.
When a finger is dislocated to any part, or a finger is dislocated to the back of the palm, or its belly, then extend the finger, and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it towards the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend it, and extend it, until it is straightened in its own day.
Therefore, when it is night, bind it according to the narration itself, and do not cease to unbind it in the day, and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night.
Do that for a few days, until it is strengthened.
And similarly do this with it, if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand, and bind it to the part itself, and perform your first operation on it until it is healed.
And similarly do this with it when it is dislocated to the other parts.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the spinal column.
When it happens to someone that the spinal column, or the neck, is completely dislocated, or many vertebrae are removed from their places, then there is no cure for it, because death quickly approaches the patient.
And a sign of it is that the patient's excretion comes out involuntarily, and he cannot hold it; and often some of his limbs are softened, or both of his legs, or both of his arms, or one of them; or one vertebra is removed from its place.
For often it is removed, which often happens with their removal to four parts.
Therefore, that which is removed to the back is called Gibbosity.
However, the treatment for it is to consider if gibbosity occurs from childhood; then there is no treatment, nor is there any cure from it at all.
However, in that which happens from a fall, or a blow, or similar things to those, the ancients already described methods of treatment in a lengthy account. From which much does not return with profit.
However, I have already abbreviated it, from which little excuses itself from the great deal of it from my proximity to the intention: and by my exposition of it, and formed its instrument in a different way from that in which they showed it, and explained it.
Therefore, I say that in gibbosity that occurs on the anterior part, there is no remedy, nor cure from it.
And similarly in that which happens to both sides also.
And it is not cured from them, unless that which happens in the back properly, with what I narrate: and it is that the sick person should be laid out on their face on an even surface near a wall: and moist straw should be spread underneath, so that their chest is not injured.
Then let a straight piece of wood be fixed in a hole in the ground, at the end of the platform, approximately at his head; and another piece of wood, approximately at his feet, at the other end of the platform; and let an attendant hold the wood, and let it not be firmly fixed in the hole. And another attendant hold the other.
Then wrap around the head of the sick person, and under their armpits, a soft, strong bandage; and extend the end of the bandage to the wood which is at their head; and tie it to it.
Then bind another bandage over their thighs, and above their knees, and at their ankles. Then gather all the bandages together, and tie them to the other piece of wood which is at their feet.
Then let each attendant stretch the bandage towards the piece of wood, and do not move the two pieces of wood from their places where they are fixed; however, let them not be firmly secured, as we said.
And let the physician place his palms upon the vertebra with force, until it returns to its place; or let him place a board upon it. Then he binds his feet to the board until it returns to its place.
If, however, it does not return through this treatment, then take a board whose length is approximately three arms' lengths. And make a hollow in the wall, of which we said that it is near the sick person, a place where the end of the board may enter. Then place the middle of the board upon the vertebra. And let the physician place his feet on the other end. And let him pull tightly until the vertebra is constricted and returns to its place.
Then, if you wish, work with the lathe which is wrapped around your hand: and it is that you fix two pieces of wood into the earth near the head of the sick person on the last part of the platform, the length of which is one arm's length; and near his head on the last part of the platform two pieces of wood.
And let the length between each piece of wood be one palm: and now a hole has been made in every place through which the lathe runs: and let every piece of wood be firmly secured in the earth so that it does not move at all.
And let a round piece of wood be inserted; and there is a lathe in which the bandage revolves in the two holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its end is a hole in which the wood is secured, whose length is a palm with that which revolves; and in the two other pieces of wood there is similarly one like it.
Then tighten the bandages which you have tied around the chest of the sick person on the lathe which is near his head; and the bandages which you have tied around his legs on the lathe which is near his feet.
Then let a servant stand near each lathe, who shall turn his hand with the cord with which the lathe revolves; and let the physician adjust the Spine, according to what we have said.
This is the form of the lathe, which follows.
Then, after the Spine has returned and the place is adjusted, then it is necessary that you place a drying plaster with egg white. Then, linen. Then place a board splint, whose width is three fingers, or approximately that, over the plaster. And its length the amount which takes the place of the hump, and over the healthy part of the Spine.
Then it shall be bound with the bandage which we have said. And let the sick person use a light diet until they are healed.
If something of prominence remains in the place at the end of healing, then it is necessary that medicines which soften and soothe be administered, with the administration of the board which we have described for a long time. And now a lead plate is administered in it.
And sometimes a prominence occurs in the last vertebra of the back, and it is thought that a dislocation is through it, and that is the bone which now protrudes. Therefore it is not appropriate to presume to use this medicine with it, for perhaps it will cause death.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated shoulder.
Know that dislocation of the shoulder joint, and the humerus joint, does not occur except by complete dislocation; nor does it happen to them what happens to the other joints from slight displacement and concavity.
And indeed the shoulder joint dislocates according to four parts. That is, because it dislocates forward and backward.
And it dislocates most often inward; and it dislocates little forward and backward.
And the treatment for its inward dislocation is this: for when you join the healthy leg of the weak one with the diseased one, it is longer; and the knee of the diseased one is more prominent than the healthy one, and the diseased one cannot bend his foot at the groin; and there is a place following the groin that is festering with a hot fester, because the head of the thigh has already reached there.
And the sign of someone who has suffered an outward dislocation is that the symptoms are the opposite of these symptoms.
And the sign of someone who has suffered a forward dislocation is this:  because the healthy leg extends fully; however, he does not bend it without pain in the knee; and if he tries to walk, he cannot move it forward; and his urine is retained, and his groin festers, and while walking, his heel strikes the ground.
And the sign of someone who has suffered a backward dislocation is that the knee does not extend, nor can he bend it before bending the groin; and his leg is also shorter than the other; and the groin is softened, and the head of the thigh is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction of this type of dislocation, however, is that you should consider whether the dislocation is old, which has already become established in the affected person. Then if you administer its reduction, and it remains in its position: then there is no cure for it at all. Therefore, it is not necessary that a complete presumption be made about it.
However, in the case of one whose dislocation is recent, and is of one of the four types of dislocation: then hasten to attempt to manipulate the joint, and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left; for perhaps it will return, and will not require further treatment.
If, however, it does not return in this way, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong assistant, and have them extend his leg from the lower part; either with their hands, or with a bandage that is tied above his leg above the knee: and another assistant should extend their hand from above, so that they can insert their hands beneath his toes. Then he should tighten it with a soft bandage over the root of the thigh, and hold the other end of the bandage.
And its extension should be either from the front of the groin to the crotch, or from the back to the back.
And let the extension of all of them be at the same time, until the sick person is lifted from the ground with their body, and remains hanging.
And this species of extension is a common species of the four species.
If, therefore, the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for each species that which I shall say concerning its own cure.
In the reduction of it (the dislocation) itself, when the dislocation is inward, it is necessary that the sick person be laid on their healthy side. Then let a bandage be placed over the root of the thigh, in the area between the head of the thigh and the place below the groin. Then extend the bandage upward from the groin area to the higher part of the body, towards the fork (pubic area).
Then let another strong attendant take his arms, and reduce the thick place of the sick person's thigh, and extend it outwards with a forceful extension. For now it returns to its place.
And this type is easier than the other types of treatment, whereby this limb is reduced.
However, if it resists you, and does not obey you at the entrance with this type of treatment at all: then it is necessary that the sick person's two feet be tied together with a strong, gentle bandage, over the two heels, and over the two knees: and the length of each one from its companion should be the quantity of four fingers: and the sick leg should be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
Then let the sick person be suspended from the head of a wooden beam, which is in the house: and let its length from the ground be the quantity of two arms.
Then command a strong young man to secure the head of the thigh: and let him be suspended with the sick person by another young man: and let another young man, securing the thigh with strength, push, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
In its own return, however, when the dislocation is outward, it is necessary that the sick person lie on a platform, according to what we have narrated concerning someone with a hump. And let a bandage be tightened over his sick leg, and over his chest.
Then let two pieces of wood be placed, one at his feet, and the other at his head. Then let a piece of wood be placed added in the middle of the platform, firmly fixed: upon which let wet cloths be wrapped, so that it does not injure the sick person: and let the wood be between his thighs, so that it is not pulled down at the extension.
Then let every attendant pull in their direction, and let the physician adjust the reduction with his hand.
If therefore it yields to the return, it will be good.
And if not, place it upon the board itself, and press it, as we said in the case of a hump, equally. However, it is necessary that the sick person lie on their healthy side.
And when the dislocation is forward, then it is necessary that the leg of the sick person be extended quickly; and it is according to this same account on the platform; and let the physician place the palm of his right hand on the groin of the sick person. Then grasp it with the other hand, and with that grasp place it stretched downwards toward the part of the knee.
And if the dislocation is backward, then it is not necessary that the sick person be stretched downwards and raised from the ground; rather, it is necessary that he be placed on a hard thing, as it is necessary to do also if his hip is dislocated outward, according to what we said about his lying on his two hips; and it is on his face, and the ligaments extended, as we recently said.
And it is necessary that pressure be administered with a board also over the place to which the joint goes out, when the reduction is completed according to what is necessary.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you: and it is that you extend the two legs of the sick person; and when you see them equal, and the sick person tightens his leg, and extends it without excuse, then know that the limb has now returned, as is proper; then join the two hips, and place a plaster, and bind it with a bandage, that is, a roller: so that the hip does not move to any parts; and the sick person adheres to rest for three days, or four.
Then loosen the bandage and the plaster, and compare the leg with the other; then if you see them equal in condition, then know that the dislocation is now firm; therefore release the sick person to walk.
And if you see any softening, then return, and plaster it, and bind it according to your first binding; and leave it also for three days.
Then loosen it and make him slow in walking on both for several days, until he is well strengthened.
Concerning the dislocation of the Knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outwards, inwards, and downwards, that is, backwards.
And it is not dislocated forwards.
And a sign of its dislocation is that you should instruct the sick person to apply their leg to their thigh. If however it does not adhere to the thigh: then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all the ways of its dislocation is to make the sick person sit upright, so that if he can, he extends his leg over it, and you apply your back to his face: and put his foot between your feet.
Then make his knee adhere with your palms, and straighten them between your fingers above his knee. Then apply with your palm the two sides of his knee with strength. And another attendant should extend his foot, until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh with gentleness, not with effort.
Then plaster it, and make the leg adhere to the thigh. Then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days. Then loosen them.
And let the infirm person not use walking for some days, until they are healed.
If its reduction is refused, then administer strong extension with the bandages, whose description preceded in the treatment of a dislocation, until it returns.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the Heel bone.
The heel bone sometimes moves with a small displacement, and sometimes is dislocated completely. And its dislocation occurs either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel bone swollen, protruding towards the side to which it is dislocated.
The treatment for its displacement is easy repositioning: it is to extend it gently with the hands and adjust it until it returns to its place.
However, in treating it when it is dislocated completely, it is necessary that you make the patient sit upright; and a strong assistant should hold him firmly from the back of his back, in the middle of it.
Then hold his foot with your right hand from its upper part, and with your left hand from the lower part of his foot at the ankle joint.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with the left hand twice.
Then extend with the left hand, and propel the foot's sole with the right hand towards the leg without effort.
Do that twice, as we have described.
Then propel the sole of the foot to the leg a third time, and you pull the heel.
If therefore it returns in one time, or in two according to this method, and you see the foot made equal, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation upon it: because it returns.
If, however, its reduction is prevented by what we have described, then make the sick person lie on their back on the ground, and fix a firmly planted peg in the ground, so that it falls between their thighs; and you have now wrapped a cloth around it so that it does not injure the sick person.
Then let the attendant take his thigh; afterwards let another attendant extend the foot, either with their hands or with a bandage that binds around the foot's ankle. Then let every attendant extend against the extension of their counterpart; and the peg should stand between the two thighs of the sick person, holding him so that his body is not pulled downwards with the extension.
Then the physician should adjust the dislocation with his hands, and another attendant should hold the healthy leg downwards. For the dislocation returns with speed.
Therefore, when the dislocation returns, and its return is made clear to you, then apply a plaster and linen, and firmly bind it with bandages, and bind the foot to the leg with a bandage.
And it is necessary that you take care, or fear, concerning the nerves that are above the heel bone behind, so that the bandage is not strong over them and injures them.
Then leave it for two days, or three. If the bandage softens, tighten it. Then loosen it on the third, or fourth day.  And forbid the infirm person from walking for forty days. If, however, he attempts to walk before that period, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not be dislodged again, and be corrupted, and receive no further cure after that.
If, however, a hot abscess happens to him, then it is necessary that you administer to its soothing that which our preceding narrative related in another place concerning its cure, and an embrocation, until it is removed.
Concerning the dislocation of the toes of the foot, and its cure.
It is necessary to put what is dislocated of them back in place with a slight extension without effort; and indeed this is not difficult, but rather easy.
If, however, the dislocation is in certain knots on the back of the foot, then it is necessary that the afflicted person sit with their foot on level ground, or on a board; and let it stand as if walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot on that which protrudes from those joints. Then press it with your foot with strength, until it returns and you see it now in place, and no prominence appears in the spot.
Then place under the sole of his foot a board that holds the entire foot, with two ends. Then bind it with a suitable bandage for three days. Then loosen it, and keep him from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and secure from reduction.
Concerning the types of dislocation with wound or fracture.
When something like that happens, and you attempt its cure and restoration, often death results.  Because of that, one should not presume to treat such a case unless one is skilled in the art, of long practice, merciful, pious, slow, not bold nor presumptuous; and one should use at the outset medications that soothe only hot inflammations; and leave the sick person to divine judgment. Unless you see or hope for salvation from destruction with the gentleness of the illness, and in which something of hope appears. Therefore, attempt its reduction immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot inflammation occurs.
If, therefore, the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regimen that soothes the inflammation, and treat the wound with what is suitable for it from drying ointments.
If, however, the dislocation is with a fracture, and separate projections occur in the bone, then attempt their removal, and do in that what we said in simple illnesses, from what preceded in their places.
And beware according to your ability, and guard yourself from entering the path of fear, according to what my instruction to you preceded.
For that is better for you before your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and more safe for your body among those in your time.
